ago Henry Lawson prophetically
spoke : —-
The self-same spirit that drives the man to
the depths of drink and crime
Will do the deeds in the heroes’ van that live
till the end of time.
The living death in the lonely hush, the greed
of the selfish town,
And even the creed of the outlawed push is
chivalry—upside down.
And this moral, that almost forms the
basis of Dennis’s philosophy, is neatly per-
sonified in the person of Ginger: —
’ls name is on the records at the Melbourne •
City Court,
Fer doin’ things an’ say in’ things no reel nice
feller ort;
Aii’ ’is name is on the records nv the Army,
over there,
Fer doin' things—same sort o’ things that
rose the Bench’s ’air. ...
An' then there came the Call uv Stousli, or
Jooty — wot's a namet
Att 5 Ginger eoirked ’is ear to it, an’ found ’is
flamin’ game ....
’E’s one uv our brave boys, all right, all right.
’ls eurly trainin’ down in Spadger’s-lane
Done 'im no ’arm fer this ’ere orl-in fight;
’ls loss o’ culcher is ’is country’s gain. . ..
An’ them that shudders at the sight o’ gore,
An’ shrinks to ’ear a drunken soljer's oath,
Must ’ide be’ind the man wot 'eaves the
bricks, . , .
An’ thank their Gawd for all their Ginger
Micks ....
Lovin’ an’ fight in’... .when the tale is told,
That’s all there is to it; an’ in their way
Them brave an’ noble ’ero blokes uphold
Wus Ginger Micks —the crook ’ints uv then-
day. . ' ~
Jist let the Call uv Stoush give ’im is
chance,
An ’ Ginger Mick's the ’ero 6f Ttomance. . ..
Ev’ry feller is a gold-mine if yeh take an’
work ’im right;
It is shinin’ on the surface now an’ then; (
An’ there’s some is easy sinkin’, but there s
some wants dynermite,
Fer they looks a ’opeless prospect —yet
they’re men.
An’ Ginger — ’ard-shell Ginger’s showm signs
that ’e will pay;
But it took a flamin’ world-war fer to blarst
’is crust away....
And Ginger learns the value of discipline ;
and pathetically looks back on chances
missed by the push :
We never ’ad no discipline, that’s wot we
wanted bad.
It’s discipline that gives the push its might.
But wot a time we could ’ave give the cop-
pers if we ’ad.
Lord! We’d ’ave capchered Melbourne m a
night.
When I think nv things that might ave been
I sometimes sit an’ grin,
Fer I might be King uv Footscray if we d
’ad more discipline.
He does his whack, gets his stripe, and
bravely dies in action. Ilis mate, Trent,
writes of his death: —
’F ses strange things in this ’ere note ’e
sends:
“He was a gallant gentleman,” it ends.
A gallant gentleman! Well, I dunno.
I ’ardly think that Mick ’u’d like that name.
But this ’ere Trent’s a toff, an’ ’ort to know
The breedin’ uv the stock frurn tvliich ’e
came.
Gallant an’ game Mick might ’a’ bin; but,
then —
Lord! Fancy ’im among the gentlemen!
And this gallant gentleman’s last words
were, “Look after Rose... .Mafeesh !” And
his cobber has pardonable doubts whether
we will look after his Rose, and all the other
Roses. For in a previous epistle Mick had
given us the straight griffin about the hero
stunt: —•
We ain’t got no objection to the cheers;
We’re good an’ tough, an’ tee can stand the
noise;
But three ’oorays and five or six long beers,
An’ loud remarks about “Our Gallant Boys,”
Sounds kind o’ weak —if you’ll ixcuse the

Beside the fight in’ sounds we’ve lately ’eard.

The loud ’Osannas uv our native land
Makes us feel gaud an’ glad in many ways.
An ’ later, when we land back in a mob,
Per'ays we might be arstin’ fer a j0b....
’Eroes! It sounds a bit of reel orl-right —•

But Ginger, when ’e’s thinkin ’ there at night
Vv Rose, an’ wot their luck is like to be
After the echo dies uv all this praise,
Well—'e ain’t dazzled wiv three loud 'oorays.
The casual lightheartedness of The Senti-
mental Bloke is hardly to be found in Ginger
now. Or in Australia. The old days are
done:—
The gilt is on the wattle, Mick, young leaves
is on the trees,
An’ the bush birds in the gullies swap the
ole siceet melerdies,
There’s a good, green land awaitin’ you when
you come ’ome again
To swing a pick at Ballarat or ride Yarrowie
Plain.
The streets is gay wiv dafferdils — but-~
haggard in the sun,
A wounded soljer passes; an’ tee-know ole
days is done.
Fer somew’ere down inside us, lad, is some-
thin’ you put there
The day yeh swung a dirty left, fer us, at
Sari Bair.
So the moods of Ginger Mick are serious
moods. Only in “Ginger’s Cobber” —’E ivears
perjarmer soots an’ cleans ’is teeth —does the
old joyous humor of Dennis revive, though
“Rabbits” embodies a humorous idea. But
do we need that old careless humor any
more? This is a finely patriotic book, a
uniquely Australian book, a book with a
dirty left.
The publishers have turned out the book
as we expect Angus and Robertson to do.
The illustrations by Hal Gye are the
daintiest of decorations, of the calamitous
ugliness of Ginger's chiv.
The Red Page
The Dirty Left of Ginger Mick
Dennis has done it again, done it with an
effort, maybe, but distinctly pulled it off.
The Moods of. Ginger Mick, by C. J. Dennis
(Angus and Robertson, Sydney), is a worthy
successor to The Sentimental Bloke; and a
sequel to a popular success is always the
very devil. In his first book, of which
surely a world record in the sale of verse has
been achieved, since within 12 months 50,000
copies have been actually sold, Dennis had
the advantage of surprise—the thing was so
original in matter and in form. But Ginger
Mick, who can be as sentimental as his
cobber, merely follows the well-worn track
of the Sentimental Bloke. lie speaks the
same language, he has his Rosie as the Bloke
had his Doreen; the element of dramatic
surprise is lacking. True, Dennis has made
good use of the war, and is thus enabled to
shift his scene; but there is a perceptible
effort throughout: one feels that Dennis
doesn’t quite believe in Ginger as he believed
in the Bloke. Occasionally Mick becomes
wearisome. Yet though Dennis uses the war
he uses it well, to show the manhood that
lies behind the inarticulateness of our Ginger
Micks and to prove that in the trenches
Australia, which is made up, more or less, of
mere blokes, is finding her soul.
Fiction
Bohun Lynch belongs to the Bennett
school of painstaking fiction. The Complete
Gentleman (Seeker, per Dymock’s Library)
is a detailed analysis of an English type
that the war was specially created to elimi-
nate, with what success we do not yet know.
Lynch depends upon a slowly culminating
narrative of minutiae, painting his elaborate
portrait with tiny strokes, each one of which
ultimately takes its essential place in the
completed portrait. And the figure stands
posed against an equally elaborated back-
ground. Lynch is the sort of author
who describes all the furniture in the
room, and even points out the subject and
the location of every picture on the walls.
He possesses a delightful style. Henry, the
complete gentleman, is the sort of English-
man that it would be sacrilege to address as
Harry. He does no work —that is always
the trouble of this species of hero. He has
no enthusiasms; “he’s not mad bitten about
anything”—except marrying money. Well,
he married it. “Money—money-money ! He
was keen enough about that for years. And
now lie’s got it, with the usual result. He
devoted himself entirely to getting money
without working for it, and now lie’s got
nothing left to devote himself t 0.... Wait
till they’ve got some children.”
Until the last few pages of this elaborate
book there are no thrills, with the bright
exception of Dolly’s incursion into her cor-
rect husband’s bathroom. Dolly is a charm-
ing woman, and. unlike her husband, she is
alive. He was in his bath —a long para-
graph tells us exactly how he. comported
himself in his bath —and with his ears full
of soap he didn’t hear Dolly’s knock. When
her head appeared he told her not to come in.
“But I wanted to see you in your bath.
You’d be such a dear,” his wife laughed.
Henry blushed crimson; she saw that he
had blushed crimson. “In consternation and
utter surprise she backed out hurriedly;
but in another she had put her head back
for a moment. And you are a darling,
in your bath,’ said she, and fled.” Horribly
embarrassing for Henry. Of course, he
could never speak about it. It was better
ignored. And when he came down to break-
fast, and she told him he was the most
beautiful person in the whole world, “all
the gods of Greece rolled into one, and alive,
too,” he quietly changed the subject.
Of course there is the other man, a pre-
possessing rotter. The child arrives, and
the girl grows up. Lynch does not mind
admitting that Doily grew middle-aged. “She
looked at herself in the glass, touched her
hair, and observed with pleasure that her
flesh still looked firm, that the exercises she
had been practising had indeed removed her
tendency to a double chin,” How many
other authors would have given their heroine
even a tendency to a double chin? Lynch,
indeed, gives a double chin to another char-
acter. “There is a comfortable suggestion
about the doubling chin, the perfect line of
the protruding paunch—made so suave and
so befitting by the artfulness of his tailor.”
But Henry never gets a double chin.
Lynch treats the affairs of life in a plea-
santly common-sense manner. The final
interview between Dolly and the rotter is
a triumph of matter-of-fact realism. And
the tragedy that is so long coming, the
details of which it would be palpably unfair
to disclose to the reader, is solved as Life
would solve it. The Complete Gentleman
is a solid and satisfactory achievement in
a genre that like its subject is nowadays
somewhat outmoded.
The Sailor , by J. C. Snaith (Bell, per
Dymock’s Library), is indubitably the finest
novel of this'year. Snaith is the chameleon
of novelists; in Mistress Dorothy Marvin
he out-romanced Stanley Weyman; in
Broke of Covenden he wrote a Galsworthy
novel that equals the best of that author; in
Henry A T orthcote he produced a remarkable
fantasy that is probably pure Snaith. since
it is like little else in modern English fic-
tion ; in William Jordan, Junior, he failed
to square his fantasy with fact; in Araminta
he suddenly took us into the region of Mere-
ditlxan comedy, diluted by William J. Locke;
several popular novels followed which any'
professional novelist could have dictated;
and now, unexpectedly, comes The Sailor.
Here, too, despite the fact that it is an
achievement, complete and rounded, the
book cannot help reminding the reader of
John Masefield and May Sinclair. The
“Biography” by Masefield might have given
Snaith his idea of the life of his hero at
sea, whose subsequent ipcursion into litera-
ture has the impact of another Conrad ; and
the situation of a successful author who
drops his aitches and is utterly unable to fit
himself in society irresistibly calls up the
figure of the tragic aitchless poet in The
Divine Fire. The fact that the Sailor also
worked in a bookshop need not necessarily
be considered a plagiarism of May Sinclair’s
poet; rather it may be considered a plagi-
arism upon himself, since he utilised the
same situation in a previous novel.
And there is this to be said for Snaith.
The professional novelist lives in an atmo-
sphere of the latest fiction. He can’t escape
the deluge of all the novels of the world;
he can’t bury himself in a bookless country;
and if he is a chameleon he can’t help tak-
ing his color from the literary world in
which he swims. Yet the final test is not
the source of his material, but the pattern
that he weaves from his material; and in
The Sailor he has drawn fulHength the por-
trait of a genius.
The book opens with Auntie, “bare-
armed, bare-headed, savage chest half bare
and sagging in festoons, stayless and un-
ashamed, breathing gin and wickedness,”
armed with a heavy carter’s whip, ecstati-
cally intent on flaying alive a terror-stricken
kid of 13. By the agency of blind chance he
escapes, and ultimately finds himself on
shipboard. Enery Arper belongs to the
gutter; he is unable even to read or write;
he is inarticulate and lacking even the
instinct of manliness. And the life upon
that ship, commanded by a fiendishly brutal
captain, is for years nothing but a long
apprenticeship to horror and despair.' Even
his meeting with a white man, who goes by
the name of Klondyke, affords but a. brief
relief from his misery. Klondyke tries to
teach him to read; but when that friend
leaves the ship at Honolulu “to walk over
Asia”—though how he could reach Asia by
walking from Honolulu is not explained by-
the careless author—Enery Arper relapses
into the mere sailorman. He had no ambi-
tion —how should he have? —and it was only
because the ship had to be broken up that
he came miserably ashore. Chance again
put him on the first step upwards, by a
friend’s discovery that the sailor had the
makings of a wonderful football player.
With his £2 per week and his spare time,
the youth makes pathetic efforts to educate
himself. It is the strenuousness of these
efforts, for about the only useful asset, apart
from his genius, that he was endowed with
was a dull capacity for “keeping on keeping
on,” that led to his disgracing his club in an
important match.
Further aimless wanderings lead him at
last to the right path, and the youth dis-
covers that he can write. His long years
at sea have given him, as it gave John Mase-
field, an unique vision of life. A magazine
that is meant for Blackwood’s accepts Ins
first Story, but not until the author had
laboriously re-written it —a useful corrective
of the habits that hero-authors have of
leaping instantaneously into masterpieces.
Whether Blackwood’s would have accepted
this new It. L. Stevenson is a minor point
to which this page good-humoredly takes
exception. But Enery Arper, the new lit-
erary figure, is hopelessly lacking in all
knowledge of the world. lie is even ignorant
of the fact that “he hasn’t an aitch to his
name.” When he makes this appalling dis-
covery he happens to discover a teacher of
correct speaking. He learns that there are
quite a lot of manners of correct speaking:
the Oxford manner, the Service manner, the
Naval manner, “hardly to be recommended
for social life,” the Civil Service manner,
“which combines utility with a reasonable
amount of ornament,” the Diplomatic
manner, and the Number Three Commercial
manner, which, on his teacher’s recommenda-
tion, he proceeds laboriously to acquire.
But, of course, a shrewd young lady con-
nected with the stage had already snared this
remarkable husband. Henry Harper, knew
as little of women as he did of
aitches. And now launched in the society of
his equals, he meets The Girl. The tragedy
of their love, with the inescapable back-
ground of a drunken and immoral wife, ends
at last in one of the most poignant scenes in
recent fiction. Before the stark horror of his
life-story the conventional English society
girl shrinks as from an abyss ; and the man,
recognising that he has lost her at the
moment when he is free to confess his love, in
his agony drops back into the speech and the
manners of his youth. But Snaith is kind
kinder than perhaps life would have been—
and enables these two to close the abyss that
had so horribly opened between them.
The book is an achievement. I lie host ot
other characters are real, alive, faithfully
observed ; there is comedy as well as pathos ;
arid the vivid story, that snatches the reader
at the first page and hurries him to the last,
will ensure it an immediate popularity.
\n eighteenpenny edition of Damaged
Goods the well-known syphilis drama by
Brieux has been published by Angus and
Robertson. Svdney, in view of the announce-
ment that this celebrated sermon on vene-
real diseases will shortly be staged m Aus-
tralia bv J.C.W. Ltd. This edition, rightly,
is unexpurgated. Is it too much to hope
that the stage version will also pass the box-
office censor?
War
There is gold on the Gallipoli Peninsula.
“Anzae,” the author of On the Ansae 1 rail
(Heinemann, per Dymock’s Library), has
made this discovery. “It’s there,” he says,
“for I panned off the dirt and found the
color! I know the spot, and some day,
perhaps, I’ll have a try for the big seam. I
have a fairly good idea —but that s another
tale.” So, after the war, we may expect
to see a rush of prospectors to the Darda-
nelles, headed by the blokes who have been
there before. It seems a pity now that we
didn’t send Randolph Bedford: lie would
have floated a company in the firing-trench.
The author, a Maoriland sapper, writes
good Australian. His opinion of the
Tommies is hardly complimentary, but
apparently typical. For one thing, Tommy
and Billzac have different standards of per-
sonal cleanliness; and when, on a transport,
the two are perforce bedfellows, Billzac is
apt to criticise. “The spectacle of a big,
husky bushman cleaning his teeth night and
morning was a tiling the Tommies couldn t
understand at any price, much less appre-
ciate. ‘If I did that,’ observed one in my
hearing, ‘I’d have toothache bad’; which
seemed to be the general opinion And
we like our tucker clean : we take all sorts
of fine care that the cook washes both him-
self and his utensils. But those Terriers
didn’t seem to care a cent whether the stuff
was clean or filthy. And they way they used
their knives! Still, they were wonderfully
expert: I didn’t see a single cut mouth all
the time.... Tommy Atkins can fight; _we
admit it, and we take off our hats to him,
but compared with the Australasian bush-
man, he is, in many respects, an uncivilised
animal.”
Apparently “Anzae” knew all that there
was to know about Cairo—and a bit over.
His information about certain streets of that
wicked city is evidently gathered first-hand.
The visit he and a cobber paid to a native
village is high comedy. They informed the
Sheik that they had come in search of
wives, and the old gentleman, scenting back-
sheesh, immediately paraded the goods'before
them, “with a full inventory of each laugh-
ing-eyed young lady’s charms and accomplish-
ments made out.” But no matrimonial busi-
ness was done that day, and on the day fixed
for their return the prospective husbands
were on their way to the Dardanelles.
Equally graphic is his account of the
Landing and the subsequent, and more im-
portant, Sitting Tight. He adds the inevit-
able grisly touches. And this sapper has an
appreciation of the right proportion of things
at the Front. To him the day when he got a
shirt that he had been promised is more im-
portant than a victory. And he adds: “If I
could only raise a pair of trousers I’d be
satisfied.” Then he “got in the way of some
bullets,” and “altogether I don’t feel quite as
good as before I went on tour with the
Anzacs. . . .My left ear is gone ; I don’t sleeo
too well; there is a fitter’s shop doing great
work day and night in my head, and when I
walk out things are apt to spin round some,
and I fancy the dear old ladies imagine I
suffer from chronic alcoholism.” This was
after he had been in six hospitals. “And I
wouldn’t have missed it, good or bad, for
worlds.” Also, “dear reader, I’d rather be
lying this moment in an unknown grave in
the Gallipoli Peninsula than be branded for
life as a God damned slacker !”
—s —
Over 25,000 copies of The Moods of
Ginger Mick were ordered in advance of
publication by “the trade.”
D. H. Newman’s The Socialist Case for
Conscription (Specialty Press, Melb.) neatly
puts forward the arguments for something
about which there should be no need for
argument. Newman is a unionist and a
socialist, as this page understands socialism.
And his case is lucidly and cogently put.
“The real issue is. . . .Australian conscrip-
tion for a few months, or German con-
scription for life. .. .Compulsory trades-
unionism is pure conscription.... Ihe
worker parasite who keeps out_ of trouble
and lays himself out to reap, in higher wages
and improved conditions of work, the re-
wards of other men’s sacrifices, they call a
‘scab,’ but the citizen parasite who skulks at
home....in a security that is costing other
men’s lives, they exalt into a champion of
liberty.... In presence of real danger these
fire-eaters of the Trades Hall become men
of peace, preaching seabdom with a kind of
religious zeal.” This pamphlet deserves the
widest and most immediate circulation.
[Arthur H. Adams, who dosses on this page.]

Blackheads Fly Away.
Instantaneous Remedy for Blackheads, Greasy Skin and Enlarged Pores.
A practically instantaneous remedy for
blackheads, greasy skins and enlarged pores,
recently discovered, is now coming into
general use in the boudoir. It is very
simple, harmless and pleasant. Drop a
stymol tablet, obtained at the chemist’s, in
a tumblerful of hot water. After the effer-
vescence has subsided bathe the face in the
liquid, using a small sponge or soft cloth.
In a few minutes dry the face and the offen-
sive blackheads will come right-off on the
towel. Also the large oily pores immedi-
ately close up and efface themselves natu-
rally. The greasiness disappears and the
skin is left smooth, soft and cool. This
simple treatment is then repeated a few
times at intervals of four or five days, to
ensure the permanence of the result.
Grey Hair—Home Remedy.
An old-fashioned Recipe restores Youthful Appearance.
There are plenty of reasons why grey
hair is not desirable, and plenty of reasons
why hair dyes should not be used. But, on
the other hand, there is no reason why you
should have grey hair if you do not want
it. To turn the hair back to a natural color
is really a very simple matter. One has
only to get from the chemist two ounces of
concentrate of tammalite and mix it with
three ounces of bay rum. Apply to the
hair with a small sponge for a few nights
and the greyness will gradually disappear.
This liquid is not sticky or greasy and does
not injure the hair in any way. It has been
used for generations with most satisfactory
results by those who have known the
formula.
To Kill Roots of Superfluous Hair.
The most Effective Formula ever Discovered.
Women annoyed with disfiguring growths
of superfluous hair wish to know not merely
how to temporarily remove the hair, but how
to kill the hair roots permanently. For
this purpose pure powdered pheminol may be
applied directly to the objectionable hair
growth. The recommended treatment is
designed not only to instantly remove the
hair but also to actually kill the roots so that
the growth will not return. About an ounce
of pheminol, obtainable from the chemist,
should be sufficient.
Good News for Fat People.
Something New in Obesity Cures.
A London chemist says: “The latest
method of reducing obesity certainly is far
more pleasant and convenient than all pre-
vious methods. It consists merely in eating
clynol berries. The fat person who wants
to reduce without the usual rigid diet, exer-
cise, sweating baths, etc., now puts a few
of these little brown berries in his or her
pocket and eats three or four each day.”
Clynol berries not only eliminate fat from
the body, but also correct the tendency,
which is usually constitutional, to create
fatty matter. No discomfort whatever is
caused by their action; in fact, except for
the loss of superfluous fat and the feeling
of “fitness” so created, you would not _be
aware that these little berries were doing
their work.
Local inquiry shows that clynol berries are
not very well known in this country, but
the demand is increasing daily,. and any
chemist can quickly procure them if specially
requested to do so.
VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cathedral Bells.
Long ’tis since from their mellow throats
The music showered down,
A sudden storm of golden notes,
Like laughter on the town.
I see them in their narrow room,
And feel as when a boy
They live and breathe condemned to gloom,
With hearts that ache for joy.
They wait the whisper in their cell
Will stir them, and release
The soul of Christ in every bell,
The passion that is Peace!
VICTORIA.
E.D.
The Little Man.
You, that rose when day began,
Tell me, roving fellow,
Did you meet your Little Man
Dressed in green and yellow?
“Sure, I did that same at dawn,
’Mid the clover’s honeyed foam;
And ’twas he,’the Cluirichaun,
Brought me singing to your home.”
Did you find him at his work
In the Fairy Hollow,
Where the slanting sunbeams lurk
Till the sunbeams follow?
“Yes, I found him crouched and small,
Sitting at his last, the rogue,
With his hammer and his awl
Patching up a broken brogue.”
Did you grip him good and tight,
Clutch him, as ’tis bidden,
Till he showed with cheeks affright
Where his crock was hidden?
“Yes, I clutched him sure and fast,
Bringing all my strength to play,
Till he showed me at the last
Where his hidden treasure lay.”
Now, ’tis fooling me you are
With this idle-story—•
You that saw the morning star
Drowned in golden glory.
“Nay, ’tis fooling you I’m not;
False to you I could not be ;
All the treasure that I’ve got—-
Care to get —he gave to me.”
You that laugh at me and mock, •
Fooling me at pleasure,
Tell me was this ancient crock
Filled with golden treasure?
“Ancient ’twas not, but as young
As the girl that I behold,
And it held (gemmed things among)
Gold outshining coin of gold.”
Then ’twas fairy-gold he gave,
Coin ’tis vain to cherish;
And his gems, though seeming brave.
Like the dewdrops perish?
“Nay, ’twas neither this nor that,
And I’m saying what is true—•
’Twas the face beneath your hat,
And your eyes and all of you!”
You, that spied the Little Man
In the foam of clover,
Long ago your quest began—>
Is your questing over?
“Yes, for holding you, I hold
All the round world in my grip;
Gold outshining coin of gold,
Crock o’ wondrous workmanship !”
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Golden Shoes.
Golden shoes my lady wears;
Silken stockings for her feet;
And a diamond buckle bright,
Flashing like a warning light
From a haunted dell at night,
Where the shoe and stocking meet.
Thin red lips my lady has,
Mouth that never satisfies,
So a man may taste in vain,
Longing spurring him like pain
That he may lie once again
’Neath the drugging of her eyes.
Golden shoes my lady wears;
Silken stockings for her feet....
Hid her rivals rule the land,
We should see my lady stand,
With a candle in her hand,
Shivering in a penance sheet!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
I Wonder Why.
I met a portly citizen
And smiled and bade him time o’ day;
And he —he looked me up and down
A moment and with gathering frown
Went on upon his way.
I wonder why.
I met an aged laboring man
Who toiled upon a mountain track;
I hailed him as I passed, and he
Leaned on his pick and smiled at me,
And gave me greeting back.
I wonder why.
I met the spectre of my youth,
And oh what shining eyes he had I
He hailed me merrily and strode,
Head high, along a golden road.
He seemed so strong and glad.
I wonder why.
I met myself again grown old,
And oh the eyes were dull and spent!
He passed me by without a word
And somewhere a harsh-throated bird
Croaked hoarsely as he went.
I wonder why.
N.S.W.
A. de R. B.

The Story of the Lamp
I
%
rfT3
j
M
The “Slush-Lamp” of the
“Never-Never”
The further the pioneer of Australasia
pushed into the “Never-Never” country,
the greater became his privations. Lamps
were to him a luxury, so he set about
finding a makeshift. An empty jam-tin
filled with melted fat —a piece of rag tied to
a stick standing in the middle of it—and
his “slush lamp” was complete.

THURSDAY, OCTOBER 19, 1916.
NOW, WHAT IS THE DECENT THING
TO DO?
Wiiat in the name of high Heaven has it
got to do with the business in hand if Hughes
or Pearce or Joe Cook or somebody else can
be proved to have said some foolish thing or
been guilty of some silly act? When it has
seemed worth while this paper has not hesi-
tated to jump on these people, and it is fairly
certain that it will leap on them a lot more.
But if The Bulletin maintained that when
it showed a politician to be either a blather-
skite or a muddler it also incidentally
proved that a 6xB tent was a dreadnought
or iKEir Mo a new sort of jumbuck, there
would be reasonable ground for suspecting
that there were fantods on these premises.
Yet all over Australia there are industrious
individuals toiling like coolies to show that
this or the other remark by Hughes or
someone else was stupid; and having done
this, they spring on to the wreckage, and in
a voice of 15 megaphones proclaim that com-
pulsory service is a combination of arsenic,
prussic acid, smallpox, bubonic plague and
the frying-pans of Hades. Going on these
lines it is as easy as falling off a log to
prove that the earth is as flat as
last year’s beer. All that it is necessary to
do is to expose the fallacy of Holman’S 1
arithmetic, and then the resemblance of the
earth’s surface to antiquated beer becomes
as clear as the winner of the Melbourne Cup
was three months ago. The sober truth is
that Hughes and Pearce and J. Cook are
as irrelevant to compulsory service as Wil-
son’s last Note was to the price of butter
at Wallerawang.
The question is not whether some
ephemeral person is like Cassaii’s wife. The
lady has been dead a long time, anyhow, and
the individual of the moment will go that
way, too, all in good time, as is preordained
by the Law of the Sandbag. What does
matter is whether the person who has
attacked Australia is going to see the people
of the Commonwealth throw in the towel.
It is a simple proposition. Is Australia
doing to quitf What someone says about
something else has nothing to do with this.
The answer to it must be plain “res” or
“No,” and it turns on considerations that
cannot possibly be affected by a controversy
about the censorship, or the P.L.L., or the
P.L.C., or freedom of speech, or the talk of
Hughes in England, or the obscurantist
performances of I’earce or the tribulations
of Higgs. And all the blither about
417 other things matters no more. The one
thing that does matter —that transcends
every other —the thing that is vital, and
around which all these other pifflings are
mere verbal mosquitoes, is that A iistralia is
at tear. Compared with this the defence of
Hughes or the smashing of Pearce’s arith-
metic by confused subtractions and multipli-
cations is about as important as Kink
O’Malley’s hat. The war has to be won or
lost. It is not going to be won by contra-
dicting ii politician or a newspaper. But it
may easily be lost if the allied peoples come
to the conclusion that victories purchased,
for Id. in the evening print are taking the
sting out of the enemy, or that the contra-
diction of public men is as effective as the
contradiction of Yon Hindenburg.
This paper holds no brief for Hughes.
It has little admiration for either his tongue
or his judgment. But it does hold a brief
for Australia. And the evidence it relies
upon is the casualty-lists which reveal the
holes knocked into Australia’s army, the
presence of the German on thousands of
square miles of conquered territory, and the
indisputable fact that war— our war—is
raging as no other war ever raged since kill-
ing became a public enterprise; and on that
evidence it claims that it is the business of
the Australian people to see the thing
through—to play, the ‘gitfne to their mates
and their sons and their brothers right to
the end. The Bulletin is under no delu-
sions about what the war is going to mean
to Australia. Some of the results have
been terrible enough in one way already.
Some of the others are going to be as cheer-
ful as hungry tigers. Nor has this paper
any admiration for war as a “refining influ-
ence.” or of conscription as a thing to be
worshipped for its own sake. But it has a
powerful conviction that war can only be
met bv war, and that in a fight for life a
bayonet is more useful than a mouthful of
wind. And it is more concerned with seeing
the brutes who launched this hideous orgy
of slaughter on the world walloped than
with seeing the arm that can wallop them
weakened. It is a desperate game that is
being played. But it will be made no less
so by pulling out; for the peril will still be
there. What do politicians matter in this?
The German armies and the armies of our
Allies know nothing about Australian poli-
ticians or what somebody remarked at
Ballarat or Binalpng. They know Aus-
tralian soldiers, though. They are not suffi-
ciently demented to imagine that the war
can be finished by anything except
bayonets and shells. The only war the anti-
conscription poliev can win is the war
between Hughes and the P.L.L. Compared
with the war that has to be won, this has
about the same importance as a disturbance
between a couple of roosters in a suburban
backyard. It is the other war that Aus-
tralians have to say something about next
week, and the question for them is not the
one figuring on the voting paper. The real
question is this :
SHALL WE BE THE FIRST QUITTERS?
The Bulletin puts it that way because
this is the problem. And it asks its readers
to put it that way to themselves—to put
Hughes and Pearce and Cook and every-
one of the political tribe out of their minds
—and answer nothing else.
[Written to express the views of The Bulletin,
within the limitations prescribed under the War
Precautions Act. by S. 11. Prior. D. M. Wright and
F. J. Kirby, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
HOME DEFENCE.
“In time of war the British frontier is the
enemy’s coastline.” On that simple maxim
was based the whole idea of the fleets that
carried Nelson’s flag; and the smashing
of Napoleon’s warships in Aboukir
Bay was as direct a blow for the defence
of London as if it had been aimed off the
coast of Kent. From history the nation
had learned its lesson well that the worst
battlefield is the battlefield at home. Exclud-
ing Scottish inroads, Britain has been eight
times invaded by a foreign foe; and only
twice was the invader hammered back to
his ships. So the stern logic of the attempts
at home defence when the enemy has once
crossed the shore line goes to show that
the danger of waiting until the axe is
actually in the door is far too great, and
that long-range warfare is the best guarantee
of national stability. In the present war
Britain has been called to fight much too
close to her own gate. For modern scien-
tific achievement has shortened spaces and
brought things that were once distant very
close together. Dover Straits, which even
in Napoleon’s time meant hours of
navigation, can be bridged by the flight of
a shell from a modern gun. All Europe,
measured by time spent in travel, is no
bigger than an English county in the days
of Shakspeare. With this narrowing of
the world the idea of home defence expands.
Far more than in Nelson’s day Scotland
may now be best protected at Gibraltar or
the" Yorkshire industrial centres at the
Dardanelles; and every nearer step of the
enemy increases the chances of finding blood
on the piano and death on the doormat.
* * * *
All the defence ideas that hold with regard
to insular Britain hold with equal force
when applied to insular Australia. V hat>
ever defence scheme might be adopted at
the present hour, nothing is more certain
than that if an invasion in force actually
reached these shores we should have a poor
hope of resisting it. Far beyond our present
realisation are w r e fortunate in being able
to fight our battles remote from our own
land, To argue that, with the tide of
battle now turning to our advantage—with
every hope of driving the enemy farther and
farther from us-—we should retreat and
voluntarily take up our position at the last
ditch is the final thing in futility. And
more than futile- —it would be unfair
to our Allies. That the war might
be won by them without Australian com
scription may be perfectly true. But it Will
be won more swiftly and effectively if we
lend a hand up to the limit of our power.
In any case, as this is a war of home
defence, no citizen who values his country
can allow the outsider to fight his battles
for him or withdraw from bearing his part
in the struggle until the home he defends
is safe.
But we are tokl that Australia is in no
danger and never was in danger from the
German aggressor. Only the fool will
believe it. Apart from the serious danger
of actual annexation, Australia had and still
has grave perils. There is a sense in which
every nation stands alone; but in a very
real way civilisation —the civilisation that
counts —is one and indivisible, linked
together by chains of hope and aspiration
and a faith in the future of man. Just as
the price of bread in one country depends
upon the market fluctuations of flour in
other countries, so, in the world of moral
values, the shame and injury of one people
becomes the shame and. injury of the rest.
This is no mere fancy, but stern and sober
fact. Moral loss and injury to France or
Britain must mean moral loss and injury to
Australia: If the Prussianism which is
the soul of the German military strength
could score a victory, life would be a little
darker and harder for every Australian
child, even though no foe came within a
thousand miles of 11s and the freedom of
the Commonwealth remained unchallenged.
In the fall of the Roman Empire, through
the onrush of the barbarous German fighting-
tribes, there were vast regions, enjoying a
civilisation in many respects superior to our
own, which were never touched by the
invader. They had all the things necessary
to a progressive life on the high plane of
Roman culture; yet in the moral shock and
ruin which struck Italy, they lapsed in a
barbarism more complete than that of the
countries where Goth and Vandal had left
their awful blood-trail. Had they been wise
the prosperous Greek cities of Asia Minor
might have stood and fought their fight for
home defence in the remote passes of the
Alps. They had no chance of effectively
doing it nearer their own walls. To them,
as to some people in Australia, the moral
defeat of civilisation seemed a merely imagi-
nary thing; but it proved a grim reality,
and to-day the squalid Turkish Empire
sprawls over the ruins of something finer
than Islam’s best dreams.
* * * *
For the anti-conscription Laborite to try
to make a. distinction between home defence
on the soil of Australia and home defence
on the soil of France is, then, to play
foolishly with words. We want a citizen
army in Australia —a citizen army ready to
answer any call, whether we find it neces-
sary to defend ourselves at Port Darwin or
Sydney or on the mountain terraces of
Thibet. Our immediate need is to maintain
our home defence on the red fields of
Europe, That there are no shells shriek-
ing through Australian air or bursting in
our shops and offices is all to the good. To
break the enemy at the greatest distance
from home is the thing to be desired, and
our chance of doing it is plainly by putting
our Defence Act on a logical footing and
giving it the breadth and scope that will
best serve our national safety.
[Written to express the views of The Bulletin,
within the limitations prescribed under the War
Precautions Act, by S. H. Prior. D. M. Wright and
F. J. Kirby, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
THE EXHORTATION TO BECOME A
THUG.
If the I.W.W. could be settled- by being
cursed it would not be feeling very well.
It has been damned in more languages'than
any other political organisation on earth,
and so far as this paper knows it has not
been affected thereby to any noticeable
extent. Probably it likes being bombarded
with the language of wrath. People with a
weird propaganda usually do. It makes
them feel that they are being taken notice
of, and the more the I.W.W. is damned the
harder will it toil to get damned again.
Whether it deserves to be mentioned with
vituperation is another matter. This paper
is persuaded that verbal consignments of
the I.W.W. to the Devil are mere futilities,
and that the existence in any country of
the brotherhood is proof of the failure
of statesmanship rather than of the in-
crease of insanity. For the doctrines of the
I.W.W. are the doctrines of despair—of
revolution against what the adherents of
these theories, and perhaps a great many
other people, believe to be'the blind cruelties
of commercialism. And in judging such a
concern, in condemning the revolutionary
spirit, it is well to remember that the ready
damn and the prompt boot never stayed
revolution anywhere—that before wise and
temperate reform revolution has always
melted away, and the hand of the social
assassin been thwarted.
The appeal of the I.W.W. is addressed
only to men and women who believe 'them-
selves the victims of flaming injustice—
who see no escape from it except through
chaos, the sweeping away of all existing
institutions from foundation to roof, the
pulling down of the whole social structure
into a vast heap of untidiness. It is just
a loud yell for anarchy, a howl to the
workers to arise and awake and cast off
their chains and all the rest of the iron-
mongery they imagine they are cumbered
with. And it is all as old as this weary
world, and very much like dynamite in
a State where social conditions are rotten.
Addressed to some peoples it might be
reconciled with the highest patriotism. But
addressed to Australians, who have gone
so far along the road to social salva-
tion, it sounds like an invitation to
start committing suicide on a large scale.
1.W.W.-ism offers nothing except confu-
sion and uproar. It wants everything
abolished. It is opposed to politics, to
reform, to work, to wages. It is in
deadly opposition to the entire social fabric.
It wants to achieve a Great Smash. It
calls itself a “hew psychology and alleges
that the world call be converted into a New
Jerusalem if it is turned inside out ahd
upside down and a fresh start made after
everything has beeil thrown into the Ocean.
By turning industry into hash the I.W .Y\.
considers that “the proletariat” will secure
certain economic salvation*; by political and
economic reform of what exists it is con-
vinced that man will gain nothing. It sees
crimson,
* * * ...
Now, it is easy to show that all this is
the wildest folly, that it Is an appeal to
ignorance, that it spelis social ruination.
But as the I.W.W.dte wants to create ruin
to start With, it is no use advancing as
an objectioh to his doctrines something to
prove that if put into effect they would
succeed in doing just what they are designed
to accomplish. But in tolerating the advo-
cacy of such anti-social acts —acts by which
our political and industrial life might be
wrecked —is not a State joining in a con-
spiracy against itself? There are dozens of
reasons why it would be absurd to attempt
to interfere with a fatuous individual who
tried to prove that if nobody worked the
millennium would be advanced. But if there
are any reasons why people should be
entitled to exhort others to make acts of
sabotage—the destruction of machinery and
material, among other things—part of their
method of securing salvation, this paper
would like to know what they are. If there
are none, the State should protect itself* If it
does not, then it must be entitled to be looked
upon as incapable of defending its citizens
from dangers as sinister as microbes of
hydrophobia. Australia has been plastered
with literature frankly advocating the boot;
is infested with wild-eyed persons yelling
for destruction. In a democracy this poison-
ing of the wells is an outbreak that deserves
prompt discouragement. There is not one
political or social problem before the citizens
of Australia that they cannot settle peace-
fully and effectively ; nor is there one they
can be assisted to settle by having someone
shouting in their ear to take up an axe and
smash something else. The industry and
the political institutions of Australia are
at bottom the common property of the
people. To advocate the further reform of
conditions is sane and patriotic. To pro-
ceed on the assumption that things are so
bad that everything must be torn up by the
roots and industry killed stone-dead, and to
preach this doctrine here because of condi-
tions that exist elsewhere, is wild folly.
The Bulletin is prepared to believe that
there are very earnest, very sincere men in
the movement. Let them ask themselves,
honestly, whether this country suffers from
the same social diseases as some other coun-
tries, whether the constitutional road to
reform is blocked, and whether the same
medicine is therefore necessary, even from
the revolutionary point of view. In any
case, the duty of the State is clear. If the
anarchist does not see the truth, the State
must at least be prepared to protect itself
from what amounts to the advocacy of
national murder.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Australia Will Be There.
Scene—Berlin. An Imperial Palace. Seated
in a Council Chamber the Kaiser, Betii-
mann-Hollweg and Von Tirpitz. The
news has just arrived that Australia has
voted No on the Referendum proposals.
The Kaiser : So Bull’s cub has turned
him down. Is that so?
Betiimann-Hollweg : Riehtig, Majestat
[Correct, your Majesty]. The Kangaroo has
proved a quitter and the Anzacs must look
to Russia for reinforcements.
Von Tirpitz : Donner und Blitzen. what a
joke ! Of course the numbers don’t matter,
but the moral effect—it is immense! Nothing
like a little judicious spending of money in
the proper places! Look at Greece —look
at
Hollweg: Not so loud, alter Ivnabe!
Besides, this Australian business is more
dunderheadedness than corruption. The very
idea of trying a Referendum in the middle
of a war to decide whether one should send
reinforcements ! Oh. it is superb !
The Kaiser : Nein, nein, I think the
money has done it. Note that they took no
Referendum as to whether they should enter
the war. They send their boys to the Front;
then we get to work and—the fools ! —they
take a plebiscite as to whether they shall
send their own flesh and blood assistance!
Von Tirpitz:*lt is no matter. Now it is
for us to make the best use of their folly.
Is Berlin beflagged yet?
Hollweg : The city is a sea of Imperial
banners. And I have wired to our Head-
quarters on the Western Front that our
regimental bands shall strike up “Australia
Will Be There.” Gott in Hiinmel —what
a joke!
The Kaiser : After all. these verdammten
Australians are a poor lot. Deserting their
own children ! Ach ! They don’t grasp yet
the big national idea that made old Deutsch-
land great. Ja, it is good ! I was getting
afraid that the British ideas of liberty would
come out on top, after all. Our dream was
slipping from us till this happened. .. .1 hear
London’s in mourning. Send over a dozen
Zeppelins to liven them up with an illumina-
tion and let the Kolnisciie Zeitung and
other right-thinking organs print a few
articles sympathising with John Bull over
his children’s ingratitude. Give ’em a bit
of their Shakspeare :
How harder than a serpent’s tooth it is
To have a thankless child.
Enter messenger with a telegram. Beth-
mann-Hollweg takes it and reads: “On


receipt of the intelligence that Australia had
turned down the Referendum proposal many
of the Australian troops broke out into fits
of furious rage. They say openly they are
shamed by their own people. Some demand
to be placed in the most dangerous positions,
as they do not wish ever to go back to Aus-
tralia. The common expression among them
is ‘ We are betrayed!’ and they want to know
has there been any other example throughout
the war of a people ‘turning dog ’ on its own
soldiers. The phrase ‘Australia scabbed on
us’ is on every Ansae’s lips.”
The Kaiser : Mein Gott ! This is the
richest morsel! More flags. Von Tirpitz !
More crosses for our freundts in Australia.
• • • - What did you say the bands would play?
Oh, “Australia Will Be There!” Ja, ja!
Nortrefflich ! That is the time, “Australia
Will Be There” !
BRISBANE. P, AIREY.
«.
Treasurers on the Spree.
Peacock told his tale concerning Victoria’s
cashbox last week. He also explained what
he was going to do about it. It all made a
weary story. There was the usual flam
about bank deposits and production and
“trade” and all the rest of it, but at rock-
bottom was this —that Victoria has accumu-
lated a deficit of £1,427,000; that Peacock
has to raise an additional £532,000 of revenue
(£11,169,000 not being sufficient), and that
the suggested expenditure for the current
year shows increases in nearly every de-
partment. The statement made by Peacock
is that of a helpless Micawber. The finances
°l Victoria, like those of every other State in
the Commonwealth, are going full-speed to
the Devil, and Peacock, imitating his reck-
less friends beyond the Murray, has nothing
better to propose than to go on with the
Great Push behind, dredging more taxation
out of the. people’s pockets and blithering,
about “facing the position” he resolutely ’
declines to look at. After over two years
of war, with debts growing like prickly-pear
and . taxation leaping upwards after the
fashion of the startled goat, with waste run-
ning a banker, with bloated public services
eating their heads off and allegedly national
enterprises sucking at the public purse, there
is heard from neither Peacock nor anyone
else even a suggestion for stopping the ruin-
ous leaks. All they seem to live for is to
find out how much more the community can
stand. It will not be very long now, appar-
ently, before some State Treasurer will
solemnly make a financial statement on these
lines:—•
The Assembly will be glad to hear that the expendi-
ture during the year was £175,263,842, every effort
having been made to practise rigid economy in view
of the fact that the revenue had fallen to
£32,364,896. Taxation now absorbs 75 per cent, of
the national income, so it is apparent that there is
still a margin left to come and go on. It is pro-
posed to increase all taxes by 15 per cent, and the
incomes of civil servants drawing over £6OO a year
by 30 per cent. The working day will be reduced
to 4-1 hours. The losses on the railways now. amount
to £2,075,613 per annum, but it is expected that
this sum will be recouped from the increased traffic
on suburban lines caused by the population hurrying
up to look for jobs. We have made arrangements to
convert the deficit into permanent debt, bearing inter-
• est at 10 per cent., which in a country with such
abundant natural resources as this cannot be regarded
as excessive. It is expected that the Royal Com-
mission inquiring into the cost of living will be
able to present an interim report before the end of
next year.
Then the Minister for Works will be able to
lay the foundation-stone of a new fowlhouse
at Burstenbung with a light heart and a
face, full of talk about the abounding pro-
sperity to be detected among the adjoining
poultry.
The Amazing Agreement with Norton
Griffiths.
The scandalous Norton Griffiths agree-
ment entered into by the Holman Govern-
ment is giving the expected results. Appar-
ently there is no check upon the syndicate’s
expenditure; and the more it spends the
more commission it draws. A while ago,
pushed by the Auditor-General, the Director-
General of Works, who acts as a sort of go-
between, wrote to the syndicate that “the
expenditure on miscellaneous items appears
to me to closely approach the border-line of
extravagance, and is such that the Depart-
ment would not, I consider, be justified in
incurring were the works carried out by
the Government.” He cited “the designing
of office furniture by an architect and the
payment of his fees [by the State, of
course], on which commission is charged.”
“There are,” he told the Auditor-General,
“many matters in which it is difficult to
define the line of demarcation,” but there
are “others where the impropriety of the
expenditure is obvious, such as the purchase
of horses by Mr. Morton as agent,” and
other things. As for doing anything to
check the extravagance of the syndicate,
however, the officer simply threw up his
hands and groaned at his own helplessness.
Somebody suggested that “a broad view”
should be taken of the agreement; and to
that the Auditor-General smashed in this
reply
I have seen no evidence that the broad view is
taken by the agents [of the syndicate], but rather
that the instances of what the Director of Works
characterises as cases “approaching the border line
of extravagance” abound. If this is to continue the
cancellation of the agreement, referred to by the
Director-General as the only remedy, cannot be
deplored.
But there was more than extravagance;
and, apparently unable to believe that any
Government could agree to such things as
Norton Griffiths claimed the Holman
crowd had done, the Auditor-General con-
sulted the Crown Solicitor. Actually the
syndicate, having been supplied with O.S.
postage stamps, had charged 5 per cent,
commission upon their face value; and he
wanted to know whether such a thing had
to be put up with. The reply was that such
was the nature of this scandalous agree-
ment that Norton Griffiths are entitled
to charge not only 5 per, cent, on the value
of the postage stamps with which the State
supplies them, but also 5 per cent, on the
value of the passes which they get from the
ltailway Department. Comment upon a
Government which would make an agree-
ment of that kind with a syndicate seems
superfluous.
“Treachery.”
In- nearly all their references to Britain
since the war started the Germans have
alluded to “treachery.” Why don’t they pro-
duce their proofs? There is no known utter-
ance of any British Minister or person in
authority that gives any sort of color to the
notion that England went back on the
Teuton. One London paper certainly did
suggest that it would be a comfortable thing
to stand aloof and reap the trade advan-
tages by selling chocolate creams and other
items to the warring nations; but even
Germans could hardly construe that into a
national expression of opinion. The only
visible explanation of the phrase is the racial
one. The theory has long been held that
the English are a Germanic people; and the
spectacle of a Germanic people hitting- a
German people when out for world plunder
no doubt hurts the race instinct of the
Teuton. But he blunders in attempt-
ing to get the racial measure of the English-
man. By his language John Bull is Ger-
manic ; by his skull type and his history he
is half Celt. The proof of it lies in the
fact that the early Saxon and Angle invaders
didn't and couldn’t bring their wives with
them to any extent. The same was true of
the Danes, who came later; though, in
point of. fact, the modern Dane has the
Celto-Latin head, and most likely his roving
great-great-grandfather wore a similar sort
of brain-scuttle. But the German didn’t
worry about these things. On a skull Test
the Teuton was the poorest thing in Europe ;
on a language test he could hold his own.
So the language test was stressed, and the
better and safer gauge of race was ignored.
Now. in the world-struggle, England ranged
on the side of Slav, Celt and Latin —all
identical in race type—seems to the German
an embodied treason. It doesn’t occur to
him as he stoops over his diminished sausage
that in the ages since llengist landed at
Tlianet a lot of the German taint may have
been worked out of the Englishman, just as
the Frankish taint has been mostly hustled
out of the Frenchman. Yet the solid dislike
of the average Briton for the average Ger-
man long before the war broke out might
well have aroused his suspicions.
The Human Life Bogey.
The yell that the sanctity of human life is
involved in the conscription issue, which
caused W. M. Hughes to snort something
that left him open to a lot of misunder-
standing, is frayed and full of ‘holes.
Lives are being sacrificed; and unless
it can be shown that conscription will
cause more lives to be sacrificed than
would otherwise be the case, the yell as
an argument against the national policy is
mere hot air. And of course it can’t be
shown. On the contrary, commonsense
points to the fact that, though some con-
scripts may be killed, the slaughter will
be less on account of the increased force
of the. Allied armies. When a few men
stragglingly attack a strong post, many of
the attackers are slain; when a large force
smashes into a similar position, victory is
swift and the slaughter comparatively
insignificant. Conscription, making for
stronger armies and greater efficiency, must
lessen the loss, and so all the sanctity-of-life
argument is in its favor. But what gives
life its sanctity? Is the life of a shirker
who would not defend his own home sacred?
Is there any sanctity about the life of the
traitor who would sell his home and neigh-
bors to the. enemy? Human life is sacred
only when it fulfils its purpose and stands
in the breach guarding its own and stand-
ing for its own ideals. The fool who
imagines that Leonidas and his 300 would
have been more sacred objects feeding goats
on the slopes of Arcadia than giving their
lives to stop the Persian advance is best left
to his own folly.
The Young Australian Woman.
It is no use merely howling at the Bishop
of Goulburn for his recent references to the
—call it freedom if you like —of a large
number of girls in Australia. The plain
truth is that the statistics on the subject
are wholly unsatisfactory, as The Bulletin
pointed out years ago. In the very midst of
the howls against the parson for “defaming
the young women of the land” Ivnibbs’s
“Official Year Book” for 1015 arrived. Dur-
ing 1914 there were 43,785 nuptial first
births and ex-nuptial births in the Common-
wealth, and no less than 19,442 were either
ex-nuptial births or nuptial births that
occurred in less than nine months after
marriage. Excluding the 3813 children that
were born in the ninth month after mar-
riage, then it is a fair thing to say that of
the 43,785 children born in Australia during
the year 1914 no less than 15,629 were the
result of the “freedom” referred to above.
Put it in the plainest language, 36 out of
every 100 Australian brides anticipate the
conjugal condition ; and how many more do
it without any effect upon the birth stat-
istics Knibbs doesn’t know. It is no use
pretending that this is a satisfactory state
of affairs. In thousands of cases it means
hurried marriages and in hundreds more it
means forced marriages, and both sorts are
liable to be disastrous; and disastrous mar-
riages are not good for the children, who are,
in this paper’s view, the chief consideration
in this matter.
The Bitter Wail for an Imported
Postage Stamp.
Tiie recent appeal of the Post Office to the
public to save postage stamps by using the
higher values, and so placing as few of the
little squares as possible on letters and
packets, and the threat of extra taxation
in cases where this is not done, is one of
the humors of the war. There is a shortage
of paper ; but that the economy should begin
with postage stamps, while hundreds of tons
of printed reports are b.eing wasted every
week, is too grotesque altogether, and that
the Government with small adhesive wares
to sell should threaten with penalties anyone
who buys too many of them seems about the
last thing in futility. It is true that the
paper for postage stamps is specially water-
marked, and that there is supposed to be
some protection against forgery in the letters
and signs which show on the labels when
held up to the light; but there can’t be much
in this idea when big postal countries, like
France, ignore it altogether. Plain paper,
on all the evidence, would do just as well;
and if there isn’t enough of it on which to
print the. stamps, a supply could be secured
by stopping some of the voluminous waste-
paper-basket literature of the Federal Gov-
ernment. Even if somebody did forge the
stamps and succeed in putting them on the
market in quantity—a much more difficult
thing than the actual forgery—the Postal
authorities, on their own showing, ought to
be thankful. If the Post Office is so helpless
that it threatens to punish those who use
its wares too freely, what possible objection
can it have to the forger coming along and
helping it out? Another point, too, in con-
nection with the affair ought to be noted. If
the Commonwealth Government hasn’t
enough of the paper it wants, why does it
issue stamps half as big again as those of
most countries? If the authorities won’t
allow the paper to be made here at least they
could get local artists to produce smaller
designs and effect an economy that way.
But the whole thing, first and last, is plain
humbug. The best paper that could be used
for printing stamps is the old hand-made
stuff which could be produced by a most
rudimentary plant in a suburban backyard
with the watermark in its place and every-
thing else in order. The raw material
wanted is simply a linen or cotton rag, and
the tariff-sinkers could comfort their souls
with the thought that, even if an Australian
industry did get started in this way, the
basis of the enterprise was still the shoddy
imported from overseas. The war isn’t doing
us much good, anyway, if it can’t enable us
to rise to the height of independence neces-
sary to produce a postage stamp.
♦
If conscription means militarism, as some
people say it does, then thank God for mili-
tarism ! When the German hordes had
poured through Belgium what chance would
France have had of bringing her men into
line if she had been obliged to Tevert to the
good old circus poster and the route marches
of the Wandering Wombats, the recruiting
methods of the voluntary system? With
France crushed and her fleet captured it
would have been all up with us. But, thanks
to “militarism,” no nation, taken by surprise
as France was, ever pulled herself together
so speedily and so effectively. Already
trained to the use of arms, her conscript
citizens stepped into the ranks as the call
came, until the world saw the splendid spec-
tacle of a magnificently-organised democracy
in which the business of the nation, civil and
military, moved as if by clockwork. And
when the edifice was put to the final test at
Verdun, the resilient but unbreakable wall
of France’s conscript armies proudly with-
stood the shock of Germany’s mightiest
efforts. If conscription really means mili-
tarism, which it doesn’t, because teaching
people how to fight doesn't necessarily make
them want to fight, then in remembering the
evils that Prussian militarism has brought
on the world the evils that French mili-
tarism has saved it from should not be for-
gotten. And to France, alone among all the
nations in the struggle—to her for proving
that a democracy can be great in war as in
peace, the world owes a debt it can never
pay. But it should repay as much as it
can ; and to-day is the time to do it.
[L. 0. Wilkinson, Hampton, N.S.W.]
The gallant anti-conscriptionists who said
that, having our own skins to save, we might
hire Russians to fill the gaps in the Aus-
tralian ranks, are not likely to say it again.
This is what they got from the Russian Con-
sul-General in Australia :—■
As a Russian I cannot allow to pass without
comment the anti-conscviptionist proposal to “hire”
Russian troops to fight instead of Australians. The
mere idea of such a thing is not only ridiculous, but
extremely offensive to every Russian, and I hope at
least equally offensive to all true Australian man
hood. Has not Russia ever since the outbreak of the
war been fighting just as much for all the Allies,
including Australia, as she has for her own self?
Has not every drop of Russian blood shed on the
battlefields of Galicia and Armenia contributed to
the safeguard of Australian liberty? \
The Russian soldier, thank God, fights f\om his
sense of patriotism and duty, and not because he
likes to. He fights for God, Czar and country, and
not from business motives. He fights for a sub-
lime ideal, but not for filthy lucre. Money will
never induce the Russian to fight, no matter how
Poor or ignorant a peasant he may be. He would,
indeed, feel inclined to fight against the man who
proposed to pay him for “professional assassina-
tion.” It is despicable enough for a man to refuse
to fight for his own country, but it is—at least in
Russian eyes—far more despicable still to hire
aliens to do so.

“Mizzen” : Is there any reason why, if
changes are made on the bridges of the
Commonwealth’s new line, Australian
masters and mates should not be given a
chance to apply for the jobs? Towards the
end of August the Federal Navigation Dept,
was certain of two things: (1) That a
marine superintendent was not going to be
.appointed at present; and (2) if one was
appointed later the fact would be announced
and applications invited. Well, the fact was
proclaimed later on with so much emphasis
that no one heard about it until it was dis-
covered that an “acting” superintendent had
been installed. When the first of the line —
the Australdale—arrived the other day it
was found that the master in charge was
leaving, and his place was being taken by a
master who had been sent from London as
passenger in another steamer. This man is
a master drawing a pension from the 8.1.
Co. As there happen to be men in Australia
to whom the job would have meant some-
thing, and who are not drawing pensions,
the reason for ruling them out might at any
rate be explained—if it can be.

A prominent N. S. Wales cleric has been
rushing his surplice into the limelight with
the declaration that Australia needs a “new
sense of sin.” The Bulletin thinks so,
too. Gaols are emptying, and so are the
churches; and it is evident that if those
places are not soon filled again the parson’s
job may have no gilt around its edges. A
few additions to the Ten Commandments
are therefore urgently required. Who is
going to make the additions does not seem
clear. Moses may or may not be dead;
and, even if he is, there are some splendid
Australians among the Light Horse who
could do just as well, and as they are in the
neighborhood of Sinai there is a unique
opportunity for them to get up to the peak
again and write the new sins down. First
on the supplementary table of stone should
be written the words: “Thou shalt not cry
Stinking Fish”; and the second: “Thou
shalt hold up thy head and say, ‘The other
fellow may do it better to-day, but I shall
make a start and lick him to-morrow.’ ”
With the assistance of adequate duties, no
Australian industry should be more bene-
fited by the war, when peace comes, than
woollens. In executing enormous orders for
a few types of khaki garments the mills
have demonstrated to the old stinking-fish
critics their ability to turn out a surpris-
ingly first-class article. They have special-
ised in certain cloths for uniforms and
have achieved the best results. ' The British
Tommy is envious of his antipodean cousin’s
duds. Billjim’s outfit looks well and wears
better. There are many Billjims who had
never worn Australian-woven clothes before
they went into camp. Now they hope to
be clad in local products when they have
done with the Hun and got back to ordinary
business. The immediate policy of the mills
when the abnormal Government demand for
khaki ceases should be to take up the trade
lines of least resistance, or no resistance at
all, instead of chasing the fashions as they
fly. The production of “latest” materials
and patterns could be left to British manu-
facturers who have a wider output and can
better afford to experiment in new designs,
whilst the local mills made specialities of
the plain cloths that are always in demand
and are chosen on their merits for dura-
bility and “finish”—the dark blue, grey and
brown stuffs which shrink not after being
out in a thunderstorm, nor stretch unduly,
nor get easily stained and spotted before
the. world. Also the various textile fabrics
which serve as trappings arfd suits of woe,
or evening dress, or for waiters. These are
the materials that could and should be so
well made in Australia as to justify their
protection by a prohibitive tariff—with
something with boiling oil in it for the
manufacturers who joined in any combine
to puff up prices and thereby defeat the
purposes of the national policy.
Annexing the Banana.
ivANDOLPH Bedford after inquiry:—
The bulk of the people of Fiji are seriously against
annexation to Australia, and for perfectly good
reasons. The handful of white people in the country
can be governed only on present lines unless the
Fijian is admitted to citizen rights, and that would
swamp the white man. Viti Levu alone is twice
the size of Jamaica and probably even more fertile.
Fiji is ideal rubber, sugar and cattle country, free
of malaria, and the one island of Viti Levu alone
will yet carry a bigger population than that of
Jamaica. Out of a total population of 140,000 or
so only about 4500 are vhites, and therein is an in-
surmountable reason against White Australia annex-
ing the group except as a Territory. Government
as a Territory of Australia might be better for the
white Fijian than Government as a Crown Colony
of Britain; yet the whites in Papua are as much
against the Commonwealth Government as if it
were worked from Downing-street. Cattle country
for leasing in Fiji is dearer than in North Aus-
tralia, and owing to the arrangements as to native
ownership it will continue so.. As the first reason
for any man’s being against the Government is the
condition of land tenure and rental, it is easy to
see that annexation would be up against trouble
with the first batch of new Australian settlers. But
the chief Australian objection is that the production
of a low-wage territory could not be permitted
export to a high-wage country like the Common-
wealth. The Crown Colony scheme is, indeed, the
only one possible at present. Under it, though there
are elected members, officials and nominees govern as
absolutely as if Downing-street were removed to
Suva. To the planter it is sympathetic, to the
native paternal, and what its attitude to the
C.S.R. Co. nobody 'appears to know.
The country doesn't need annexation, but it badly
calls for some ordinance to put the impudent C.S.R.
in its place. And it wants roads more than any-
thing. Its finances are all right; but it calls for
heavier taxation for development, and if it be pos-
sible it should have a land tax to incidentally force
hundreds of thousands of acres into work. The
Public Service, too, should gradually wipe out its
too-long pension list in favor of compulsory insur-
ance for all new officers. T notice that a retired
Chief Justice receives £9OO a year. I wonder
whether this was the judicial officer who in a land-
titles case had the Torrens Act mentioned to him
by counsel. He waved Torrens out of existence.
We don’t want to hear about any Australian experi-
mental legislation, ’ he said. And the planter wants
money on the Credit Foncier scheme. To the direct
money-lender he now pays 10 per cent., but that
is mere philanthropy compared with what he
suffers from the clutches of the merchants who
handcuff him more tightly than a tied house is held
by a brewery. On the .sentimental side an agitation
for annexation comes from the homesick Australian
Who compares Fiji with Australia to Fiji's disadvan-
tage, the more because of the di Here pees in trade,
opinion and feeling between Australian and English.
The English people won’t have Australian goods if
they can help it; even the Indian coolie won’t buy
tobacco manufactured in Australia, although certain
lines are now being exported to England. For this
misunderstanding the narrowness of the oversea
people seems mostly to blame. That narrowness in
l 1 iji prevents the Australian settler seeing that the
despotism of Downing-street is in the main bene-
ficent. And this is a pity, for Fiji is very Aus-
tralian, North Queensland on a big scale, in fact,
and the Australian is naturally at home in the
country.

The Cock and Bull Story and the Daily
Perjury.
During the last six months 7242 yarns have
been printed in Australian newspapers to
prove that Germany was on the verge of
being down and out. Also there have
appeared multitudinous efforts in expansive
type about victories and the enemy’s dwin-
dling man-power and empty bingy and all
the rest of it. The Bulletin has continu-
ally protested against this sort of thing, and
it protests again that the war news served
up to the people has been poison. Through
the combined influences of a censorship
which was either fat-headed or German, we
don’t know which, and a press stuffed with
pothouse brag, the public has been induced
to entertain ideas about the war that were
not only ludicrous but shndderingly danger-
ous. Instead of the public realising that
every month emphasised the magnitude of
the disturbance and the need for every ounce
of power the Allies were capable of exerting,
90 per cent, of the population have gone
around with mouths full of cocksure prophecy
about an early win. For this the Govern-
ment is very largely to blame, and most of
the trouble it now has on hand can be
directly attributed to its folly in giving a
prolonged imitation of an ostrich on the
sandhills. The evidence about Germany
beina on the down-grade has never been
worth 2d. Much of it has been manufactured
by the German himself, in order first to cool
off British recruiting and afterwards to
choke off conscription. The real testimony
that the Hun had a tremendous punch in
both hands has always been emphatic and is
that way still. The promptness with which
he hit Boumania shows his condition. A
country that can do a thing like that is
about as debilitated as an elephant in the
prime of life. Whether Germany over-
whelms Koumania or not, the readiness with
which Fritz has met the threatened danger
discloses him to be still so powerful that
we cannot afford to waste a pound that we
can put into our punch ; and glib assurances
about victory for the Allies being in sight
bear a close resemblance to treason. There
is no getting away from what this paper
has been saying (when it has been allowed
to) for over two years—that hopes of vic-
tory before the Allies are all-in are fatuous.
And most of them are all-in, or very near
it. Now what about Australia? Is she
going to take a punter’s chance or keep
busy ?
[Written to express the views of The Bulletin,
within the limitations prescribed under the War
Precautions Act, by S. H. Prior. D. M. Wright and
F. J. Kirby, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
Reflections for the Laborite wlvo says
that conscription is all right for home
defence but not for foreign service: —
Since the beginning of time the warrior has
always “gone forth” to war. In none of the
world’s battle-songs does the hero gird on his sword
and sit in the front parlor waiting to be sniped
through the window.
This war only once called on Germany at home.
The results were so appalling to the Germans that
the Kaiser wept over “my dear province of East
Prussia.”
About the only advantage Germany retains is
that all the fighting is going on in somebody vise’s
country. If Germany could have one day of the
Somme programme on German soil it would agree
to almost anything.
Where would England be if it had waited for the
war to come to it instead of going out and helping
to stop it far from home?
[Ernest O’Furr all, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
Killed in France, Captain C. A. Littler,
D.S.O. At the time of the V.D.L. Bank
smash, Littler was manager of the branch
at Zeehan (Tas.), and was transferred
to Hobart as one of the liquida-
tors. He afterwards started in busi-
ness at Devonport, and, later, was
general manager of the North-Western
Farmers’ Ass., which has branches in all the
coast towns. Rubber took him to the Phil-
ippines, and he did a little contracting for
supplies for Uncle Sham’s army. When
Europe went mad, the ex-banker remem-
bered he had had some experience in the
old Tasmanian Rangers, and was soon in
uniform again. He won his D.S.O. at Gal-
lipoli. Two sons at the Front are both
wounded, and one has just been awarded the
Military Cross.
The vacancy for a F.M. at Port Pirie
(S.A.), caused by the promotion of S. J.
Mitchell to Adelaide, is to be filled by Noel
Webb, one of Adelaide’s numerous literary
lawyers. Webb is a solid man every way—
a crack rifleshot, among other things, and an
enthusiast on that subject. Years ago he
did good work as mayor of another outport,
Augusta. Since moving to Adelaide he has
been in partnership with the Nesbits, and
latterly with Kelly, formerly of Darwin.
Now that Webb has gone on to the Bench.
Kelly joins Barwell. another country lawyer
come to town as M.L.A.

Last week Sydney opera-goers listened to
tlie melodies of Donizetti’s “La Favorita.”
The pretty stranger was an immediate
success. In spite of the occasional vagaries
of the horn department the orchestra was in
good form. The fiddlers showed up much
better than usual —inspired, perhaps, by an
almost ferocious demeanor on the part of
their usually placid conductor Gonsalez.
With more consistent support tenor Cappelli
surprised even his- warmest admirers. As
Fernando, the novice who forsakes the mon-
astic life for the love of Leonora (Miss
Russ), he was an alchemist who turned his
music into gems. . After a glittering interval
as a hero, Fernando returns to the Simple
Life. In monk’s garb, plus a heavy mous-
tache, he sings the famous “Spirito Gentil,”
while the repentant Leonora dies by inches
at his feet. But, of course, she has first to
warble a death-song against Fernando’s
shirt-front. It was a tremolo in excelsis.
Belloui, a majestic figure as King Alfonso,
suffered from the same vocal ague. Though
Cacialli was evidently in pain from a
damaged ankle, his voice was as sure-footed
as usual. liis big basso did its duty behind
the solemn beard, of a chief monk or papal
delegate. The chorus (did Gonsalez shake it
up with a threatening fist?) was in tune
from start to finish.

Tlie fire is dying down, and “While the

The success of Madge Fabian in
“Romance” has happily led to an extension
of the season at Sydney Criterion. Edward
Sheldon’s play is so admirably written that
it improves on acquaintance; and there
aren’t many (or any) other American plays
of which that can be said. Frank Harvey,
when lie isn't deplorably bishing, is usually
pillowing his head on his arms and shaking
with emotion. He shakes well, too.

play at the Palace (Sydney), is still going
strong. The Bulletin mentions . tliis in
case a stranger happens to drop into the
city from Alice Springs.
