The St. Ivilda (Melb.) show season
started last Saturday with a rush to Arca-
dia, where the Pink Dandies have dug-in
for the summer. Nigel Brock’s little crowd
of Dandies have a pretty pink setting behind
and above them, and a pretty bright orches-
tra in front. Also they break the mon-
otony of their frivol by changing their cos-
tume two or three times, instead of remain-
ing stereotyped seaside entertainers to the
end. This seems the more necessary
because the programme has no burlesque or
vaudeville turns to speak of, one of the
comedians having been called away to a
temporary engagement at a private hospital.
The combination as it stands is practically
a humorous concert -company of educated
vocalists, who shine in the concerted num-
bers and rarely permit any item to
remain a solo. The baritone, Marshall
Lawrence, has a father tremendous voice:
the comedienne, Ida Newton, is quite a
modest little Idaisy in her way; Joseph
Brennan treats some ancient pantomime
business with a light, graceful touch ; Lalla
Ward has a nice soprano voice, and the
other half-dozen contribute freely to the
show’s musical air of refinement.
The. absence of anything like intelligent
conduct in “The Matinee Girl” and “In Joy-
land” was responsible for The Bulletin
spending another mournful evening at Mel-
bourne Princess’s on Saturday. The humor
is again the most painful feature, each
comedian revealing inexplicable ecstasy in
his attempts to pinch his partner’s surplus
cash. Quantities of dollar bills change
hands by pocket-picking, crude swindles and
other doubtful transactions; in fact, the
performance is mostly a display ot uncle
Samuel’s favorite reading. Meanwhile Lew
Dunbar puts the English language to ter-
rible uses in strange altercations with Lon
Moreni. The latter, apparently replacing
the recent Yiddish jester, Murray J. Simons,
arouses commiseration by his efforts to load
an English johnny part with mote vacuity
"THE FOLLIES ,” AT SYDNEY TIVOLI.
Walter Weems and Billy Rego.
than the character can bear. The sihgiag
ranges from good in the case ot the
Duquesne quartette to worse; and the chorus
provides the usual physical backing, though
its legs are not availed of with the old
enthusiasm.

The first part of the Melbourne Tivoli
bill is no better for the reappearance of Dan
Ahern, the whistling fiddler, who is nothing
much of either. The second part remains
in the hands of the Strollers, who keep busy
for 70 minutes without giving encores, lheii
programme was somewhat . changed o 1
Saturday, two fresh items being a skit on

company and a new naval song wherein
George Gray stone defies the Germans with
the utmost vigor of his bass.
Official pictures of the Somme fighting
brought the Big Push very close to Hoyts
supporters in Melbourne last Saturday, d.he
film, which is nearly a mile m length, is
the most intimate record of actual war con-
ditions that has yet been screened in this
part of the world. The camera hasn t
spared the spectator many details; but still
if he gets depressed by the sight of the
casualties he will also be heartened by the
portents of victory as shown by the huge
accumulations of guns and the bright, con-
fident grin of Thomas Atkins. Some of the
best parts of the picture depict innumerable
Mr. Atkins marching gaily into battle with
a song on liis lips. In Sydney the pictuie
is shown at the Lyceum and Crystal Palace.
Branscombe’s various Dandies have long
had the Exhibition Garden in Adel-
aide. but this summer they cross over
North Terrace to the Austral Gardens.
Wondergraph pictures also start their
open-air branch. At the Tivoli, Andei-
son puts up the unlikely but strongly
Australian “Bush Girl,” and promises
Maggie Moore to follow. At the Majestic
are now Early and Laight and the Raleighs,
and in the “revue” half Jack Kearns con-
tinues to stand about and score off his com-
panions in the Jack Cannot manner.

AT POVERTY POINT.
“Fax”: There is beauty and talent in the
Williamson chorus as of: old, and better
"style” than ever there was, but the vocal
strength of chorus and principals seems to
have steadily declined within the past 39
or 20 years. We get a new sweet voice of mag-
nitude occasionally, though Gladys Monerieff
is the only one of recent note ; or a clever,
sparkling Dorothy Brunton with a nice little
voice; or a winsome Nellie Payne, who
has no singing powers to speak of. But
from the two or three musical companies
that tour under Williamson management
nowadays you couldn’t pick an array of
vocal talent to compare with what the Firm
had under engagement, on and off, during
the late ’Bo’s and early ,’9o’s. Australia
isn’t producing the wealth of voices that she
did when Melba was a girl. And that’s all
about it. Florence Young at her very best
was only one in a little bunch of good,
honest songstresses with plenty of stuff in
their notes. She was contemporary with
Nellie Stewart in her prime, Flo Granpner,
Violet Yarley, Fanny Liddiard, Ida Osborne,
May Pollard and two or three others who
had voice enough to let an audience hear all
the words of their ditties. The Firm has
never had such an abundance of material
within the present century. Bright new
voices are heard in the chorus but infre-
quently, and if the owners also possess a
bright intelligence and appearance they soon
take their goods to a seaside showman and
become Dandies, or Buttercups, or Friskies,
or something.
James W. Hazlitt, manager for J.C.W.
at Sydney Criterion, has just lost another
son, this one in France. He was a sergeant
in the 51st Westralian Battalion, was
wounded at the Front and being removed
to a hospital at Cambridge died there on
September 21. Twelve months ago Mr.
Ilazlitt lost his eldest son. the well-known
cricketer. All Poverty Point sympathises
with him.
“Minim” : Died the other day, at Uxbridge,
England, Dr. Osmond Carr, who was respon-
sible for the score of “Morocco Bound,” the
first of the present type of musical comedy.
After the Gilbert-Sullivan split the librettist
turned to him, and together they produced
“His Excellency.” But either the vein had
been worked out or the new partnership
didn’t know how to tackle it.
“C.M.”: “Lookout Man” (B. 12/10/T6)
ought to get down to earth. He says that
the forthcoming production of “Damaged
Goods” shows that the J.C.W. management
“even cares for Australian morals.” The
question discussed in the Brieux play is not
morals but health. He has got fact mixed
with conventions. If he would climb down
from his perch and get a copy of the play
lie would realise that he was one of the
benighted crowd Brieux is addressing.
Shaw in his preface to “Damaged Goods”
also has a word for him when he says, “One
hardly knetos whether to laugh or cry at
such perverse stupidity.”
The lovely anti-conscription suggestion
that Russian troops should be hired to sup-
plv Australian deficiencies was worthy of
Deacon Skinner, the sanctimonious rascal in
“Struck Oil.” The Deacon, you remember,
engaged honest John Stofel, the Dutchman,
to serve ns his “substitution” in the Ameri-
can Civil War.
Pinero’s “Sweet Lavender” doesn’t strike
one’s recollections as being the sort of senti-
mental comedy that could be effectively
adapted for the film. Nevertheless, the
author received £lOOO for the cinema rights.
Many another play, almost forgotten, has
lately earned a fat fee for the man who
thought, when lie was writing it, that dia-
logue was necessary. To-day he gets quite
a lot of money for permitting the creatures
of his brain to flit like silent ghosts across
the screen.

"Glen II.”: It is a fixed delusion with
the Firm that its patrons do not care tup-
pence about the "book" of a musical comedy
provided the "production" is all right. Tin-
result is that practically no musical play is
put on here until its book has been merci-
lessly backed, its plot cut off in the flower
of its youth, so to speak, and some of its
musical numbers fired to make way for
others, often absurdly unsuitable. “The
“Cinema Star" is tiie latest case in point.
It was fixed up by Jean Gilbert. J. Ilulbert
and Harry Graham, two of them, at least,
experienced artificers; but so little of their
work remains in the version presented at
Her Majesty’s (Melb.) that the manage-
ment has not the heart—or “hide”—to put
the names of authors and composer on the

programme, although, naturally, the "pro-
ducer” is mentioned in large type. Appar-
ently when the firm buys a London musical-
comedy success nowadays it pays for the
name, for often this is about all that is left
of the original script after the “producer”
and comedians have done their worst. Some
day managements may realise that a good
“book” is one of the things most likely to

vided iu this respect.

“J.F.H.”: Queenslander William Archer,
leading London dramatic critic, lias been
stirring up a hornet’s nest by a slashing
attack on the variety theatres and the frivol-
ous fare they provide. From a literary stand-
point their songs are certainlv beneath con-
tempt. In 50 years not one of them
has made a permanent contribution to Eng-
lish literature. The Bananalander suggests
as a remedy something after the fashion of
the U.S.A. Drama League, which draws up
a report upon every production of note, and
circulates it among its thousands of sub-
scribers, thus providing a guide to intelligent
play-going. He would like to see in London
“a list, easily accessible to everybody, of
those entertainments which decent and intel-
ligent people regard as generally desirable —
a handy guide to respectable and not too silly
entertainments." Such a catalogue certainly
would have its value. The person who
wanted mere amusement—as most persons
who pay at the ticket office do —would know
where not to go to get it.
"F.N.T.” : Probably the least exciting
bob’s worth offered by any Melbourne show-
management is at the Aquarium. There is
an orchestra, the seals give a performance
every day. afternoon-tea is on tap, and there
are plenty of fish to look at —altogether a
pleasant, quiet afternoon’s entertainment for
Bill and bis girl when they want to get away
from the crowd. However, there are weeds
in most gardens. For some unexplained
reason the Aquarium people have these many
years included a trained-dog performance,
and latterly there have been monkeys as
well. Neither of these animals has anything
to do with lish. It may be urged that the
most popular turn the Aquarium ever pro-
vided wasn’t a fish-act either. Thai was the
Kellcrman swimming and diving exhibition.
But a trained dog is such a poor, shivering,
humble, frightened, anxious thing that it is
hard to understand why the Aquarium
authorities don't “have a heart,” and just
stick to their lish. If they can’t make a
success of things without pressing a row of
cringing, barbered dogs into their service the
place ought to be shut up.
Dear Bulletin,—l have been a playgoer
all my life. True, I have never paid for my
seats. But I have supported the theatre at
all times and seasons. On no occasion have
l refused to accept a pass. Many and many
a night. I can truthfully claim, the curtain
would have been raised on a beggarly array
of empty benches but for me and a few rela-
tives ana friends. Also, I have attended
dutifully in all weathers —wet, muggy, torrid
and frosty—especially frosty. I have
a pula tided, with the help of the ushers, more
rotten plays than I can count. I have laughed
and set the house in a roar at the perspiring
comedy man when, but for my .presence, the
poor wretch would have been unable to raise
a smile. I have cheered with might and main
every Neutral piece that lias been put on—
and Heaven knows there have been many!
1 have made it my business to encourage the
down-hearted Yankee mummers, far from
their homeland, who have obviously been
overburdened with grief at the thought that
Australian fooling has been against their
country. In short, I have done more —much
more—for the drama than hundreds of blase
“regular” patrons who pay for their seats
only when the word goes round that the show
and the legs are worth seeing. And what is
my reward V A cold-blooded, ungrateful
Federal Treasurer threatens to make me
plank down my money at the box-office &nd
tax me for doing it! Not if I know it, sir.
The tb“atre« can go to tlm Devil las they
assuredly will) for me! Henceforth I shall
stay away from them. —Yours, Ex-Free-
Lister.

“Hamer”: The Clerks’ Union is a busy
body in X. S. Wales just now. Since it
secured the right, after a fight extending over
seven years, to turn the crank of the Indus-
trial Arbitration Act, thousands of pen-
pusliers have become unionists, and. section
by section, awards are being made. Last week
a log for wharf and tally clerks was issued,
rates being fixed at Is. lid. per hour, with a
minimum of four hours on any job, rates and
a half for overtime, and double rates for holi-
days, Sundays and meal hours. The scale is
equivalent to about £4 4s. a week. In another
sectional award £2 12s. 6d. was lixed as the
minimum weekly wage, it being, apparently,
Hie idea of the Wages Board to consider the
general wage rates in the industry in which
the clerks are employed, rather than the
reponsibilities of their work. Generally the
awards are improving the conditions, and the
traditional 30-bob-a-week clerk is presently
to disappear. The fly in the ointment is the
refusal of tin 1 Boards to recognise the prin-
ciple of equal pay for equal work done by
men and women. .Jill may still be secured at
about one-half or two-thirds of the salary
that .Tack commands, and so she remains a
menace to goodwill and Christian amity.
Killed in action, Captain J. McNamara,
good sport and dead shot, lie represented
the Speck at the inter-State rifle matches
of lIKIS, 11410 and 1012. In 1908 he scored
the Launceston double —the grand aggre-
gate, carrying the Gardiner trophy, and the
champion medal : in 1909 he won the north-
ern district championship: and at the
I’ II.A. meeting of 1913 he carried off the
State championship.
.1. I*. Piekburn, just appointed to act as
District Court Judge in X. S. Wales, is a
son of a former well-known Sydney doctor.
His new Honor graduated at Sydney Uni. in
1892, and took his LL.B. in '95, when he
was admitted to the X.S.W. Bar. lie has
not yet taken silk. With him goes up —for

of X. S. Wales's highly-respected old
gaitered water-waggoner.
A female old age pensioner who died at
Bendigo the other day took no chances in the
matter of securing Christian burial. She was
found lying dead in her house, and on the
premises was a document which set forth
that in return for a certain sum a local
undertaker agreed to do all that was neces-
sary. Receipts showed that the full amount
agreed upon had been paid by instalments
extending over three years, so the prudent
old dame was well and truly tucked away.
“Charter -Party”: An irate Slav sailed
from Port Jackson last week when the
skipper of the Russian barque Lindisfarne
got under weigh. Three months ago the
vessel arrived, and after discharging cargo
was about to leave for Newcastle to load
coal for Chile, thence with nitrates to
France. But the Commonwealth authori-
ties wouldn't allow her to take coal to
Chile, nor would they allow her to go to sea
empty. Instead, they ordered the skipper
to load wheat. He dug himself in behind
his charter and refused. For months the
dispute raged, while the Lindisfarne lay idle
and the world at large howled for tonnage.
Finally, on an appeal to the Czar or some-
body, the Russian skipper won, and his
ship departed in ballast and much danmski.
No enemy critic can say that the hated
Australian army can't read and write. The
last inward English mail (most of it pencil-
scribbled in France) created a new record,
with 850,000 letters, besides newspapers and
registered articles. After all, it’s only the
proud result of compulsory education, which
the friends of “freedom” were violently kick-
ing back less than a generation ago. Every
honest, decent thing has ancient boot-marks
on it somewhere.
Although woman is suspect amongst the
army of pen-pushers, Miss Rose Smith has
been nominated by the craft to sit as assessor
with Justice Hodges in the Victorian State
Court of Industrial Appeals. She is not
the first of the long-haired sex to sit as
member of a wages board, but she will be
the first woman in A’ic. to act in a judicial
capacity.
They don’t treat a man as they find him
nowadays in Maoriland. One citizen who
tried to do it at Tauranga last week has to
pay £5 under the Act which forbids “shout-
ing.” It was the first penalty of the kind
since the war strategists in Parliament en-
listed Jimmy Woods.

THE AMUSEMENT TAX.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My Kate has heard of the. intent
of an exacting Government
Amusement taxes to impose.
She wonders how they’ll gather those.
Her most perplexing problem this is
How will the wretches tax- oar kisses?
And 1 an explanation vamp —-
They may devise a duty stamp.
She smiles and cheerfullv insists,
“We all will be philatelists! - ’
But pouts again. “In time, no doubt,
Their duty will stamp kissing out.”
“Fear not,” I answer, “ ’neath the rose,
Where no intrusive taxman goes,
Still urgent youth and yielding beauty
Will kiss for love and not for duty!"
VICTORIA.

THIS FORMULA REALLY MAKES
HAIR GROW.
Some time ago I read in a paper a formula
for a preparation which was claimed to
destroy the dandruff germ and to strongly
stimulate the hair-growth. In spite of the
fact that I had already tried many prepara-
tions without benefit, and my hair was thin
and falling out very fast, I decided to give
the formula a trial. Accordingly I got my
chemist to put up 3oz. of Bay Rum, loz. of
Lavona de Composee, and 5-dram of Menthol
Crystals, and applied it regularly every morn-
ing and night, rubbing carefully into the
scalp with the finger-tips. Much to my
delight and astonishment the intolerable
itching ceased with the first application, the
dandruff disappeared, the falling out of the
hair stopped completely, and by the time I
had used four bottles I had a finer, longer
and more luxuriant growth of hair than I
ever had before. I have since recommended
this formula to many of my friends with
equally wonderful results. I understand that
this formula can be prepared by all chemists
and druggists, and I strongly advise all who
have'not yet given it a trial to do so imme-
diately.-— I T.H.P. — Advt.

A Bulletin man went to buy a book :
The young man was dusting the Psalms and psy-
chological novels. “Good morning. Have you used
—I mean, have you a copy of ‘Sabotage’ ?” “The
Cabbage Yard,” he replied, meditatively flicking a
grain of sand from “The Sins of Susie.” “Let me
see—-‘Cab’ ” “No —S-a-b-o-t-a-g-e. The I.W.W.
yelp.” “Nothing like that here, sir; never heard
of it.” And the young man backed away as from
a snake. In the next shop the proprietor’s deputy
asked if his customer wanted to get him life. In
the third the attendant ran away. The fourth was
a pub. At the fifth the boss said he was as deaf

as a post. In the sixth three respectable ladies
fainted when the youth in charge lifted up his voice
and bleated “ ‘Sabotage’! We do not deal in that
kind of literature.” The next was reputed a bit of
a rebel. “Sorry, old man,” he explained. “Try Squith.”
Squith said he thought he could manage it. But
he would only promise to deliver the goods under
cover of night. And that is what a man is driven
to in search of knowledge. It is easier to buy
elephants and baboons in Sydney now than to get
first-hand particulars of this new cult —and when
you do get them they read like Callan Park holding
a public meeting over a couple of prawns.
“Uloola” : A word in the ear of the fat-
heads who comfort themselves with the
thought that Germany is up against it in
the matter of supplies. Early in August,
when the big punch was being delivered,
we rushed some enemy dugouts. They were
80ft. to 40ft. deep and lighted with electri-
city. The captured mess tins had butter
and tins of brawn in them, there were loaves
of brown bread in the haversacks, all the
overcoats were new, and the clean under-
clothing that we got made us inclined to
forgive Fritz his sins.
* * *
Jack Sommers meets the Quality:—
I’ve just come back from a visit to the Idle Rich
—a huge country house, a disgracefully opulent
hostess, clock golf and Aunt Sally on the lawn.
Other items were brass-buttoned flunkeys carrying
trays and gaping Tommies wondering whether to
call ’em Sir. We had tea in the baronial halls, sur-
rounded by freak clocks, imitation Murillos and
beautiful tapestries—“like a bloomin’ museum,” as
Atkins justly remarked.
I started to talk to break the awed silence while
the Tommies rolled their eyes appreciatively and
replied in cake-muffled voices. The hostess said it
was sweet of me to come all the long way from
Australia to fight. I played goff and won a jar
of barley sugar. I’m going to be photographed
leaning on it. I threw sticks at Aunt Sally and
said I vastly missed my boomerang. There was an
immense garden and two platoons of gardeners, but
the ladies regretfully explained that the war had
set the garden back, “Oh, shockingly.” We expressed
our regrets and said we’d get the war over as soon
as possible.
The hostess opined that it (the war) was the
last of a natural series of events recurring at set
periods, and added that the peacocks roosted high
at night. 1 played with a Pekinese spaniel and
smiled. Afterwards we motored through squalid
streets and nearly ran over ragged little kids who
cried, “Hooray!” Women stood at the doors, and
little tickets in the windows with red and black
crosses advertised how many of the family had
enlisted or were dead. Prayers, after capturing a
canary that had escaped to the delights of the
garden, and so virtuously to bed. God save the
King !
* * * *
“E.J.C.”: The joke has all the camp
laughing. One day 30-odd men got their
marching orders for the following morning.
It was pretty short notice, so after serving
out their accoutrements, etc., the O.G.
promised them the afternoon “off” to say
their good-byes. Just at midday, however,
an order came from Headquarters that the
District Commandant would inspect the men
at 8.30 p.m. This put a damper on things.
Then heads got together and mates were
called in to help. In the afternoon when the
squad fell in for inspection the O.C. nearly
dropped dead when lie saw 33 men who are
not likely to be leaving for months lined up
in service gear. Knowing that if the truth
were revealed it meant something nasty the
O.C. was oyster, and a dignified Commandant
solemnly warned the reeruities of the dangers
and temptations on the other side of the
water, and besought them to keep up the
reputation of Australia, as he felt sure they
would. Then 33 men winked unanimously
and went back to camp to peel off their ser-
vice gear and wait for their pals to come
back after having their last beer.
* * * *
The anti-conscriptionists have had a busy
week explaining that they have nothing at
all to do with the I.W.W. They didn’t men-
tion that a fortnight ago. They only discov-
ered it when evidence was produced
in Sydney connecting members of the
I.W.W. with the recent fierce outbreak
of incendiarism, which threw hundreds
of men out of work and imperilled
the jobs of thousands more. But anti-
conscription can’t shake off its friends like
that. It is the tool of the 1.W.W.,
just as the I.W.W. itself is the tool
of the German concentration camp. The
mysterious "Long Franz,” one of the escapees
from Holdsworthy, was in the thick of the
pleasant little surprise which the police have
been unfolding in the court in Sydney. He
was the honored guest of the I.W.W. men
who are now in safe keeping; for his sake,
according to one witness, one leader at least
of the I.W.W. was prepared to commit
murder. “Long Franz” has earned his Iron
Cross, at any rate. But how he must chuckle
as he sees the Australian worker (whom he
loves so much that he was prepared to burn
him out of a job) dancing to a German tune,
and voting “No” in the name of Australian
patriotism !
[S. H. Prior, 214 George-street, Sydney.]

“E.T.”: Shades of W. S. Gilbert! A
soldier outward-bound for the Front in Aug-
ust had six hours’ leave at Fremantle. While
returning to the transport after posting a
letter to his mother he was run down
and killed on the railway. Last week the
mother of the deceased received an official
letter advising her that the payment of the
allotment money “had been cancelled on
and from Oct. 6 on account of the soldier
failing to re-embark at Fremantle!”

That splendid worker for the wounded
Billjim in England, Alice Grant Rosman,
writes from London :
My note in The Bulletin last November, asking
for old copies of Tiie Bulletin to be sent for
wounded Australians, brought me a great response. We
had hundreds every mail for nearly six months, and
you may be certain they were never wasted. Every-
one took a vivid interest in the “pink post,” which
was sent all over Great Britain. As for the letters

they brought us—well, it rather made one feel there
was nothing really worth while for any Australian
woman to do except spend her days posting Bulle-
tins to her brothers. Now many of these boys
are back in hospital for a second time —wounded at
Pozieres. They are writing daily for papers and, as
fate will have it, the supply has gradually fallen
off, just when we have more than 20,000 in the
hospital lists. Would you kindly put in a word or
two for me, for I’m sure the people at home will
send them when they hear how tremendously they
are appreciated? It may interest you and the boys to
know that copies have been coming regularly not
only from all over Australia, but from Maoriland,
Fiji, San Francisco and Hongkong, and one has been
posted regularly from somewhere in the remotest
south of South America. Perhaps you would let me
say “Thanks,” too, to all these people; for myself
I have been ten thousand times repaid by the grati-

“0.K.” : The paragraph re “The Case of
John Verran v. Germans, in the South Aus-
tralian Assembly” (B. 5/10/T6), deepens
my conviction that all the alien-blooded citi-
zens of the Commonwealth should be offici-
ally classified in the districts where they
abide. Many Germans and Austrians, born
here or abroad, are “enemy aliens.” Many
others are not. The truth about all these
people is quite easily procurable. Even in
crowded Melbourne, where treacherous and
disloyal sentiment is not placed so easily as
in a little country township, any intelligent
policeman could collect convincing evidence
as to which Germans celebrated the sinking
of the Lusitania and which of them didn’t.
There are Huns who got drunk in honor of
the Sinn Fein rebellion, and Teutons who
had no sympathy with it. No occasion to
cry “Let them lie crucified!” Let ’em be
classified.

“Transport”: The announcement that in
future Australian notes will be worth their
face value in Colombo, Durban, Capetown,
Plymouth and London is very welcome to
troops and men employed on transport duty;
also, it is none too premature. Hitherto, in
London, Cook’s gave 15s. for every pound’s
worth of Australian silver, and in other parts
of England silver bearing the kangaroo and
emu w_as not negotiable. In Colombo a per-
son presenting an Australian fl note would
invariably receive 19s. Gd. in English silver;
and then for every 2s. of this English money
tendered 80 cents were deducted as exchange.
The net loss therefore was about 9f times 30
cents plus Gd. (paid in first instance) =4s. 4d.
Seeing that troops and transport crews re-
ceive their allotment in Australian paper
money, the loss has been a serious one. The
Government asks the public to hapd over all
gold to the banks; but it was two years
before it took steps to prevent soldiers and
sailors on transports from being victim-
ised through obeying that instruction.
DREAD OF CHANGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I hate to shift, I hate to leave;
At ev’ry parting I must grieve;
For spectres I would like to stun
Remark, “Another period done!
Another corner turned ! My friend,
We’re one step nearer to The End ! ’
And if the Past you try to taste
By turning back
Upon your track,
You’ll find your time has gone to waste!
For nothing’s ever quite the same
In this tremendous living game;
No day is like the next—-no hour
Can e’er be matched. The loveliest fiow’r
In any garden finds its mate ;
But though the splendid fact you hate,
You cannot find to-morrow’s twin.
So ev’ry year Spring ushers in
Is full of strangeness. That is why
Most people do not wish to die.
Take Life just as it conies ! Be gay
And let Time feed you day by day.
Don’t stare behind or peep before
Or see a sword behind the door.
Don’t view all moves with consternation,
Or try to do Life’s punctuation.
But slap yourself upon the chest
And tell yourself to-day’s the best
Damned day you ever saw ! Don’t sorrow —-
And look for Something Great to-morrow!
’Tis good advice ! You saw me bake it !. .. .
/ wish to Gocl that I could take it!
N.S.w.

After a careful trial Jethro Brown has
resigned the Presidency of the Industrial
Court in South Aus. As he had obtained a
mere leave-of-absence from his duties as
Professor of Law at Adelaide University, he
must have contemplated a possible retreat.
He followed Judge Buchanan, the original
President, who was put definitely on
the Supreme Court Bench last January. It
was a popular appointment, chiefly because
of his fine judicial mind and deep knowledge
of economics, though the profession was
inclined to think that his lack of experi-
ence in court practice would tell against
him. As it turned out, the work proved
to be too heavy for him —perhaps for
any one man. Brown has been work-
ing 10 hours a day, seven days a week ;
and the new business of adjusting hotel-
rents on the early-closing Act is still
untouched. In view of this a Bill
was run through the S.A. Assembly
giving the President the status and
powers of a Supreme Court Judge,
and allowing him the help of another
Judge when urgently required. But the
Leg. Council refused him the status, and
offered him a stipendiary magistrate as
assistant. So he threw in his resignation.

“Boyd Cable”: In the argument on the
“man-stopping” value of various bullets (B.
S/6/’l6) an important factor appears to
have been overlooked—the variety of man to
be stopped. It may be true that the high-
velocity nickelled bullet has little stopping
power, but it has quite sufficient to do its job
against the ordinary civilised nations’ fight-
ing men. I’ve seen a good, stout, husky
specimen dropped as if pole-axed by a clean
bullet wound through the arm, and I’ve seen
hundreds of men wending their way back
from the front line casualtied by bullet
wounds that would certainly not have pre-
vented their doing another strenuous half-
hour’s scrapping. But modern fighters know
top much about the results of apparently
trivial wounds, and don’t want to waste time
in getting an injury dressed and some anti-
NOT IN THE AGREEMENT.
"You said you would come to me if Hell
yawned between us.”
“Oh, yes, but—you know, lust night it rained
like billy-oh.”
tetanus mixture pumped into them. So they
go down to a wound that a Soudanese black
would just ignore. On the other hand I’ve
seen a Maoriland red deer go off gaily at
about 60m.p.h. with a high-velocity hole
drilled through and through his vitals, and
it is a matter of history that our troops have
found even the smashing Martini-Henry .45
calibre leaden pill insufficient to full-stop an
Afghan ghazi who was wading into business
behind a large-sized tulwar. Therefore my own
opinion is that for savages, deer, big game
and other animals not sufficiently educated
to know when they are casualtied, the small,
high-velocity bullet is no good. But for white
troops or even an untamed burglar it will
do the trick.

A tale of people who have more money
than is good for them was unfolded to Jus-
tice Hood in Melbourne lately, when Mrs.
Ellen Claye, of South Yarra, called upon
Mrs. Agnes J. B. Falconer to deliver up and
hand over to her the body of her son, Samuel
John Claye, an “infant” aged 20 years 11
months and 29 days. The
narrative of Mrs. Falconer
was that, one evening in
July, ’l5, Samuel bor-
rowed 2s. from his mother,
and married Mrs. Fal-
coner’s sister, aged 20.
Two months later he had
transferred his affections
to Mrs. Falconer herself,
she being a grass widow
aged 23 or so. After that
the pair dwelt at many
boarding-houses, some-
times together, sometimes
apart. A month back the
two : sisters and Claye were
housekeeping at Elstern-
wick, the real wife acting
as chaperon; but three
weeks ago, “after a slight
argument,” she left for
parts unknown. Claye
also disappeared when the
writ was served, and his
lady assured his Honor
that she hadn’t the least
idea where he was either.
The unusualness of the
tale wasn’t lessened by the
fact that, while Claye
“comes into a large sum
of money” when he is 21,
his wife has £14,000 and
Mrs. Falconer an income
of £7OO a year. Justice
Hood declined to swallow
a lot of th,e yarn, but he
couldn’t get over the mar-
riage certificate, which gave the wife
a better claim than the mother to the
person of Sammy Claye, so he merely invited
Mrs. Falconer to pay her own costs and drew
the law’s attention to certain irregularities
in the marriage certificate.

“Snell”: Tentover’s Kate had mysteri-
ously disappeared. She had lost two young
men* at the war, and a third had had a
wing amputated. Kate was discouraged;
and now she was absent in circumstances
provocative of the greatest alarm. Her
mother, searching for a clue, discovered a
note under the butter-dish curtly informing
the family that she was “full up,” and her
body would be found in the river below the
ironstone bar. There was a rush for the
river, and Kate was found sitting on a
semi-submerged log, thinking it over. She
was asked to go home. She refused—she
was going to drown herself, and people
ought to mind their own business. Very

How Billjim chases Johnny Turk
It was a 40-mile ride, and from 7 a.m. that day till 8
p.m. we had nothing but a couple of biscuits which
we ate as we rode along. After tea we again mounted
and travelled all that night. At daybreak we halted
and had a snack, and again started off, continuing all
day except for one or two short spells for the horses.
We didn’t travel that night, but slept—the first rest
after two days and a night in the saddle. It’s just
as well a fellow has seven skins. . I still had one
to go. My only fear that night was that the Turks
would shell us and disturb my sleep. But they
showed their consideration. At daylight we started
again, riding still farther out into what I thought
might be Asia Minor, though I was too uninterested
to think much, i was not an exception either. We
had had two or three days of 124 degrees in the
shade, and there was no shade. No wonder we are
all anxious to get out of Egypt. Every day the tem-
perature is 100 degrees or more, and we have to work
in it. And there’s lots of work in connection with
Light Horse on Active Service.
All our supplies have to be carted about from place
to place, both for the horses and ourselves, and a
hundred small jobs are done in the scorching sun
and sand. To make things worse there is no com-
fortable rest to look forward to when it's over. Just
throw yourself down in the dirty sand of the tent
and let the flies chew your ears off. Flies aren't the
only things either.
At 3 o’clock next morning we started off again.
We rode for about six or eight miles, and then
camped at a place called Romani. There is nothing
to distinguish Romani from any other piece of sand,
no way of knowing its boundaries, where it begins or
where it ends. We were told its name was. Romani,
and we let it go at that.
Being in enemy country and close upon the Turks
Active Service is very active. A chap can hardly
visit a friend in an adjacent tent without carrying
rifle and ammunition, full water-bottle and 24 hours’
iron rations with him. “Iron rations” are bully beef
and biscuits. Every morning it’s stand to arms from
3.30 till 4.30, and then commence the daily routine.
One day last week we came up with the Turkish
outposts, and I never laughed more in all my life.
We rode out of camp at midday, rode all that after-
noon and all that night, only stopping once or twice
to give the horses five minutes’ blow. Just as
day was breaking we sighted Turks ahead. Terrific
excitement, of course. The Turks had seen us, and
were getting away over the hills as fast as they
could. Most of them had mounted their camels, but
one or two were too frightened to waste the second
that it would take to board, and so were scampering
away on foot. Of course, when we saw the possi-
bility of their getting away from us (camels, by the
way', are faster than horses) it was the end of every-
thing. We ripped the spurs into the horses’ sides,
and then, remembering that we could not go without
the command of the officer, pulled them up with
sufficient suddenness to stop their hearts beating.
Well, we waited for three or four seconds, which
really seemed like three or four hours, and our officer
did not give an order; so, like Australians, we took
the business into our own hands. Some went to the
left, others dashed off to the right, and my section,
four men, made one terrific plunge and bolted into a
gully directly ahead of us, into which we had seen
a half-dozen Turks disappear a minute before.
Just as we rounded a bend we came upon a Turk
who had been a bit slower than his mates in getting
away. He was running as hard as he could, and
dragging a camel along behind. As it happened, 1
was the first to grab him, when to my great surprise
he threw his arms round my neck and kissed me.
Yes, well and truly kissed me, and he had got in a
good couple of dozen, too, before I had sufficiently
recovered from the shock to give him the “don’t
argue.” Evidently thinking I was going to shoot him
then and there, he frightened the very dickens out of
the lad standing nearest him by making one terrific
bound straight at him and kissing his hands. After
some minutes’ careful manoeuvring we managed to
surround “Jacko” and got him to understand we had
no intention of killing him jiist then. After that,
having disarmed him, we led both him and the camel
back to the colonel, to whom he was duly presented
in the name of the King.
“Billy Blue” : One of the Sydney morning
history-mixers lately had an article on
the “Oldest House in Australia,” and
awarded the long-service medal to Vaucluse,
the harbor-side home of Wentworth. The
honor really belongs to the house built by
John McArthur. McArthur arrived in Aus-
tralia in the ship Scarborough on June 28,
1790, and on February 12, 1793, he received
a grant of 100 acres near where the Rosehill
racecourse is to-day. His wife, writing on
August 22, 1794, starts a letter thus: —
I write you now from our own house, a very excel- '
lent brick building 68 feet in length and 18 feet
wide.
The old home still stands, and ought to be
resumed and preserved if only for the con-
fusion of quick-lunch historians.
* * * *
Pearce, Minister for Defence, when he
was explaining the other night why the
finger-prints of recruits are now being
taken, said that at least 2000 men had got
away with the expeditionary forces by im-
personating others. The fraud was easy to
work. Smith, who wanted to get through,
but was either over the age or under the
standards, got Jones, who didn’t want to go
to war, to represent him at the medical
examination. Jones received a certificate,
but Smith went into camp. However, only
300 of these 2000 were returned from Egypt
as unfit, and some of the others had scored
very well in the fighting. You can never
judge a cigar by the picture on the box.

MOLLY: “Yes, but, Dolly is smarter than
that. With one little thread of golden
hair she landed a Jewy that weighs
thousands.”
The reflections of “Flossie Fluffytop” :
Love is a game and kisses the pool.
Love is a game. If the maid know each rule,
With a handful of trumps she will lose, pretty fool.
For Love is a game where the man scoops the pool.
TRANSPORTS leaving.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
1 cannot work ! The ftirewell whistles blowing
Make a mad tumult ’neath the list’ning sky !
While I live here some better men are going
Out to far fields of agony to die.
I cannot work! The office seems to stifle
When whistles blow! With hope and courage high
They go to live where living is no trifle,
While I stay here —while I stay here to die!

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Ashmead Bartlett was on the liner which
carried ex-Native Minister Jimmy Carroll
(M.L.) to London, and at a concert aboard
gave a burlesque of “Timi” speaking to the
Maoris on the war. It was a clever if merci-
less burlesque, and made everybody feel
delightfully uncomfortable. Carroll, who
was chairman, on rising to thank the enter-
tainers, provided a little show of his own. It
was a fine parody of Bartlett lecturing on
the Gallipoli campaign. The dropped “g” was
a gladsome feature, and the torture wound
up with the characteristic: “I, myself, per-
sonally saw these things—with my own eyes
—through my field-glasses.” The audience
that night rapped its marrow bones on the
flooring of its several cabins and thanked a
generous Providence that it had been spared
to make the voyage.
WILLIE WATT, M.H.R.
Passed out last week, in Queensland,
Charlton Kable, who was born in Bathurst
in 3831. lie was a grandson of Henry
Kable, who came with Governor Phillip in
the First Fleet. Henry held several offices
under our earliest Governors, and then was
a partner in the firm of Lord, Underwood
and Kable; he had a taste of “fine and gaol”
from Governor Bligh, who gaoled the firm
for writing him an "impertinent letter.” Kable
settled later at Windsor, and brewed beer. His
son William migrated to Bathurst, and kept
one of the earliest inns. Grandson Charl-
ton, having tried the Turon for gold and
failed, struck .out for Moreton Bay, and be-
came a pastoralist.
Died at Bega (N.S.W.), W. J. Lane, who
had served the goddess Cow for over half a
century, and owned a big stretch of South
Coast country adjoining the Lucas Tooth
property at Kameruka. Lane came from the
North of Ireland in the 50’s of last century
with the brothers Robert and Henry Ritchie,
whose sister he married ; and the trio got the
pick of the district. One of his brothers was
a brigadier-general in India, and another
was the “father of the Irish Bar” in Dublin.
When the flood came down at Murchison
(Vic.) Mrs. J. II. Campbell, the wife of a
local farmer, went out alone to save some
sheep. She found the only way she
could get to them was by swimming;
so she swam. The current swept her
down, but she got a rest on a culvert
and then pushed on again. Her hands
were badly cut against barb-wire fences;
but she got the sheep out.
Moles worth Greene, one of the oldest
squatters in Australia, died last week, aged
SJ. lie was son of Captain W. Pomeroy
Greene, R.N., who came to Port Phillip in
the early 40’s in a ship chartered by him-
self, with his family, live stock, servants
(male and female), and all the necessaries for
the establishment of an elaborate home-
stead. He settled at Woodlands, 14 miles
from Melbourne. Molesworth, this pioneer’s
son, married in 1852 Emma Browne, a
daughter of Captain J. Sylvester Browne.
The wife of the late Chief Justice Darley
was also a daughter, and Rolf Boldrewood
IF. A. Browne) was a son. A sister of
Molesworth Greene, still living in London,
is widow of the late Chief Justice Stawell,
of "Victoria; and the late G. H. Greene,
M.L.C., of landra (N.S.W.), was a brother.
In the mid ’4o’s an amateur steeplechase
was run at Woodlands, the competitors be-
ing friends of the Greene family. W. F.
Stawell, afterwards Chief Justice, rode the
winner. Rolf Boldrewood had a mount. So
had Molesworth Greene and a brother —not
the landra man; he was too young. Inci-
dentally, through the Browne influence,
“Moley” Green became interested in mining
and got on to one very good thing—the
Broken Hill South, of which he was chair-
man.
William M. Hughes and his clothes now
turn the scale at 7st. 21b.
Ex-Police Inspector Richard Saunders,
who has just passed out at Adelaide, aged
85, was with Major Warburton’s expedition
that explored the country west of Port
Augusta half a century ago.
The big, close-cropped, muscular Sydney
barrister, Dr. Brissenden, who went away
as a lance-corporal, is a sergeant now. But
he hasn’t been poking Fritz in the liver, as
he hoped to do. Somewhere in Europe they
have had him at a base depot supplying
legal opinions at 10 bob a day, or whatever
corporals get. Which is pretty cheap law
and (considering all things) a pretty bad
joke.
Laurence Grayson, just dead in Adelaide,
was a democrat of the old Kingstonian
school, and no friend of the party system.
When he headed the poll in ISS7 in
Kingston’s own West Adelaide district, he
looked like having a long Parliamentary
innings; but in 1593, after he had had one
taste of office as Public Works Minister in
a Downer Government, split voting pushed
him out, and he gave up the game for good.
In the S.A. Railway workshops he had
helped to get the eight-hours day, but from
1881 he was either manager or director of
a big engineering co., and with later Labor
developments he was out of sympathy. A
solid man always, he was at one time Pre-
sident of the Chamber of Manufactures, on
the Council of the School of Mines from the
beginning, and the senior member of the
Public Library Board. He was a Lanca-
shire man by birth, but a good Australian
of nearly 60 years’ standing—and 78 when
the end came.
The coming of the Torrens Act for sim-
plifying dealings with land has been re-
called in Adelaide by the death of E. J.
Heath, at 86. As registrar of deeds he
knew all about the complicated system of
conveyancing inherited from England till
Torrens cut the knot and made work in the
Lands Titles Office “like living in anew
earth.” It is one of the two big though
simple things that South Aus. has given to
the world, the “cross in the square” voting
system being the other. Other States that
followed suit tend to commemorate the name
of Torrens, but S.A. calls its deeds tersely
“R.P.A. title.”

BRENNAN, M.H.R.
Jim Wal'd ley, president of the Bakers’
Union of Melbourne, who claims that he will
be 101 on October 20, and has a certain
amount of evidence to show for it, “shouted’’
himself a holiday jaunt to Sydney for this
year’s Eight Hours’ celebration.
Passed out at New Farm (Q.), G. C.
Willcocks, aged 59. As contractor, Willcocks
had a hand in most of the big Queensland
jobs of recent years, including the Cloncurry
and Gym pie railways, the Rockhampton
bridge, and the harbor works at Port Alma;
and on the Sydney side he had shifted
almost an equal amount of muck. Outside
his contracting he was best known as a
plucky mining speculator who had dropped
money in most parts of Australia. A son,
now at the war, is medical superintendent of
Sydney Hospital, and another son is on the
land in Queensland.
Adana Hope, one of Melbourne’s oldest
cricketing identities, passed out last week.
He played for Victoria in the first inter-
colonial match in Sydney, and was an
umpire when Ivo Bligh’s English team
played Victoria. His late wife, by the way,
was a relative of the frirl whom Ivo Bligh
married. Old-man Hope, a chemist at
Albert Park, begat a bunch of bright daugh-
ters. The eldest was about the first qualified
woman chemist in Victoria, and a younger
daughter is Rita, the pianist.
Memories of the famous Speight v. Age
lawsuit were revived the other day by news
of the death of Allison Smith in England.
Smith was the man from Maoriland who
managed the locomotive dept, of the Rail-
ways under Speight’s extravagant rbgime.
Those w T ere the roaring Boom times, and the
evidence in the libel case dealt largely with
Allison Smith’s habit of scrapping ' costly
rolling-stock on the least suspicion of old-
age. or infirmity. It was the joke of the
period. A panto, comedian, engaged in
smashing things, would announce that he
was Allison Smith on the job.
Nobody could moralise more interestingly
on Japan’s connection with the present war
than Lord Redesdale, who died lately. He
was Mr. Mitford of the diplomatic service
half a century ago, one of its best brains,
and afterwards author of “Tales of Old
Japan.” He was there when .the Western
Powers forced their acquaintance upon the
Mikado’s kingdom, and was present at
the last official hara-kiri performed in expi-
ation of an attack on foreign subjects. And
that was 45 years ago.
Brigadier-General Chayton, who was re-
cently decorated with the Servian order of
t!ie White Eagle, is another Maorilander to
make a name for himself at warfare. In
1892 he was appointed captain of the Marl-
borough Mounted Rifles: during the differ-
ence of opinion between John Bull and Jan
Boer he was in charge of M.L.’s third con-
tingent. For a tii e he was assistant-
adjutant-general in M.L. and was later
detached for instruction in England.
Lieut. Henare Koliere, lately killed, was
a grandson of that Major Morgan Kohere
who was a hot pepper-box of the Ngati-
porou tribe. Morgan was one of the fight-
ing pals of the distinguished Ropata.
Young Kohere (about 87) was one of the
giants that showed London, on the occasion
of Edward Vll.’s Coronation, what ‘the
Maori was like at his best. Haere ra: liaere
hi to Tcainga!
Phil Fargher, who crossed the mound for
the last time recently, was one of the most
persistent disciples of rifle-shooting in Aus-
tralia. Thirty years ago he joined the
militia at Bendigo, and among the prizes
he won afterwards were the Queen's at
Williamstown (Vic.) in ’O2; Vic. It.A. gold
medal and aggregate in ’O3; and N.S.W.
chanfpionship in ’OO. Fargher represented
Australia twice at Bisley (Eng.), and on
one trip brought the St. George Badge back.
As secretary of V.R.A. in the last, 10 years
of his life lie was a hard-working enthu-
siast who rolled up sleeves and did tilings.
“Homo’s” tale of a tough ’un :—•
Thirty years go, at the Glenrowan (Vic.) level
crossing, an unannounced train made sausage-meat of
a mob of passing Cattle and also of the horse the
ctrover (Michael Mohan) was riding. What was re-
garded as the drover’s mangled corpse was fished out
of the carnage and carried into the pub that had
been re-erected on the site of the Kelly Gang’s last
Stand. There, was no funeral, but the leading sur-
geons of the day readily testified that Mohan would
be'a hopeless cripple for the rest of his life. So he
obtained some £2OOO compensation from the De-
partment. During the recent floods this persistent
Australian (now over 70, and a slight contraction of
one leg the only trace of his accident) whs- crossing
the Ovens at night, after delivering cattle at Ever-
ton, and floundered into about Bft. of swift current.
Wild roo-ees of distress from the murky waters a chain
or so down-stream inspired a local sawmill hand to
swim in with a rope, and the wrecked drover was
detached from a friendly snag and hauled ashore.
The horse was found next morning standing in about
2ft. of water, and Michael patted his neck and
climbed back into the saddle to resume business.
Never say die!
“Edson” : The - most discussed pictures in
the Australian (Melb.) Art Association’s
fourth anuual gathering are the six sam-
ples from the brush (if it isn’t a broom) of
that French-Australian of Caledonian ex-
traction, Max Meldrum. It is the most
courageous stuff seen in Melbourne for
years—courageous because it shouts defi-
ance from the canvas. Meldrum is person-
ally a truculent little man ; he is so pugi
listic a painter that the great wonder is he
is not in the French trenches, swiping Hans
in the eye with a full brush as broad as a
bill-sticker’s. His portraits are put down
with swathes of paint an inch wide. They
are most dexterous, and really are portraits
when you contrive to see the picture in
defiance of the paint. In the one sitter I
know emphasis of personality is secured
with more than a touch of caricature. This
artist does not paint his subject; he hits
him off, and hits him hard. “Smax” Mel-
drum should be the signature.

William Langdon, the Labor Mayor of
Fitzroy (Vic.), who recently eschewed the
rabbit-skins of office and elected to keep
the council in order without the aid of
cocked hat or dog-collar, has declared him-
self in favor of conscription and enlisted —
and so far the local branch of the P.L.C.
hasn’t bumped William out on his neck for
being a free-speaker and a patriot.' Lang-
don describes himself in his enlistment
papers as a “construction engineer,” and he
is leaving a big business behind him. Mrs.
Bill reckons she can run the show until
William gets back.
THE WAIL OF A TRANSPORTING DRIVER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The rations came in late that night, the limbers went
astray,
And out across the darkness I heard a brother pray
Such prayers as bullock-punchers use when mountain
roads are steep,
Or drivers when the S.A. carts are buried axle deep.
The rain was coming down on us in one unending
stream,
And God had put the devil in the newly-broken team ;
Each time a night-bird hooted, each time a shadow
lay
Aeros3 the thing they called a road, the mules would
break away.
“Lift up, lift up, you blighter ! God strafe that Ger-
man flare!”
(What blasted mule could stand the strain of shrapnel
in his hair?)
“Stand up, you blanky nigger, that cloud is only
smoke!”
“Whoa, boy! Look up ! Oh, hell and earth! The
blanky trace is broke.”
My stomach’s grinding out a plaint for food to put
inside;
I'm frozen to the very bones, and wetted to the hide ;
Sy mind has lost its bearings ; but one thing’s stick-
ing through—•
v transport driver wants to swap his blanky job
with you.

COLUMN
The Savings Bank of South Australia,
which isn’t the State Bank of S. Aus.,
though it ought to be part of it:—

*On amounts under £350.
If you looked at the figures of this bank
and knew nothing of what had been going
on in the world, you would judge that this
little bit of it at any rate had been enjoying
great prosperity. Not only were the profits
larger, but deposits were larger—everything,
in fact, was larger except withdrawals.
Deposits, which shrank a little in 1915
owing mainly to the awful season S. Aus-
tralia had, have bounded along again :
1907 ..£5,304,704 |> 1912 ..£8,223,260
1908 . . 5,803,301 | 1913 . . 8,608,478
1909 . . 6,328,870 ! 1914 . . 9,018,837
1910 .. 0,771,150 i 1915 .. 9,017,061
1911 .. 7,411,710 I 1916 .. 9,265,705
It only means, of course, that people are
hanking their own lOU's. We have bor-
rowed millions during the last couple of
years, and we have put the proceeds of the
borrowings into the bank. That’s all this
splendid increase means; and it will be
madness for people to he misled by it. What
will happen by and bye is not so clear. To
simplify the position think of a small island
with a hundred people on it. One village
starts fighting with another. Each wants
food and ammunition for the fighting men.
Instead of making a levy of food and ammu-
nition, which would be the direct way, the
village issues lOU’s, gets money in return,
and pays with the money. The fighting
over, the lOU’s have to be met. The ques-
tion is, Is that going to cause distress? The
village has given the lOU’s to its own more
affluent inhabitants. Now it has to get
money from its own inhabitants to pay its
own inhabitants. It looks very much as
though the easiest way would be to say,
“Now you owe yourselves £150.000,000.
Consider yourselves paid.” But with us it
won’t be so easily done as that, and the
future will consist of a struggle to make
the other fellow pay. In the past, as Mill
pointed out, the laborer paid for war —paid
with starvation. Probably it won’t be like
that this time. The point to grasp is that
somebody has to do the paying.
Like all savings banks this one has no
capital of its own apart from its reserves,
and if they were larger they would be no
worse. They total under £BOO,OOO, and
they have to safeguard the following invest-
ments :
Mortgages.. _ ..£2,037,901 21.1 p.c.
Public securities.. 5,839,740 60.5 p.c.
Premises .. .. 79,545 .8 p.c.
Cash, deposits, etc. 1,698,705 17.6 p.c.
Still, so long as the public securities haven’t
to be realised, the reserves are probably
enough. And in any ease the depositors
needn’t worry, because behind the hank
stands the State of S. Australia, which
would have to go broke before this hank
went broke. This identity of State and
hank is thoroughly well understood : yet
even that wouldn’t altogether explain the
remarkable fact that 59 of every 100 people
in S. Aus. are Savings Bank depositors, and
if you include the kindergarten branches—
the penny school-banks —04 of every IcO.
The proportion in N. S. Wales is now about
41 ; in Queensland and Tasmania much less.

Though there has been a considerable
shrinkage in the value of securities and the
management has thought it well to provide
accordingly, this great co. has had another
clinking year. It does a big marine-insur-
ance business; yet actually the loss rate is
distinctly below the average. This paper
doesn’t mean to say that losses have been
lighter than usual. They haven’t been. But
on the risky stuff the premiums have evi-
dently been raised quite as much as the
circumstances called for. with the result
that the loss rate in proportion to premiums
shows up remarkably well during the last
two years. Here are the figures:—
Ex- Loss Expense
Premiums. Losses. penses. Rate. Rate.
1895.. £209,239 £136,160 £50,229 65.0 p.c. 24.0 p c.
1900.. 215,730 130,123 57,925 60.3,, 26.9 „
1905.. 297,822 180,009 82,366 60.4 „ 27.7 ~
1910.. 336,542 197,320 94,824 68.6 „ 28.1 ,
1915.. 553,297 287,117 159,063 51.8 ~ 28.7 ,
1916.. 558,799 296,038 171,537 52.9 „ 30.7 „
The expense rate has moved along pretty
briskly. That, no doubt, is due partly to
the high cost of living and partly to the fact
that this, like other cos., is paying part-
salary to men at the Front. But even with
the higher cost rate the underwriting profit
is. a very satisfactory IG£ p.c. of the pre-
miums. And as the interest earnings are
within a few hundred pounds of the total
required to pay the dividend, it follows that
practically the whole of the underwriting
profit goes to improve the security of the
policy-holder.
That some of this surplus profit is used
just now to write down securities isn’t of
much practical consequence. It really only
matters in case the co. has to sell securities.
If it doesn’t have to sell—if it holds on till
they mature—it doesn’t matter what the
market value is.' The interest earnings are
not reduced just because the market value
has fallen. In fact, this co., though it
shows £21,000-odd written off investments
this year, may actually be benefited by the
circumstances which lead to that writing
off. For, as said just now, the writing off
may be purely nominal, and the depreciation
provided for may not make the co. a penny
the poorer; yet it is one of the consequences
of a rise in the value of money, and that
rise will increase the earnings of nil the
new investments. So the old earnings won’t
be a penny the less and the 40 or 50 thou-
sand of profit which the co. can put by every
year earns at least £5OO a year more than
if would have done three years ngo. And
of course it’s the same with other cos. with
new money to invest.
While things are thus very good from the
shareholders’ point of view, the policy-
holders have good reason to feel contented.
This is what they have to fall back upon
(the percentage figures show the proportion
of each class of holding to the total of
assets) ;
Premises £215,083 18.5 p.c.
Mortgages . . . . .. 272,440 23.3 ~
Deposits and cash balances .. 257.109 22.0
Stocks and debentures . . 48,796 4.2 ~
Public securities .. .... 357,951 30.6 ..
Sundries * 16,955 1.4 ~
Funds, after providing for liabilities, total
£981,029. Add the probable premium in-
come for the coming year in excess of
expenses—say £370,000 —and it will be seen
that the co. could during the next 12 months
pay losses in excess of the total losses of
the last live years. Behind that again there
is £700.000 of capital for which the share-
holders are liable.

Ir. is no use guessing at some of the figures
in the last line. All that can be said is
that the directors appropriate enough profit
to carry the formal reserve fund to £145,0*.'0 :
so the total reserves will exceed that figure.
This is equal to nearly 14s. per ordinary
share; hence shareholders in that class do
very well now in being permitted to increase
their holdings at 10s. premium. Pref. shares
(0 p.c.) are available at par.
The directors are making steady efforts
to increase the share capital; ard it seems
highly probable that a very fair proportion
of the £307,802 on current account could
well be permanently invested. If the share-
holder is prepared to let the co. have the
use of the money he might just as well put
it in the form in which the co. can make
the best use of it; and that isn’t on current
account. The total of this debt has been
increasing pretty fast; and it is not the
best feature of the accounts, by any means.
True, the money is deposited by share-
holders only ; but that isn’t much guarantee
that it won’t be disturbed just when dis-
turbance would do the most harm, because
most of the shareholders are not registered
for more than three shares, and that doesn’t
give them much of a stake. It is quite pro-
bable that many of these shareholders think
they are doing just as well for the co. by
taking a deposit receipt for their money as
by taking a share certificate. They need to
be ■ disabused of that. In this paper’s
opinion, at any rate, the big liability for
current account money is, so far from being
a help, a very distinct element of weakness:
whereas the addition of share capital would
be an element of strength.
The co. has made splendid progress; and
some day The Bulletin is going to fit out
an expedition to swoop down on Maoriland
and capture for Australian use the general
manager and half a dozen of the other men
who have established co-operation so firmly
among the farmers of Maoriland. These
figures disclose some of the recent history
of the concern :

As capital has become available new depart-
ments have been opened. The co. manu-
factures chemical fertilisers, and there is
a tremendous amount yet to be done, in that
way. A lot of it could best be done no
doubt by the new federation of co-op. cos.
in which this co. is taking a hand. The co.
also manufactures binder twine and handles
practically all agricultural requirements as
well as all agricultural and pastoral pro-
ducts. The multiplication of departments
has swollen the turnover out of all recogni-
tion. For the 12 months to the end of July,
1906, the volume of business measured
£850,759. In the past year it was
£3,591,321. As business has grown over-
head charges have decreased, and there has
been a steady improvement in the return to
the co-operator. The bonus on purchases
has moved from 4 to 5 p.c.; and 30 p.c.
instead of 25 p.e. of selling commissions
(except on specific lines) is rebated. Mean-
time, certainly, the dividend on ordinary
shares has been reduced from 10 to 8i p.c.
But something better has happened—the
reserves have grown from £31,849 to over
£145,000. They have been more than doubled
in seven years. And that is a performance
far better deserving of three cheers than
the mere maintenance of a 10 p.c. dividend
would be.

The share capital in the business is £10,000;
and that carries G 4 p.c., with a bigger bite
now and Then when the actuary reports
that there is a bit of a surplus to cut up.
It is rather a misfortune that, time after
time, the actuarial report for the previous
three years isn't quite ready for issue with
the annual report.. The things should be
sent out together, especially as nowadays
the co. is doing a little ordinary life insur-
ance. And if that is impossible—though it
isn’t—then the general result of the exami-
nation should be set out in the next follow-
ing annual report. For the present this
paper has no reason to doubt that policy
liabilities are well covered, though it is very-
questionable whether it is prudent to have
as much as 72 p.c. of the funds (capital and
insurance fund combined) represented by-
mortgages.

Taking the increase in book debts to balance
the decrease in cash, then over £30,000 has
been added to stock, another £lB,OOO to the
investment in the McCulloch Carrying Co.,
the. profitable Victorian concern which
Wright Heatons own entirely, and £20.000
has been put into the War Loan. All this
has been done out of undivided profit with
the exception of £14,000 got for a new issue
of shares, Possibly not quite all has been done
out of profits made since the beginning of
1909, because there was a revaluation of
assets in 1912 and £21.000 of internal re-
serves. were. then disclosed. On the other
hand, it is highly probable that on a revalua-
tion now there would be a considerable addi-
tion to the present figures. Moreover, given
satisfactory management, there is every
reason to expect that this co. will continue
to do well, because it is pretty certain that,
even if Australia suffers a slump after the
war, the cultivation of wool and wheat will
not tail off. We shall certainly be in the
soup if it does.

had a freehold valued at £151,082 and fit-
tings, etc., a trifle under £BO,OOO. A few

The timber trade has had an amazingly
good time during the past few years; and
because it is likely to sag pretty badly
before long it is advisable to examine timber
accounts with that probability in mind. In
this ease the co. has a capital of £75,000,
and that answers all requirements fairly
well. The total liabilities are £22,838, in-
cluding all trade accounts, and the assets
include £14,965 of book debts and liquid
assets, £32,752 of stock, and £61,150 of
plant, mills, vessels and interests in similar
things. The financial position is therefore
easy, and the co. wouldn’t apparently be
embarrassed by a slump in trade, as a co.
which had heavily stocked on borrowed
money might easily be. Still, there’s
precious little margin left now after paying
the 8 p.c.; and, though there will always
be a demand for hardwood, and though on
that account this co. may expect to do
better than most, it can’t expect affluence in
the years when we shall be paying-up. As
for past profits they don’t seem to have been
exaggerated; at any rate, where one can
trace it, depreciation appears to have been
well provided for.
* * * ■*
The appointment of a Government actuary
to assist the friendly societies of N. S. Wales
has straightened up those bodies wonder-
fully ; and most of them are now on a sound
basis. The results of the investigation of
one of the oldest of them, the Oddfellows,
Manchester Unity, to the end of 1914, have
just been reported to headquarters. This
society has in the State £622,804 of funds,
and that is reckoned by the actuary to be
£162,714 in excess of requirements —assum-
ing, of course, that present contributions and
benefits are maintained.
* % * * *
Great Cobar (N.S.W.) is being visited
by mining-engineer Pellew Harvey, a Lon-
don director. Some weighty debenture-
holders are getting rather anxious, so
Harvey was asked to try to find out what
is the matter with the property and why
the estimated quantity of ore is not being
treated. J. R. Godfrey, of the Mines
Department, had a good look round a couple
of months back, and perhaps the, Govern-
ment is also getting a little nervous about
the country’s hard-borrowed £40,000 put
into the concern to enable it to resume
operations 12 months ago. Meanwhile it
is carrying quite sufficient embroidery for a
co. 10 times as great and 100 times more
prosperous.
Bourke’s Hill (N.S.W.) got out £3216
worth of tin last half-year; but that was
£l9 short of exes., without providing any-
thing for wear and tear. The two plants
were shifted to new positions during the
term, and that and shortage ot watei helped
to bump up the costs to 9.9 d. per yard toi
No. 1 and 11.7 d. for No. 2. Both plants are
now on Preston’s ground, with good pros-
pects ; and as the financial position is easy
they won’t have to go far before share-
holders get a bite again.

Golden Butterfly has signalised its resur-
rpction by acquiring a nine months’ working
O ption over a group of leases adjoining the
New Boddington at Goongarrie (W.A.).
One of the blocks, known as the Roaring
Gimlet, was sold in early days for £25,000,
but after being worked to a depth of 130 ft.
it wag abandoned. The New Boddington,
after being tested by a co., was also aban-
doned; but eventually it was acquired by
two prospectors who found a new reet and
took out several good crushings and then
so ld o ut to a local syndicate, which has since
fi o;lted a co . i n Kalgoorlie. Several other
blocks in the viainity are also being nibbled
at by option hunters. The neighboring
Broad Arrow field has, after a long period
0 f dreary stagnation, started to buck up,
an d several leases, which were abandoned
after yielding big quantities of gold, are
again being successfully worked,

Shortage of water is interfering with
milling operations on Amalgamated West-
ralia’s Burtville show, and the manager
urges the deepening of the shaft to 2Ooft.
to see if an ample supply can be got there,
A parcel of 25 tons recently cleaned up
yielded between 10 and 12 dwt. per ton;
but a lot depends upon whether it was a
fair average sample. The report now being
made on the show by G. Beresford, late of
the Golden Butterfly, may throw some light
on the subject. Yilgarn Consols claims to
have just obtained a 2oz. average from a
3 00-ton crushing from its Burtville holding,
and according to the terms of the option
this return goes towards the purchase of
the property. A fair amount of ore is
stated to be showing above water level, and
the stone is, according to official reports,
going strongly underfoot. But it’s a dis-
trict habit for a reef to show strongly under-
fOot o ne day and vanish the next,

(W.A.) obtained £194, <95 from '
tons, compared with £249,878 from 125,990
tons in the previous year. The decrease in
tonnage is attributed to increased moisture
in the ore and the lesser revenue to poorer
grade ore. Mining costs at 20s. 9d. were up
a little, the rise being due to increased costs
of supplies. Reduced tonnage also, had an
adverse effect, and until normal times re-
turn there is little hope of improvement at
this or any of the other big \\ estralian
mines. In his report to the Board, general-
manager R. S. Black states that though
nothing of very material value was un-
earthed during the year moderate addi-
tions” were made to the ore reserves, lhe
average grade of the ore raised was oos. Jd.,
or 7s. 3d. less than for the previous term,
and according to the g.-m. it can only be a
matter of months when a further decline
in value will he shown. Only a limited
known quantity of rich ore is left'in the
mine, and on its exhaustion the co. will
have to work on a grade which, treated at
full mill capacity, will show only a bare
margin over present expenses.
.)umbuuua, Gippsland (Vic.), netted only
£43 from 18.1 SO tons coal mined last half-
year; and the chairman threatens to shut
the mine down if the Arbitration Court
shortens hours and puts wages up. Like all
other Outtrim mines Jumbunna has good
coal, but it’s not easy to get. Up and
down throw-faults and narrow seams in-
volve so much dead work that prices have to
go high for the co. to earn a decent profit.
Then, too, it has a fire smouldering away in
the north side-tunnel. Coal sales totalled
£17,598, but wages, etc., absorbed £16,017
and railway freight £l4lB. These figures
only relate to working charges, and as the
co. makes its little bit out of trading account
it is there that the profit of £43 is shown.
Liquid assets amount to £7500, including
stocks of coal. Against that there are £3224
of liabilities. It might pay Jumbunna to
forsake narrow seams and try its luck at
briquetting work on the brown coal.

Dunno what’s coming next, but “Edward
Dyason Proprietary, Ltd.,” has bought out
“the interests of the management of the
[Bendigo] cos. now controlled by the follow-
ing legal managers: North and Stanfield,
W. B. Walker, ,T. 11. Craig. L. B. Birch and
W. Weddell.” -The new co. will put all the
mining cos. in these stables into one office, to
be managed in Bendigo by J. G. Stanfield
and in Eaglehawk by W. P». Walker.
* # * *
Clonc-urry is making solid progress and
railway deviations are being built to the
Great Australia and some other mines. The
extension of the line to the Dobbin has
brought a new road to Oxide into use. Oxide
is developing a new shoot of ore on the
hanging-wall side of the big formation. A
crosscut was driven east from the main
adit, and the new make of ore is running
north and south and parallel to the main
lode. Option hunters had a heap of luck in
obtaining the Lady Margaret prize. The old
workings were only 6ft. from an ore body
20ft. wide containing splendid values.
* * * *
Kulamadau (Papua) was accused the
other day of sudden silence after producing
£6789 in six weeks. But its modesty is
quite natural, because this good run was a
special effort to pay off debts. The latest
return was £2363 (the value of retorted
gold concentrates and cyanide bullion) for
six weeks. During that time 968 tons of
stone were crushed and 1450 tons of tailings
cyanided. No estimate of profit is attempted.
The next wireless message should have some-
thing to say about No. 7 level, for the rich
lens of stone is to be followed deeper.
Things are in trim for the opening up of
the No. 4 shaft level at the North Farrell,
Tullah (Tas.), so it won’t be long before
the sampling of No. 3 lode will begin. See-
ing that stopes from No. 2 level are nearly
beaten through to No. 1. a big drain is on
No. 3 to supply the mill. Stopes on No. 2
lode run about sft., and those on No. 3
about 12ft. The “firsts” —what there are of
them—run to 70oz. silver and 60 p.c. lead,
and the concentrates from the poorer stuff go
over 40oz. silver and 50 p.c. lead per ton, so
the co. has a chance to make a bit while the
fat war prices rule for metals. Beside the
big Broken Hill formations the North
Farrell is a toy. Still, its No. 3 shaft level
on No. 3 lode carries ore over a width of
18ft. This should mean a good chance of
getting something decent at the next lift.
But on the West Coast nothing can be taken
for granted until the pick converts “pro-
bable” ore into actual reserves.
* * * *
“D.F.”: The views expressed by “Coot”
(B. 5/10/T6) are fairly common in Tas-
mania, more’s the pity for Tasmania. The
idea is that nobody should be given the
right to do anything lest he should make
money out of it. When a co. like the Amal-
gamated Zinc comes along and makes a deal
for a big block of water-power at a price
that ought to give the State a good profit,
these people get the shivers, howl about
monopoly and say that anyone who wants
power in future will have to go cap in hand
to the big co. to get it. “Coot” says, by the
way, that the diversion of the Upper Ouse
into the Great Lake is a big question, but
engineers don’t seem to think it’s very big.
Anyway, why howl if the Amalgamated
Zinc does eventually take a big proportion
of the power from the Great Lake? There
are plenty of other sources to sup-
ply future “enterprising investors” without
adopting a dog-in-the-manger policy towards
the people who are prepared to sign up the
business right away. There’s Lake St.
Clair, the Arthur Lake scheme, the falls on
the Franklin and on the West Coast and
elsewhere waiting to be yoked up. Certainly
it will cost money to do it, but the Speck’s
bound to be borrowing the money, anyway,
“to keep the wheels of industry going,” and
it had better spend it fin going nap on water
power than fritter it aw’ay in expensive
and nearly useless harbor works and rail-
way extensions and odds and ends as it is
in the habit of doing.
“Jans Tas”: Mention of the Jupiter
(Tas.) recalls its discovery in ’95 by two
prospectors—W. T. Hodge, of Zeelian. and
the invalid who is now writing this par.
The outcrop was pyrites, and we suspected
it to be the continuation of the Hercules
lode, then recently discovered. Though we
were millionaire's in enthusiasm, we were a
bit short of cash, and it required £23 10s.
to take up an 80-acre section. We con-
sidered the position; it was a lonely spot,
the scrub was dense horizontal, and bush-
men will know what that means. Few men
were roaming the bush, so we thought we
could risk it while we earned enough to
take up the section. About four months
later, after driving a hard tunnel on con-
tract, making 7s. a day, we came with high
hopes to peg the ground. We pushed
through that awful scrub, sat down on the
spot where we had broken the pyrites
months before, and were considering how
we’d peg the section, when the eye of my
mate caught sight of a fresh tomahawk-
mark. We leapt to our feet to investigate.
The blazed line led ns to a notice posted
on a tree, setting out that two sections had
been taken up for a Launceston syndicate
two days previously. It was hard luck, but
prospectors meet a lot of that.
"Bad Australian” from S. America: —
Many years ago a party of Australians arrived
in this country and unloaded a lovely wild cat on
to Buenos Aires. It was the notorious San Juan
de Oro, and the fall thereof caused so much bitter-
ness of spirit in high places that for years after
the great awakening every Australian who arrived
was asked if he had a rich uncle in Fiji. But that
is all past and forgotten, and Argentina, after a wild
financial jamboree of three or four years’ duration, is
starting in to develop her “great natural resources,”
and as she is a born gambler she naturally turns
to mining. A number of abandoned copper and
galena shows have been re-opened, and wolframite
and sheelite deposits are being' worked, chiefly in the
Province of San Luis. The boomsters are thrusting
their heads up modestly at first, but 1 feel assured
that the Gold Brick Renaissance period has arrived.
