LETTER
Sydney, October 16, 1916.
My Dear Arini, —
The Fairguson is on the trail of Duty, and
.has a rousing list of engagements. On
Saturday he addressed a Manly crowd and
unveiled a tall column to commemorate the
Village’s share in war’s death-roll. Mark
Mitchell handed over the long pillar—a
roll of honor in perpetuity. It is his monu-
ment to his boy, a lieutenant, whose name is
in the list. Next day, being' the Sabbath,
his Ex. and his tall lady went to St. Ste-
phen’s kirk to hear big John Ferguson
expound the Word.
Lady Helen started on her cycle of opening
this, that and the other with a ceremony in
the back woods of Waterloo. Some months
ago her Ex. lifted the latch of the soldiers’
wives and mothers’ centre in the same spot.
Now it’s a going concern, and there were
new club rooms to he officially talked to. The
rain had cleared. So Aide Haskett Smith
hadn’t to do the Walter Raleigh act with his
best frock-coat on a muddy path. In the
early spring of this week Lady Helen takes
charge of a patriotic party in the North Syd-
ney School of Arts. It is the local chemists'
ladies committee, and there’s no homoeo-
pathic measure about their work.
The end of the A.J.C. races began under
weepy skies. Gloomy weather prophets un-
furled their gamps and set forth prepared
for a web-footed day. The butterfly bri-
gade, tucked away in taxis, sped out to
Rand wick in summer muslins. The optimists
had their reward. A brilliant afternoon gave
them a blue and gold background. But the
gee-gees had to mind their feet, and the
steeplechase was a roly-poly game.
Backers of Leslie Stuart’s Louvre on a
previous day had the joy of again collecting
their winnings—at a shorter price—when
the game little nag hurried past the post in
the scamper over the big timber. And
Quinologist brought joy to some of the khaki
boys from Randwic-k as the result of a
friendly 'tip. So the Spring meeting gave
the poor old public a few shillings here and
there.
In the inner shrine of the official stand
• best frocks were dotted about amongst the
sac suits of the sporting male. A few years
ago women were barely tolerated, certainly
never encouraged, in this eyrie. Now voii
go there to find the latest thing in tulle.
Mrs. Fred McMaster, in a biscuit-colored
frock, was one of the good-looking young
country matrons who had her own squatter
to consult about the satin-skinned beauties
on the course. Dr. Pat Ivenna, a keen
sport, had the luck to fancy Louvre, and
Mrs. Pat did ditto under the brimless croWn
of the latest spring millinery. Theo Marks
followed the races with the look of concen-
tration you’ll mostly find in the Man Who
Knows Something. The tall A. E. Hughes
—a Darling Point squire—escorted his Best
Girl, whose white cloth outfit was the ulti-
mate word in the fashionable flare. Per-
haps, in a Better Planet, some of these good
things will be distributed amongst the de-
serving poor. The Hugheses, by the way,
would have been on their voyage to Colombo
if both their babies hadn’t suddenly held
tilings up with the loud, determined bark of
whooping-cough. Little Mrs. Ken Richards,
m dove-grey plumage, noted the scratchings
under a floppy white hat.
Black, striped like the zebra, or merely
cross-barred in inky lines, was wedged in
between the eternal taffeta puffs, in bronze
or dull indigo blue. Mrs. Jackson, who used
to be Leonie Dodds, was in a black frock of
literally, outstanding merit—a feat only
achieved by unseen terraces of silk frills.
Harking back to blue, a dark-eyed damsel,
.Miss Woolf, was in .the first flight in a
shining navy frock with an audible rustle
But the fourth race-day found a serious con-
tingent of winter tailor-built women—the
minor weather prophets who had prepared
for a second deluge.
Decorative feet were in evidence on such
race days as did not call for goloshes. The
new shoes are as fanciful as were the heavy
winter boots. They are laid out in designs
either check or mottled like the lizard’s
Sunday frock. Or they are white with
black points. At prices en suite, as they
say in the upholstery department, they are
not for everybody. And how tired' you
would get of your only walking-shoes if
they had to be cleaned with two or three
different brands of “mottle” to suit their
iguana complexion!
Government House loses another A.D.C.
on October 25. The nice, upstanding New
I Soy, young Boulton, of tlie Cameron High-
landers, leaves for England via the States
on that day. He is barely convalescent
after his knocking about in France. Still,
lie packs up his kilts and goes looking for
more trouble.
United Charities Day had to struggle
against the stream. There’ll be a dead-end
feeling about sports meetings—even for
charity—till Australia votes “Yes” to rein-
force our weary brothers in the trenches.
It is not generally known to the doubting
Thomasinas perhaps that at the present
moment a big division of artillery is with-
out reinforcements. “To my knowledge,”
wrote an Australian about his elder brother
lately—“to my knowledge he hasn’t had his
clothes off for a fortnight.”
i In the city something like £BOOO fell into
the United Charities’ collection-boxes. In
gorgeous sunshine the Show Ground carni-
val failed to attract more than a moderate
attendance. Secretary Somer, of the Agri-
cultural Society, rode about the show ring
as lion, overseer, with a keen eye for the
bogus collector who slips into every charity
sports meeting. Miss Kemp, after super-
intending the buck jumping in a small,
fenced-in compound, won the ladies’ high-
jump competition. Mrs. Staee was a close
rival. The “bucks,” by the way, had a
rope round their bodies. When the
equestrienne gave her end of the hemp a
pull they arched their backs like infuriated
cats. One youngster, a mite of a girl, stuck
on through an enthusiastic bucking perform-
ance by pony Tipperary. An older boy
then tried his luck. But his luck was to
hit the earth like an india-rubber ball, so he
picked up his hat and retired while he still
had a whole skin. As usual, State-school
kiddies contributed to the show.
Lieut. T. Amphiett, who left with the
35th Battalion, was recently married on
the other side. The bride was Ella Ryrie,
of the N. S. Wales squatting family.
Evie Yates, of this hamlet, has said “Yes”
to Arthur Patrick. He belongs to a family
which has contributed four sons to the great
fight. The newly-engaged Arthur himself
came back from Egypt the other week. Eye
trouble was the nuisance. But it’s getting
better, thank you.
The versatile A. B. Piddington, K.C., is
president of the Spanish-Australian Associa-
tion. He look the chair on Monday evening
while Consul Macedo (for Peru) discoursed.
The war has whipped up local interest in
foreign talk. Sydney is now getting its
tongue round all the leading languages that
fell out of the Tower of Babel.
Here is the Mother of the 2nd Divi-
sional Ammunition Column Comforts com-
mittee—Mrs. Bal-
lantine. Nominally
she is the hon.
secretary. In fact,
she is a tireless
commanding officer
and general utility.
Mrs. Ballantine,
whose son is with
the 2nd D.A.C.,
went to Melbourne
in November, 1915,
to get the glad con-
sent of the O.C. to
forthwith start the
Sydney committee.
Since then there
has been an un-
broken record of
harmonious work
by a band of un-
selfish women in the Faimouth Chambers
depot.
The personnel of this column was so
changed in Egypt that Bill and Jim made
way for Tom and Harry. “Don’t mind the
changes, girls!” she said. “We’re working
for Australian soldiers —God bless ’em !” and
the mother had her way. This coming
Christmas 102 cases of comforts (carefully
examined in detail, for, you’d scarcely believe
it, the lady enemy in our midst is up to queer-
tricks) will reach the D.A.C. boys in the
trenches. With shrewd foresight, Mrs. Bal-
lantine saw that the changes in Australian
artillery units should be met by the amalga-
mation of comforts committees. After Trojan
efforts she succeeded in persuading some
others to fall in line. She formed the
nucleus of the “O” Siege (now 36th Heavy
Australian Artillery) Comforts Fund, and
joyously welcomed the 22nd Howitzer and
the Ist and 7th Artillery amalgamation. Even
a lone camel corps, ’way out amidst Egyptian
sandstorms, is looking to this big-hearted
woman to see about its socks. Now, with
Heaven knows how many boys in khaki to
help, she lias a rapidly-emptying cashbox to
fill. You know what that means.
Big Bob Maddrell, of Braidwood, gave
his arm to Gracie Mates, a Melbourne girl,
one day last week. The occasion was
trimmed with flowers in St. John’s, Darling-
hurst. Brother Edgar_ Maddrell was second-
best man. The home-coming will be in a
handsome nest in Goulburn. Houses, it
appears, are hard to get in Braidwood a
where the inhabitants stick like lichen to
their humpies. Hence new husband Bob
•means to motor 50 miles or so every week to
have a look at his flocks and herds in the
Maddrell family’s paddocks. The wedding-
cake was passed round at the Cairo.
Did I tell you that pretty little Madge
Hardy—father sells diamonds in Hunter-
street —is engaged to Captain Jack Playfair?
The French Bank in Sydney has had
almost a rush of people wanting to change
French notes. Sending little presents home,
these dear fellows, said an official. “No,
no,” said wounded Billjim who had been
changing one lot of money. “The chaps are
sending over to have a bit in Tatt.’s for the
Cup.”
The arch smile
that greets you in
this little picture
belongs to Rosa
Alba, a young Aus-
tralian lyric
soprano. The win-
ner of one of the
principal scholar-
ships in our Con-
servatorium, she’s
going to work hard
there for three
years. Then, with
luck, Europe and
a final polish. Ver-
brugghen is so in-
terested in the
voice—sweet and
pure in tone as the
silvery notes of our
bush bellbird —that he won’t in this case
countenance the usual student’s public ap-
pearance. Her one warble for a long time
to come happened in the Conservatorium
the other evening. John Brownlow arranged
this “one night only” song-burst. There
wasn’t a bean for him in the business. And
the nightingale won’t get many new feathers
out of it either, for the week bulged
with conceits. Rosa’s voice has tlie limpid
beauty of Melba’s, with the freshness of a
boy-chorister’s. She’s an advanced student,
as her facile trills in Handel’s “Ye Cupids
and Graces” proved. Admirably taught
before joining the Conservatorium (I fancy
Maggie Stirling had a hand in the prelimi-
nary breaking-in), Roland Foster has the luck
to be her present teacher. Luck, because
Rosa Alba sings like a canary, being made
that way.
The concert for the Italian Red Cross
brought out the usual square of blue Axmin-
ster for the feet of distinguished and undis-
tinguished visitors. The attendant damsels,
organised by Miss Ferrari, met the audience
at the Town Hall gates. Turned out in the
national costume of our Ally they mostly
wore geranium-red skirts, lattice-work chemi-
settes and Neapolitan headgear. Signors
and signorinas contributed coal-black hair
and eyes like midnight. But the bulk of the
concert crowd consisted of the good Aus-
tralian citizen who is dragged out o’ nights
by his citizeness to every patriotic show no
matter what the cause. Consul Eles hadn’t
gone back to Melbourne, after all. So he
took one of the best chairs, with Consul
Chayet at his elbow. Dr. Marano, Italy’s
local consul, was also in the forefront.
Tenor Balboni, of the Gonsalez co., sang
with difficulty through a heavy cold rather
than send his regrets for absence. Fillipini,
in good voice, and little soprano Visoni, in
yellow silk flounced with black lace, con-
tributed. The male songbirds were handed
boomerangs, suitably inscribed, and a bottle
apiece of old Italian wine. John Lemmone
starred in a flute solo, Albert Goossens sang
his best, and Malcolm McEachern assisted
and arranged it all.
Hon. treas. Gagliardi has about £5OO in
hand as the result of the concert.
There was a rumor that Dr. Cecil Purser
was going officially to ’Murlca to have a
look at Sam’s methods of fighting the tuber-
cular enemy. But it’s not likely to be till
after the other enemy has been whacked.
Mrs. Kernot and a pretty young daughter
go back to Wellington (M.L.) this week.
They’ve been over for six months or so. The
Maoriland lady describes herself as merely
“an intermittent writer,” but she turns out
rhythmic verse when the Muse bites,
A 'full house heard Vera Cree’s good-bye in
the Town Hall. The girl’s rich contralto,
notably in the setting' of “Abide with Me,”
which every famous Australian contralto has
sung in Sydney Town Hall, filled the big-
building. She won the only scholarship
awarded this year in Australia by the Royal
Academy, as you know. With her teacher,
Miss Ada Gee, pretty, confident, well-set-up
Vera leaves for England early in 1917.
A Schools’. Exhibition to demonstrate the
arts and crafts, the plain and fancy cooking,
etc., was opened during this week by Peter
Board. It’s in the loft of the Education
Palace, under the glass roof. In stifling
heat a dense crowd, with swarms of young-
children, mopped its perspiring face while
the long Director of Education made his com-
ments. Chief-Inspector Dawson followed
with choice flowers of Scottish oratory.
Infants in arms, a large contribution from
the mothers present, gurgled their protests at
the suffocating entertainment, and the girls
and boys who “demonstrated” must have
had a stifling time.
The ever-youthful-looking Mrs. Archie
Blackton, .known on concert programmes as
Geraldine Rivers, deserves a mention in this
letter. Husband and eldest son both wear
major’s crowns somewhere along the lengthy
Front. No. 2 son is a lieutenant on the
instructional staff, while a third is getting
points on gunnery out at the Warren, the
ex-Carmelite abbey on Tempe heights.
Electric trams will soon be shattering the
mellow peace of Spring and Bent streets.
There are few places in Sydney where you
can get away from the whine and roar of
the cars. The area sliced by Spring, Bent
and Gresham streets was one of them, and
it was possible for the dazed wayfarer to go
there and collect his thoughts while breath-
ing the chloroform that hangs around Gov-
ernment offices. But it was too good to
last. They’re putting the quiet by-ways in
irons now, and Progress will be roaring
along in a week or two. Then when you
hop into a tram at the Railway to go to the
Quay you may get a nasty surprise to find
that you’re being whisked back into Pitt
street by this new loop.
Paul Du-fault says good-bye to Sydney
within the coming week. His adieu begins
on Saturday evening in the Town Hall.
The 17th Battalion ladies had an after-
noon-out in the Tom Raines’ beautiful
garden. Guyong, trimmed with terraced
lawns and having a real stage-setting of
tall tree-fern, is the ideal background for a
hungry comforts-fund party. While the band
played, young things in diaphanous muslins
sold and raffled anything from a lace hanky to
a red Rhodes Island rooster, which crowed
vociferously to show there was no deception.
With Mrs. Raine and a daughter were stall-
holders innumerable. Mrs. Oscar Back-
house and Mrs. Norman Pope were moving
spirits. Mrs. Maxwell Allan floated round
with a raffle-book to get the rooster off her
hands. Mrs. Richard Binnie brought her
youngsters along to splash their pocket-
money. E. Gard Trouton (the Man •of
Mystery) searched every hole and corner for
a southerly gale. But the Thing wouldn’t
budge out of its secret hiding-place, so the
Messages to Mars (per kite-wireless) had
to be postponed. May Macleod lent her aid
in one of the trade centres; and Spring
materialised in Mrs. Tom Watson’s lilac
gown.
Tram-travellers from Double Bay on
Saturday afternoon were inundated with
gaiters and bishops’ hats at the Darling
Point junction. The visiting graces of the
Synod and their friends and relatives had
been drinking tea and ginger-beer with the
Archbish. _ Wrights at gabled Bishopcourt.
Melbourne’s Lowther Clarke, Brisbane’s fav-
orite Donaldson, and a battery of canons
(major and minor), with deans (rural and
city) and every archdeacon in town, came
hurrying along the dusty Point road. When
the tram service couldn’t stand the rush a
few senior clerics folded their aprons round
their knees and crowded into cabs.
After all, the Society of Artists will
chance war-time, and hold a November show.
As usual, they remain faithful to their eyrie
in Queen Victoria Markets. Take the lift
and go as high as you can. Then climb
the Society’s corkscrew stairs. Mildred
Lovett (Mrs. Paterson) is back in Sydney
after a long absence in Brisbane, so her
china-painting ought to gladden the eye at
the coming art show.
Basso Cacialli hurt his ankle and couldn’t
drag the injured limb through “Ernani,” so
first-nighters either went elsewhere or heard
Balboni in “Pagliacei.” On Thursday evening
the deep-voiced singer saw the Gonsalez Co.
through the music of “Favorita.” But he
had to make heroic efforts not to go dead-
lame as . the Prior. He wore an orthodox
beard, but his monkly comrades (be careful
of that!) showed moustaches.
The usually-correct Cappelli "hadn’t even
disguised the ornament on his upper lip
while, as a Novice, he warbled of his love
for Eleonora. The lady (Miss Russ) had
Christmas-tree decorations in her hair for
her first appearance. But this was nothing.
In a later act she was dressed in ropes of
pearls as large as pigeons’ eggs, with her
front hair in a crown of the same quality.
The musical McQuades were in front seats.
As usual, conductor Gonsalez allowed late-
comers to bump his back as they squeezed
past into their stalls.
Florence Beliagg, only daughter of the
Elizabeth Bay household, has said “I will”
to J. A. Ellison, of the 2nd Australian Fly-
ing Squadron, Melbourne. The wedding
party was at the Australia.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
xMelbourne, October 10, 1910.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Fashion was lame at the Guineas at Caul-
field on Saturday. There was a moderately-
large crowd, but most of the women were
covered simply like pianos. Here and there
a light-hearted youngster had got herself up
like a fashion-plate; but you had to look for
her. One miss was arrayed in old-fashioned
snuff-colored silk bunched up at the back.
The hat was a good old “pork-pie.” One of
the Watson girls wore a real crinoline cov-
ered with hundreds of little grey glace frills.
A group of pretty Western District flappers
relied on weird hats to get them noticed.
One, in grey check tweeds, wore a black
belltopper. Another had scarlet zigzagged
on to a white transparent leaf. They all
wore their flare skirts like circus riders and
high boots of elaborate design. Mrs. Nox*-
man Falkiner had a fiat black silk hat
painted with brilliant birds. Mrs. Leslie
Macdonald wore Futurist headgear with
black and pink-silk stripes travelling from
ear to ear. The stripes were two inches
wide and the hat was 30in. in circumfer-
ence. The rest of her elaborate oufit was
white. A neat, navy-tailored girl wrnlked
with her as a rest for the eye. The terrace
of the grandstand was glorified with huge
tubs of white azaleas, which helped to show
off the gay rags.
Sam Mackay disguised himself with a
budding moustache, and wore lily-(A-the-
valley in his buttonhole. Mrs. Sam was not
in evidence. Mrs. H. K. S. Ward walked
under blue leaves climbing on a hat like a
black chimney capped with cabbage-roses.
Mrs. Purves’s hat kept to the horizontal, and
had mud-colored roses. The hats are truly
marvellous this year. The women are stick-
ing to sombre tailor-made clothes, but they
wear all sorts of wild dreams on their heads.
Squatter MacLeod escorted his sister, who
had a huge greengage plum on her hat. Mrs.
Dickenson (Mt. Lyell) sported two pretty
olive branches and a pink hat. The Widdis
and Mills Gippsland partnership threw their
crowns in the air when Ettefred got away
with the Guineas.
The grandstand and lawn was a mushroom
bed of umbrellas several times during the
changeable day. Crowds of the social also-
rans did not turn up. They are waiting for
the Caulfield Cup and its sunny-blue
weather.
“Guineas Night,” at Her Majesty’s, made
a packed and joyous house. The dress-circle
was pink with the best shoulders and necks
and the stalls were studded with the well-
groomed heads of fashionable dames and the
shirtfronts of their wage-earners. The boxes
were • nearly all occupied by men who had
raced and dined together. L. •K. S. Mac-
kinnon was in the stalls with a bunch of
soft-frocked womenkind. Mrs. Buchanan
sat in black velvet, with her husband. Mrs.
I lorsfall, in deep mourning, came with the
little Australian-French nurse who is Cap-
tain Fefgusson. The squatting Grants from
up Stawell way made a bright patch of color
with their gaily-frocked girls. All the sweet
young things in the audience were entranced
with the crinoline ballet. The frocking in
“The Cinema Star” is of the day-after-to-
morrow, and “just too fascinating” in these
drab times. Florence Young climbs out of
melon-colored tulle in one act. Minnie
Love’s tulle dress for the Baby Doll song has
white frills which turn up and down and go
criss-cross. The detail is outlined by loose
strings of jet beads, and there is a petticoat
of sparse jet fringe after the tulle furbelows
end. The Honolulu ballet is evidently what
decided Melba to book her passage. The
setting is entrancing. A white satin spooky
Pierrette ballet is backed by illimitable
space. Even the black-velvet hangings are
shadowed, and the wdiite-satin girls do their
gymnastic dances on the edge of the world.
Slim Ethel Cadman has her chance with
modern-frilled frocks. She moves in the
centre of oceans of outstanding frills. Florrie
Young, in another act, uses up yards of pink
glace for her gown, and punctuates it with a
garden full of pink cabbage-roses. She is
also a good deal taller than a white and blue
creation which she wears, and her arms are
bare as a boxer’s.
Moleswortli Greene is dead at just-on 90.
The wonderfully robust old squatter was
game to the last. He came to town for
the Show, but fell ill very suddenly during
the visit and died at the South Yarra house
of son Pomeroy, far from Greystanes, the
Bacchus Marsh palace-home he had built
in the very early days. In spite of his
great age the old man kept to his early-
rising habits. He had a den which he called
liis “workshop,” and which he used mostly
for interviewing his managers and overseers
and making them walk the chalk-line. And
a very rigid line it was; for Molesworth
Greene was an old-fashioned Tory, and all
his opinions were armor-plated. He would
tut about the run on horseback or behind
a high-stepper in a dogcart, and the Earth
would have to stop before the handsome old
boss would neglect to get into correct din-
ner dress in the evenings. Fellow-members
of the Melbourne Club acted as his pall-
bearers. Major Kerr-Pearse represented
the absent G.-G.
Mrs. Archie Alger, from Ceylon, gave a
sumptuous party the other night. She was
formerly Lassie Forbes, a Melbourne girl
whose good looks and Junoesque figure re-
peatedly got her jobs in private theatricals
and tableaux. Now she and her husband
grow elephants or moonstones or something
in the hills of Ceylon. Her pals responded
gamely to her invitation, and occupied a
third of the stalls of the Theatre Royal to
watch Hale Hamilton and Myrtle Tannehill
while waiting for supper at the Savoy,
which had been faked up to look like Indian
jungle infested with white-faced waiters in
Indian clothes. If anyone begged them to
black their faces they didn’t do it. The
menu _ included devilled guavas, stewed ele-
phant’s heel, and cocoanut curry, and a
party of vaudeville artists from the Bijou
threw in a turn when there was a pause in
the conversation. The Savoy almost became
a cabaret for the time being. The hostess
smiled in a smart pink dress whilst dainty
little Mrs. Guy Sherwood sported a red
opera cloak scrambled over with gold bro-
cade. Creswicks, Dalrymples and Robbs
were amongst the revellers.
_ Dr. Barrett’s name has been little heard
since the fuss in Egypt; and now we know
what the medico lias been doing to keep so
quiet. He has been catching flies. Lady
Helen lately received from him a sample of
his latest invention. Pharaoh would have
been able to do with less fan-hands if he
had possessed one.
Griffin, the un-Yankeelike Yank imported
to lay-out the Federal capital, is remodelling
a big eaf<§ in Collins street. In his private
practice Griffin works with his wife and her
brother, and the partnership is a happy one.
Mrs. Griff, is Melbourne’s second she-archi-
tect, the other being Muriel Scott, an Aus-
tralian girl. The decoration of the cafe will
be something absolutely new to Melbourne,
and for this part of it Miss Murphy will
surely catch a cheer from the critics.
Mrs. Woolcott is abroad again with a
mended ankle and a pink flapper hat. The
next big raid of the Buttoners she leads is
due on Friday, October 20, and will help
all the hospitals.
Owing to the crowd of race functions,
Mrs. Barlow has put back her party at the
Repertory Club to the first week in Decem-
ber.
These clever war nurses of ours gain
stars and crowns, but their rank carries no
extra pay, which is a pity if not an injus-
tice. Australian Rose Quarterman, who
has made a name for herself since the start
of the war, has just returned to England
as matron-in-charge of six sisters. She
proudly wears the crown of a major on her
shoulder, but there is no major’s pay in her
pocket. Sister Rose is the daughter of the late
squatter John Quarterman. Her mother is
80 years of age, one of Toorak's grand old
dames and the Australian-born daughter of
squatter Moodie, of South Brighton station,
which sits on the Mackenzie River, in the
Western District. She remembers the first
Germans that came into Victoria overland
from South Australia and settled on the
Moodie run. The invaders rolled over the
horizon in big white waggons, and the squat-
ter’s Slaughter has a vivid memory of a fat
German pushing a plough which was pulled
by the family cow and the farmer’s frau
harnessed together. That was a primitive
endurance test, but the watcher almost bet-
tered it at SO by standing outside the Fort
Melbourne pier gates from 11 o’clock till
five on the off-chance of having a farewell
handshake with her daughter.
“Captain” Fergusson isn’t a new Gent-
help, but a little fair nurse who is home on
furlough. She claims to have been the first
Australian nurse in France, where she has
been at work for two years with the Duchess
of Sutherland’s French hospital.
Rosemary Leveson-Gower (you say that
as if it was blood) was Nurse Fergusson’s
Y.A.D. That means that the Duchess’s
daughter had to scrub floors and do ward-
chores for her. They were located at
Dunkirk and had visits from lots of
people who are in the front seats of
history. The King of Belgium and the
Duke of Teck were two who used to drop
in occasionally for tea. Captain Fergusson
put in some of her spare time growing vege-
tables and flowers: asters and daisies in one
plot and lettuce and radishes in another.
One gathers from the little nurse that there
was rivalry between the Sutherland hospital
and the one established later by the Dudley
countess.
Balsillie, the rain-coaxer, is back on the
Block again, and showing the very latest
Central Australian fashions. Striped grey
“lustre” suiting, camel’s-breath socks and ox-
blood kid shoes are, I judge, what’s worn
“inside.”
Lieut. Stan Ricketson having cabled to
his war-bride to meet him in London, the
tall lady, accompanied by sister-in-law Yal-
dene Ricketson, lias gone to join the war-
rior.
Rupert Clarke, who is busy at transport
work somewhere, was last week promoted
to the rank of grandfather. Daughter Poss
did it. The new recruit is a son.
Local flappers wailed loudly when the
wretched rain washed the Scots' College
sports right on to the date fixed by Mel-
bourne Grammar. Some young things were
reduced to tears before choosing between the
sisterly duty of watching a brother dash
round the arena or cheering a best male
chum on to victory with the Scots. Long-
legged, dutiful daughters were dragged along
by the family to see the son of the house
leap and run, but damsels of spirit just
went where they listed. The younger gene-
ration have troubles of their own.
In the English officers’ rest homes the
pretty-girl volunteer-nurses don’t get within
yards of the heroes. They mostly work in
the humble seclusion of the kitchen. One
day an attractive Australian girl somehow
managed to understudy an elderly dame who
usually carried in a tray of teacups. The
entry created a tremendous sensation in the
ward. After a breathless and astonished
silence, every patient implored her to step
along his way amidst notes of admiration
and interrogation. But before the gratified
visitor had time to take her pick she was
discovered and shooed out by the angry
chieftainess, who warned her not to repeat
the offence under pain of instant dismissal.
It’s like an Arabian Nights tale told back-
wards.
The Engineers are comforted by a com-
mittee which has its headquarters at the
Engineers’ Depot in Alexandra Avenue and
has been doing solid work for a couple of
years. Mrs. G. F. Wilkinson is president.
Miss Claridge is secretary and Major
Sheldon’s wife looks after the cash, of
which there is never enough. But if money
is scarce willing fingers are fairly numerous,
and the hard-toiling sapper, grubbing in
muck and awful danger, sends them his
blessing from a country which one of them,
after a week’s hard rain, calls Flounders.
In the latest
call-up, the adorers
of Walter Kirby
dreaded his fate
when affairs reached
section K. They
feared that hard-
hearted Authori-
ties would parade
the plump tenor
stripped of all
adornment except
his Godiva mous-
tachios and a
shower of silvery
notes. In the pre-
bellum year, the
tenor blushingly
admitted 2!) years.
Now the awful fact
is laid bare that
Kirby is facing the 40 hurdle. So the
plump parade is postponed.
The Victorian Artists’ much-postponed
pageant play is to hit Melbourne in the
interval between the Cups—October 27 and
28. At The Playhouse, of course.
The Pageant people are dealing out
tickets for a fund-gathering dinner party at
the Oriental on October 20. It is difficult to
enrol men for the tables. The other day
when some women tried to persuade one of
Sassiety’s short-haired pets to appear in
their set, “Oh!” he moaned, “Ai couldn't!
One nevah dines now, you know. Whai, I
don’t think I could even find a clean collar.”
_ Bright rose pink is “in” again, and the
girl with a decent complexion is revelling
in it. The gladdest bud in the bunch is the
brunette with a peaehes-and-cream cheek, a
cream neck and a pink kitten ribbon round
her throat. Pale darlings and blondes can
only look on enviously.
Sister Adelaide’s historical notes—
The Eight Hours holiday was celebrated chiefly at
the S.A.J.C. race-meeting, which was continued
from the previous Saturday. .Side attractions were
the Strathalbyn Show, the, usual procession and
family outings to the hills and the seaside. The Gov-
ernor escaped by leaving’ for Melbourne, aided by
Private Secretary Winser and W. J. Gunson. His
Ex. on his return will travel per river steamer
as far as Murray Bridge after having inspected the
Victorian irrigation settlements with the walking-
delegate of the Governors’ Union in Vic.
The Cheer-Up Society is adding a billiard-room
to its Hut near the City Baths. The foundation
was set by Lady Galway on Tuesday morning in
the presence of donors of cement, gravel, sand,
metal, cash and other materials. The cost of con-
struction will be further reduced by volunteer labor.
C. T. McGlew, S.A.’s consul for Russia, who
enlisted in December as a' private, has advanced to a
commission, via Duntroon. He is on duty at the
Murray Bridge camp. Another recent recruit is F.
Bennett, a St. Peter’s College master, who has gone
to join the forces in his own State (Tasmania).
South Aus. civil servants have blazoned a great
example by subscribing, up to the end of August,
over £31,000 to patriotic funds. From the Public
Service Patriotic Fund came over £14,000; from
the Public School Teachers’ Fund, £6643 ; from
the Police Fund, nearly £3000; and from the
Railway Officers’ Fund, £7678.
Surgeon-Major Jeffries, of Woodville, has got his
D.S.O. He left as a captain with the Field Ambu-
lance in the 2nd Expeditionary Force, was wounded
and mentioned in despatches at Gallipoli, and
gained his majority and his decoration in France.
Manufacturers’ Week is “off” this year. However,
that won’t stop us adding an item to our list of
local industries. The Repatriation Committee of the
State War Council is buying a toy-making business
to open up employment for returned soldiers. The
idea is to give instruction to men who will make
the trade their permanent occupation.
Dr. Helen Sexton arrived by the Medina on
Thursday and went on to Melbourne by express. Dr.
Helen is said to be the only woman (exclusive of
royalty) who has presented a British decoration to
a French soldier, a ceremony which she performed
when she was in charge of the Australian Hospital
at Auteuil. The Countess of Glasgow, who is bound
for Maoriland, where her lord once held a job as
Governor, was a passenger by the same boat. She
spent an hour or so at Government House before
sailing again in the mailboat.
A letter from Bertram Mackennal says definitely
that there can be no King Edward Memorial till
after the war. He had said so before, but the local
committee urged him to see if the difficulty couldn’t
be overcome. His reply is that all bronze foundries
are making munitions and are controlled by the War
Office. So we must wait for our statue.
Joan Dow, who was with Lady Dudley’s Aus-
tralian Hospital in France and afterwards at the
British Red Cross Hospital at Netley, has been sent
to the Contracts Department of the War Office. Her
sister, who is also a nurse, was recently mentioned
in despatches.
Private L. E. Pearce, of the A.M.C., a grandson
of the discoverer of the Boulder and Ivanhoe Gold
Mines, has died in England of meningitis, contracted
while on duty at a military hospital.
The report that S'ergeant Jacob, who won his
medal at Pozieres, was missing apparently came
from the Intelligence Department, because the ser-
geant is in hospital in England.
Four Australian-born Germans (brothers) have
announced their objection to take up arms against
the country of their parents, a delicacy of feeling
which didn’t prevent the quartette drawing salaries
from the State Treasury before and since the war
broke out.
Mr. J. D. Johnston, chairman of the Onkaparinga
Racing Club, who had been ill for some time, died
on Friday.
The late Chief Justice Way’s old home, Montefiore,
in North Adelaide—splendidly situated but out of
date —seems to have found a real owner at last. The
notorious Paul Joseph bought it first, but it was
quickly in the market again, and passed to Vincent
Zed, a Syrian financier who has been making money
in S.A. lately. Within a month he turned it over
at a profit, and now it has been sold again to Harold
Fisher, an Adelaide merchant, son of the better-

“Pick”: The most untamable outlaw is
the renegade thoroughbred horse. At Iviandra
(N.S.W.) a blood filly once eloped with the kings
of the mountain brumbies, and for two years evaded
every effort to coax her back to civilisation. Then
George Harris, the horse-tamer of that rough
country, built a trap-yard with half-mile wings,
and planned to earn the standing reward offered for
the runaway. On a favorable opportunity he rushed
her mob down between the converging fences and
into the 12ft. yard at the other end. But whilst
he galloped into Iviandra for his tackling, another
i hap who wanted the reward called at the yard.
In three acts he outed a brumby, peeled him, and
twisted a halter out of his hide. Then he wrestled
IDENTIFICATION.
THE G ADDER: “Tell
your mistress I shall
give her a call to-
morrow.”
THE JEWEL (fresh
from the back-
blocks): “Orright.
Any pertickler
coo-ec, mum?”
with the filly until she broke away and dived out
of sight through the jungles. She was never again
taken, though for years the district’s sole idea of
sport was to organise hunting parties to run the
wild mare that carried a gnarled rope of dried
horsehide attached to her head.
“Wandi”: Binghi at bis dinner is an
edifying spectacle. Wearing nothing but his skin,
as a rule, he squats with his feet doubled under him,
holding the ’possum leg in his hands and tearing the
flesh off with his fangs. A little fire burns near
him, on which a portion of the banquet is still cook-
ing-, and which he or Mrs. Binghi, who squats near,
tends from time to time. Much of the food is eaten
half raw. His dogs, a regiment of them, crowd
round the boss during the feast, sometimes snatch-
ing a bone from his mouth. They also share his
gunyah and his bed, and he appreciates the warmth
of their bodies on a cold night. Some dogs snore
terribly; others are all night scratching themselves;
some are given to barking and hunting imaginary
wallabies in their sleep. But Binghi is used to all
that. He loves his dog, and cuddles the pups in his
arms. He keeps all that come, and should the
mother die the little orphans are reared with tender-
est care, often on the woman’s breast. I once saw
a gin sitting in a gunyah suckling a piccaninny at
one breast and a pup at the other.
“E.S.S.”: A flock of bloodstained cocka-
toos (or little corellas) at a watering-place would
strike a towny with astonishment. They come in at
sundown to a regular camping-place, generally near
a big waterhole in a creek, called a cockatoo hole.
Here one may see flocks approximating 100,0’00
birds, and the ear-splitting row they make would pre-
vent one hearing a coo-ee at 10 yards from their
perching trees. Favorite holes were Burke and
Wills’s Depot and at Burke’s Tree, the latter one of
the most beautiful spots on Koopa Creek (“Cooper’s
Creek” on the map). Burke’s Tree, a straggling
coolabah, railed in, is 20 miles from the depot, about
200 yards off the main Innamincka-road, and 150
yards from the water —a hole teeming with fish. It
is a great picnic resort of the local people, who say
that Burke’s body was found under it, covered over
with wood in accordance with the aboriginal burial
custom. Twigs are carried away from it by the
drovers for their best girls to make penhandles.
About these cockatoo holes iii the breeding
season black brother reaps a rich harvest. Day after
day he returns to camp with dillybags filled with
eggs and young birds. Great numbers of birds are
knocked out of the packed trees, and also killed
on the wing, with boomerang and waddy, though
Binghi’s most successful bags are made with a drop
net, which is used at the water when the birds
swoop down to drink.
“Seety”: I will guarantee to discourage
“Inver G.’s” moths and silverfish. Let him continue
to use pepper, but let it be black pepper. He may
use white pepper till the bottle-ohs at the Front nest
again without interfering with the pests’ digestive
organs. I had an Indian rug that had battalions
of moths and silvers operating on it, but after I had
bombarded them several times with brunette pepper
the blighters surrendered.
“Y.Z.” : Billy went to the war, he said on
starting, to get away from Cows. He was back
and in civilian costume once more, but minus the
right hand, and he was hanging about Melbourne and
rather miserable. “There’s not much without a ’and,”
he told me. I spoke of the new artificial ones, and
explained the marvellous things they can do. Billy’s
face brightened visibly. A great hope shone in his
eyes. “Gorstruth!” he said, “could a man milk
with ’em ?”

“Toby C.” : The roan cow was reported to
have calved in the river paddock and I was des-
patched to find and bring home the mother a"d
infant. Ma was soon located, but a three-hour
search failed to reveal the offspring. I brought the
old lady home and put in the next three days
searching the 40-acre plot. On the fourth day I
continued the chase accompanied by the cow, but
she appeared to have no recollection of the affair
and failed to locate the poddy. Accepting the
matter as a mystery I tossed it. Nine days later
I struck the little stranger, alive and well, after
13 days without refreshment from mother. There
were no othef cattle on the run ; only two yearling colts
and a swamp to soothe the pangs of hunger. I am
busy learning the cow language in order to find
out how the little one managed.

“Yancy” : Is there a better bushie’s stove
than the plain oildrum? Cut the top out of the
receptacle, knock a few holes in the bottom, and place
two wires crosswise at the top to hold the billy and
grill the chop. With the aid of a tin-opener or a
knife make two vertical cuts of three or four inches
close to the bottom. These cuts should be about 6in.
apart. Next, cut across and pull the piece down.
This will do for the toast or the devilled herrings. If
people only knew the delicacy of herrings toasted
this way there would be an oildrum stove in every
home in Australia.

“Tasman”: “C.H.F.” (B. 21/9/’lt>) asks
if anyone can tell him how to get rabbits out of
river flats and banks. Digging, as he says, is far
too costly. Besides, it’s quite unnecessary, unless
“C.H.F.” likes the exercise. There are two or three
other methods. Ferrets are very satisfactory, pro-
vided the bunnies are lodging in warrens. If
“C.H.F.” finds ferrets too exciting he can try
one of the sulphurous preparations that are injected
through the front door with the idea of “gassing”
the inhabitants. After the operation the hole is
blocked up and the tenant is left in a ready-made
grave. But by this method one doesn’t get the skin ;
and the skin is a consideration when prices are
high. If these plans don’t suit, what's the matter
with trapping? That’s sure enough and simple
enough for anyone. If “C.H.F.” uses ferrets he
will of course need nets or a gun.

THE WINKLE AND THE TAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Stand clear, periwinkle, and give a poor tar
A chance to get in his an-ke-ar,
For the grog’s all swiped,
And the bosun’s piped
All hands to haul on the ankar.
It's asking too much of a deepsea tar
To haul both you and the ank-e-ar.
With half the crew jugged,
And some of them slugged,
There’s few to heave on the ankar.
There once was a winkle wot went so far
That he wouldn't leave go of the ank-e-ar,
So we took him down
To old London town,
Where the fogs choked him on his ankar.
And there was a winkle wot clung to the bar,
To the bar of the old tramp’s ank-e-ar,
And he frizzed away
In the sun’s hot ray,
So his gridiron it was the ankar.
But they wasn’t as heavy as you by far,
Else we couldn’t have raised the ank-e-ar.
Oh, blow winds, blow,
He’s give us a show —-
The winkle is free of the ankar.
MAORILAND.
HERBERT BARR.

The Utmost Delights in Motoring
Are Given by the Mitchell Six, the Master Car
YOU can lean back comfortably in the deep upholstery of the Mitchell
with full confidence in the entire reliability of its six-cylinder
engine, and give yourself up completely to the enjoyment of the
landscape gliding past. In the Mitchell you experience the exhilaration
and pleasure of motoring in fullest measure. The hills seem to
melt away before you. Never is your delight marred by any de c-iency
in the engine or the car. Its magneto ignition is unfailing; its whole
mechanism is efficient.
You pass over rough roads without in the least feeling the rough-
ness, for the Bate cantilever springs make the car ride easily and
smoothly. The engine is responsive, flexible, obedient to your slightest
wish. Every joy that motoring can give is brought you by the Mitchell.
The sheer beauty of its lines charms you. You appreciate the conveni-
ences it possesses-engine-driven tyre pump, electric lighting, self-starter,
ample roominess, due to its long, 125-inch wheelbase.
The cost of upkeep is low; the car has run 26f miles to the gallon
of benzine under official observation. Yet the price is moderate. The
Mitchell costs considerably more in America than any ear selling here at
the same price.

MAGNESIA FOR DYSPEPSIA.
SPECIALIST RECOMMENDS IT
INSTEAD OF DRUGS.
“Only those in constant touch with
sufferers from indigestion and dyspepsia
can fully realise the harm done by the im-
proper use of drugs and artificial digest-
ants,” remarked an eminent specialist
recently. “Personally I rarely advocate the
use of drugs in the treatment of digestive
or stomach troubles, for in practically every
instance I have proved the underlying cause
to be excessive acidity of the stomach and
consequent fermentation of the food con-
tents. Therefore, in place of the once
ividely-used drugs I invariably recommend
the use of magnesia to neutralise the acidity
and stop the food fermenting, and the
excellent results I have obtained during the
past three years convince me that there is
no finer treatment for indigestion, dyspep-
sia, etc. It must, of course, be clearly
understood that I do not employ or advise
the use of such forms of magnesia as cit-
rates, acetates, sulphates, etc.—these would
often do more harm than good ; nothing but
lisurated magnesia—the form prescribed by
physicians—should ever be used to neutralise
stomach acids. This is not at all difficult
to obtain —in fact, I find that most chemists
now stock the preparation in tablet form in
addition to the ordinary- powder. Half a
teaspoonful of bisurated magnesia in pow-
der form or two compressed tablets taken
with a little water after meals will usually
be found quite sufficient to instantly neutra-
lise the acidity and prevent food fermenta-
tion, thereby ensuring painless, natural
digestion for even chronic sufferers.”— Advt.

“D.F.” : If the popularity of “our national
flower’’ is to be judged by the score or so of people
who gathered to hear Director Cronin, of the
Botanical Gardens, and Treasurer Pescott, of the
Wattle League, talk about the plant on Melbourne
Wattle Day, it’s in a bad way. Still, as Pcscott
remarked, even the city people, who a few years ago
knew only two kinds of wattle, the golden and the
silver, are being educated a little. Now you see
in Melbourne on Wattle Day 39 distinct species
of wattle. Most people, too, have learned to
get wattle blossom without tearing half the tree
down. But there’s room for more education, and
Pescott urged that Vic. should copy the Westralian
Act for the protection of native shrubs and flowers.
Director Cronin gave a few useful hints to intending
glowers. He said the broad-leaved wattles would
not stand the smoke and grime of the city. For
instance, the silver wattle, which used to flourish all
along the Yarra banks in the early days, refused to
do any good now. The spiny-leaved wattles were the
ones to grow in and close round Melbourne nowa-
days. Manuring wattles, by the way, is a fatal
mistake; nothing is worse for them than a heavy
dose of stable litter.

“K.Q.”: Let me come to light with a
dinkum remedy for wheat-rust. Tell ’em to use
“bleached” wheat next time. Hot water and blue-
stone are all right, but there’s nothing wrong with
Nature’s own pickle. Australian sunshine and air-
moisture do the trick with exposed grain. Anyway,
that’s how a number of Tamworth (N.S.W.) farmers
account for the cleanliness of their crops.

THE UNSELFISHNESS OF THE “ANTI.”
YOUNG MAN ON THE SOFA: “Yes, I say what this fellow here says—I’m not going to

“X.Y.”: Have read many references to
Black Brother’s vegetable menu, but one item has
been omitted. On the North Coast of N. S. Wales
the principal vegetable food of the blacks was towak,
or native yam. Not the little bulbs, or tubers, which
schoolchildren and white cockatoos dig and devour
in the Spring', but large, substantial roots,
not unlike those of the dahlia. This native
food is rapidly becoming extinct. Its habi-
tat was the second-class brush land between
the rich cedar bush and the forest —country
very often covered with myrtle scrub. The
above-ground part of the plant is quite insig-
nificant in appearance; a small vine with
foliage like miniature melon leaves. Under
the soil, close to the surface, a cluster of
large tubers is found under each vine.
When I was a boy, at every ploughing of a
paddock these yams would be turned out in
quantities that were a trouble to clear up.
I have often roasted and sampled the vege-
table and found it by no means unpalatable,
though having a slightly-weedy flavor. Black
Brother was as energetic in the digging of
towak as he was in pursuit of the elusive
and smellful ’possum, or watto, and has left
tangible reminders of his commissariat
activity. A piece of ground which produced
towak can be recognised at once b,y the
presence of roughly-eircular pits about 18in.
in depth and 10ft. or more in diameter.
An area I took up 20 years ago was like an
old diggings or as though it had been shelled
with high explosives. These towak holes
are not to be confused with melon-holes,
crab-holes or gilgais, which are to be found
in wet, rush-covered areas. No bushman
could mistake them; besides, towak • was
never to be found in marshy or swampy
soil. Many years since, during a dry time,
I came upon two men who were prospecting
for water at the edge of a very large area
of swamp country. At 13ft. they came upon
a bed of petrified towalc 18in. to 2ft. in thick-
ness. The tubers were in outward appearance
exactly like those growing in patches of red soil on
ridges in the neighborhood, but they had been con-
verted into solid bluestone so hard that it required a
strong blow with a digging bar to break them. Evi-
dently a big subsidence had formed the swamp basin,
and highly mineralised water had petrified the towak
tubers. I do not know what was the limit of size
attained by towak, but I remember attempting to
transport two large specimens in a two-gallon bucket
and failing to induce the two to remain in the bucket.
“Barcoo”: “Hay” (B. 31/8/’l6) is rash
in declaring that Binglii does not like roast snake,
and prefers gohanna. There are two species of
gohanna in Queensland and Central Aus. —the big,
black, carrion-eating variety and the much smaller
species, of almost variegated hue, with yellow sides
and underparts. This fellow, who is not a car-
rion-eater, is found in shallow burrows in the
sand ridges of the Georgina and the Mulligan,
where Binghi tracks him down, digging him out
with a yam-stick. The local nig refers to this as
the corni, and it is the chosen dish at the banquet.
The big black one is neglected—“ Baal that phello
’tinkin’.” BOt roast snake, when the blacks
flourished on the Warrego, Maranoa and the Barcoo—
not many years ago, either —was preferred to the
luxuries of < ven the shearing-shed and cook’s
galleys.
“Te Babarantiko”: Generally speaking
Black and Brown take a broadminded view of the
little flirtations of their wives (“Ses,” B. 7/9/’l6) ;
but where love-making is crude and direct so is the
settlement. In some parts of the Western and North-
western Pacific the punishment is death, and away
down the Central Pacific, where women now have
some “rights,” mutilation by biting off the nose is
the method whereby the husband expresses disap-
proval. In the Line Islands, in particular, comely
noseless ladies are not at all rare. Nobody minds,
and a noseless young woman possesses the additional
attraction of a visible “past.”
* * * *
“A Borig”: “Nyxn” (B. 21/9/’l6) asks a
question re absence of hares where rabbits abound
which will take some answering. About 1896
80,000 hares were sent away from Nimitybelle
(N.S.W.) and surrounding districts. In 1900 the
rabbits arrived and the hares disappeared. Nobody
knows where they went, but as the rabbits came
from the west the hares might have been expected
to go east. Apparently they did not, or hares
would be pretty numerous on the coast by now.
Some believe that the rabbits killed and perhaps
devoured the hares. Some people will believe any-
thing but the plain unvarnished truth.
* * * *
“Ivaraji”: The fungus forwarded by
“D.Z.” (B. 14/9/’l6) is the same species as that
found upon South American beeches. Chas. Hedley,
of the Australian Museum, pointed that out years
ago in a lecture before the London Linnean Society.
This tree is not the timber commonly known in
commercial circles by that name, although it is a
valuable timber. It exists at Dorrigo (N.S.W.),
and I recently found it in the headwaters of the
Williams and Paterson, where it is called the “mock
cedar.” The settlers never use it except to burn.
It is a magnificent ornamental evergreen, but being
Australian it is, of course, not cultivated.
“The Reefer”: Traditional lore and im-
portant episodes of tribal life are by some Papuan
peoples preserved in pantomimes from generation to
generation. It is great entertainment to watch
“boys” re-acting episodes in their experiences. The
realism and particularity of detail shown in these
performances prove how keen an observer the Papuan
is. I once saw two boys playing “Temptation.” One
represented a kikeni (girl) who was having 40 winks
beside a bush track. The other was returning from
a distant village. As he perceived the sleeping
beauty he loitered contemplatively, arid then, con-
quering his inclinations, resolutely resumed his
journey. Several time he paused, each halt being
longer than the previous one, until his fortitude fail-
ing he turned with an eager, anticipating countenance
and hurried back to where the girl lay in abandoned
sprawl. The buck feverishly pressed his spear into
the sand ready to his hand in case of surprise, and
bent caressingly over the maiden. She suddenly
awoke, gaped momentarily nonplussed at the rogue,
and then vigorously drove both feet against his chest,
knocking him head-over-heels. The audience shrieked
with delight, and the ribald advice to the discouraged
lover would have boiled a billy.

“Trafalgar”: I have done an old shearing
hero an injustice. In B. 21/9/’l6 I stated that
“Parkes” Sutherland, of Coolah (N.S.W.), had shorn
over 80,000 sheep. It should have read 300,000.
And I should have added that he has been ringer
in every shed he has shorn in since 1882. As a
penance I am going on a jag for a month.
THE POLITIC PARENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In Boggabri lived Mrs. Bunn,
She had a boy—the only one—
Who was, as well she understood,
The terror of the neighborhood.
His misdeeds would a large book fill,
While his good qualities were nil.
Miss Mink called round (it gave her pain,
She said, to do so) to complain
Of Tommy Bunn—he’d stoned her cat,
And broken windows at her flat.
“I’ll punish him,” says Mrs. Bunn,
And stop once and for all his fun!”
Swift she produced a hefty stick,
Which, she opined, would “do the trick.”
“Ml beat him till he’s black and blue—
You’ll watch me doin’ of it, too!
With this, Miss Mink, I’ll whale his hide!”
But mild Miss Mink was horrified.
“Don’t, ma’am, please don’t! I never leant,”
Said she, “towards corp’ral punishment.
“A scolding, a good talking to,
As far as I’m concerned, will do.
“That stick wil! hurt him dreadfully—
Ilis screams and cries of agony
“Will almost, I imagine, reach
From Boggabri to Bondi beach!”
Said Mrs. Bunn: “Don’t worry! Lor’,
I’ve whacked Tom with it oft before.
“To hurt him, though, I never durst—
I alius chloroform him first?”

“Ouiclam” : The Savinas Bank Department
of the Commonwealth Bank doesn’t seem to
<*row more convenient as it grows older.
“While I live I’ll grow worse.” appears to be
its motto. It has abandoned the habit ot
keeping any stock of pass-books at agencies,
so that when a new account is opened the
depositor has to wait several days before the
head office can forward a book. Also, it a
depositor’s book is tilled with entries his
financial transactions must stop till lie has
posted it, at his own expense, to the head
office to have a fe\v extra leaves gummed in.
Apparently Denison Miller is too great a
man to run a Savings Bank, and when it
comes to a competition between the State and
Federal savings institutions the State wins
easily. Yet the people who lend their money
to the Commonwealth get only 3 per cent, for
if. so they might legitimately ask for a
little more consideration.

“Hampton” : Private Enterprise, with all
its boasted business acumen, frequently
wastes a good deal of energy, stamps, etc.,
on doing a useless formality, while leaving
undone what would be of practical service.
For example, consider the case of the person
who forwards a bale of skins to a Sydney
auctioneering firm. Almost immediately he
receives a post-card notifying receipt of his
letter of advice and promising best endeavors,
etc. This cheers him into believing that the
organisation of the firm in regard to details
is perfect. But later on, when for a fort-
night or three weeks he doesn’t hear whether
the firm has received the goods, and isn’t
told at what sale they will be offered, this
bright illusion fades. Some day, possibly,
P.E. will drop to the fact that the arrival
of the letter of advice, which isn’t very likely
to go astray, isn’t the principal feature of the
argument, lout that it would particularly
delight the sender to know that his goods had
been taken delivery of from the much-dis-
trusted railways, and that they would be
offered for sale on a certain date.

28/9/T6) has rivals hi the Misses Clee, of
Burnie (Tas.), who have run a hairdressers
and tobacconists’ business for years. The
only male in the business is a boy wlio puts
the lather on before a Miss Glee runs the
razor over you. She’s a good tradeswoman,
too, and all the better because she doesn t
know what’s going to win the Cup. I don t
know how it is now, but a few years ago the
only other barber’s shop in Burnie was also
mostly run by the smooth-faced sex. -Lite
owner was one Ryan, but the Misses Ryan
generally did the shaving. I fancy they aie
married now, though, and, I suppose, are
out of the trade —except for exercising the
wife’s privilege of shearing the old man.

tell this story of business, deception, taitn-
lessness and dog-stealing:
We was all eomfo'table with the Bonsons but fer
the dog. Mrs. Bonson owned it. He wasn’t much
iv a dog- just one iv them dancin’ terriers ’bout the
size iv a Vienna roll, but worth his weight > m
di’monds, an’ more trouble than three cows an’ a
motor-car. We vfes all the slaves iv Little Jim.
Old Bonson was on’y kep’ about the place as sort
iv ’ead caretaker of his wife’s dog; an’ I was danc-
in’ attendance on the cheese mite all day ’n' arf the
night. So when Sarer hinted that she’d a distant
relation name iv Whiffle dyin’ fer jist such a dog-
I wasn’t over-indignant thinkin’ of her orriu dis-
honesty. In fac\ I pinched Little Jim ’n’ Sarer
took him in her muff an’ give him to the far-away
relation with everyone’s compliments. Two hours
later, when missus come ’ome, the storm broke.
Mrs.' Bonson ’ad hysterics in eight rooms; Mr.
Bonson went off his silly ’ead in the excitement
caused by his wife’s great loss, an' the servants
looked that sad you’d think there was murder ’n’ man-
slaughter all over the place. But Little Jim was
gone fer good, an’ us girls ’ad a sort iv continuous
public ’oliday. But on the third evemn Bonson
’ad us all in the dinin’-room, an’ -sez he: It s plain,
he sez, “your carelessness is responsible fer the loss
of Little Jim. He’s a most expensive dog, n
intend offerin’ a reward iv £lO for his recovery.
What’s more, I’m goin’ to deduct the £lO fiom
the servants’ wages. It’ll make you more careful.
If any of you objects you can take your money ant
go.” Naturally none iv us raised any objections.
We knew Little Jim was provided for for life;
consequently—our troubles! Well, the advt. was
put in the papers, an’ we in the kitchen was
delighted with it, more especially Sarer. Wot ap-
pcned next? Oh, nothin’ t’ make a song about.
Only that ’ound Whiffle brought the dog back when
he saw the advt., an’ we’ve got Little Jim worse n
worse, ’n’ what’s more, Whiffle took the £lO
reward —our ten pound !”
“D.C.8.”: When “McNorth” says (B.
3/8/T6) that the cultivation of cocoa-
nuts offers a highly probable means of set-
tling the far northern coast of Australia he
is right enough, providing the I ederal Gov-
ermnent is prepared to formulate some
scheme of advancing the necessary capital
to would-be settlers. A 99-year lease would
also be necessary. It is a mistaken idea
that eocoanuts will not flourish except along
the foreshores. In Ceylon, W est Africa
and many other tropical countries there
are cocoanut estates over a hundred miles
inland, and they are payable propositions.
A frontage of two-thirds of the depth lias
nothing against it, but any area under lut
acres would not be worth while. Irom the
time of planting till the producing stage is
reached six years may easily be allowed.
One hundred acres would produce not more
than 20 tons copra in the _ seventh year,
increasing up to say 50 tons in the fifteenth
year. From the fifteenth year onwards
there may be a further slight increase, but
it would be unsafe to reckon on more than
12cwt. of copra per acre per year. The
cost of planting 100 acres and bringing it
into bearing runs from £l2OO to £l5OO in
the Solomons. In North Australia the cost
would be between £2OOO and £3OOO. Allow’-
ing‘ for good markets always, the profits
from the seventh-year crop would not exceed
£200; when a hundred acres were in full
bearing the nett annual profits would be
about £6OO. Given regular and suitable
means of communication with Southern
markets, the revenue could be considerably
augmented by catch crops, and when the
trees were old enough, say in the fifth yeai.
cattle or other stock could be introduced,
thus overcoming somewhat the cost of plan-
tation upkeep and providing additional
revenue. It is important that areas selected
should be outside the hurricane belt, other-
wise the whole scheme would be a gamble.
“Ses”: The 1915-16 season made records
in more ways than one. While N. S. Wales
astonished travellers with its enoimous
stacks of wheat at railway sidings, South
Aus. was making a great haystack display.
Not far from Gladstone are five w;ell-built
heaps that constitute one of the sights of
the district. Three of them contain 600 tons
each, one over 800 tons and the other over
3000 tons. The giant is 250 yards in cir-
cumference, but it is beaten easily by a
colossus at Riverton, which is 150 yards
long and contains over 1300 tons.

SUSPICIONS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Within the common kitchen
Where we, just now, exist,
Before each meal we gather
And talk there, in a mist
Of steam and other vapors
The singing gas distils,
Of war and rising rentals
And sordid tradesmen’s bills,
And what we’ve got for breakfast
Perhaps its eggs or meat.
But no one’s ever heard yet
What .Jobson’s going to eat.
His saucepan’s always steaming
Beside the actress’s
And Madam Bonser’s bucket,
Whose contents all can guess.
“Oh, I’ve got nice beef-olives,”
' The little typist says.
“This stew of tripe,” another
Declares, “has soaked for. days.”
But .Jobson never opens
Ills mouth or lifts the lid
Of his old, battered saucepan
That is in steam-clouds hid.
Then, by and bye, we weary
iVnd Jobson says, 111 mind
The cooking. Read your papers.”
The typist says, “I find
There’s something I’ve forgotten. ’
So, one by one, we go,
And Jobson stands there nobly
And regulates things so.
“All right!” we hear him calling.
“Thank you!” we all reply.
And coming, see him going,
With rapture in his eye.
Of late we’ve had suspicions.
I missed a spud one day,
Madam was short an onion,
While I regret to say
The typist, lost an olive,
And old Ma Shipton swears
A piece of steak went roving
When she had run downstairs.
A carrot that a spinster
Set store by vanished too,
Though Jobson said that carrots
Oft boiled themselves from view.
One day—a rare chance offered —
1 looked in Jobson’s pan.
’Twas filled with boiling water,
And that won’t feed a man.
I questioned him about it.
lie said, on certain days,
He only took hot water
Served up in different ways.
Yes, we’ve our grave suspicions,
And what has got us beat
' Is that we’ve never yet heard
What Jobson’s going to eat.
MAORILAND.
WILL LAWSON.
LIGHTS ALONG THE RIVER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When I was leaving Melbourne the night was warm
ami still,
And captive ships were sleeping as even captive.
Our well-lit decks were crowded as we turned down
the stream, • . , . „
But wharves and town and river were prisoned in
dream.
The far, faint shrieks of engines, some great clock s
parting chime,
Came floating- from the city, at this our sailing
time; .
The great screws churned the river and turned our
hows to sea ;
The lights along the river winked cheerfully at mo.
Oh, down the winding Yarra the winking lights aie
set,
And good it is to see them when eyes with tears
are wet; ..
For when the farewell racket grows fainter in the
night, , . ,
“So long! we’ll see you later!” says ev ry cheerful
light.
K.S.W.
E. FRANCIS.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The process of ousting German trade in
Australia is still going strong. The Nippon
Yusen Kaisha has had to charter four
extra steamers, which will be arriving in
Melbourne with Jap. goods in the next six
or eight weeks. And the co.’s own liners
will be coming in as usual.
Waiting for war to come and pound on
the front door is the job of neutrals. There
are too many of them already.
Dr. Burgess, of the Presbyterian General
Assembly, is a fair sample of a little mob
that has got its brains tangled. He would,
he says, have followed Hughes if Billy had
shouldered his responsibilities; but now he
must vote No. Then why doesn’t Dr.
Burgess turn down Christianity because
Judas Iscariot was a disappointment?
THE COMPENSATIONS OF WAR.
BILLJIM: “An’ what would you do if I kissed
you ?”
ANNETTE: “I would call gran’mere. Poor
gran’mere! She ’as been quite deaf
since ze last bombardment.”
Compulsory service for home defence is
the law of Australia; more than that, it is
on the platform of the Labor party. Prac-
tically everybody agrees that it is the only
right thing. Nobody proposes to repeal it.
The whole point of the objection to the
present proposal is that to fight in Europe
is not to fight for home defence. To test
that, let us consider what would happen if
Britain and the Allies were smashed on the
field of France. Germany in that event would
be in a position to dictate terms. She would
decide, among other things, what should
become of Britain’s colonies. Every big war
in history has been followed by the redistri-
bution of colonies. Even when the mother
country has not lost a foot of soil one colony
or the" other has been taken by the con-
queror. We have taken German New Guinea,
and what is happening in France will decide
whether we keep it or not. What is happen-
ing in France will just as surely decide
whether we keep Australia or not. If we
don’t keep it, overboard will go all our
ideals. Hence we are defending Australia in
France just as surely as we would be if we
were fighting in Australia. And that being
so, the fight in France is a fight in Home
Defence —the fight for which even the anti-
*conseriptionist says that compulsory service
is the only right thing.
[S. LI. Prior, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
The other day Premier Holman made one
of the most lucid statements of his brilliant
career as a Treasurer. He flung down a lot
of figures about alleged profit and loss and
accumulated deficits and credits on his brick-
works and motor garage and “building con-
struction” and bakery and so on, and the
sum total of it all was : Profit on the year
£42,392, loss £696oaccumulated profit
£86,016, loss £73,892. Therefore he claimed
the right to give three cheers. ...What Hol-
man didn’t mention, because honesty and
frankness are the last things in the world
that a party politician can afford to deal in,
was that the receipts from all the things in
which N. S. Wales’s £131,000,000 has been
“invested” amounted to £666,433 less than
the interest. Also he didn’t mention that
this was £353,546 worse than the previous
year.
Nobody who has been brought up on the
milk of trades-unionism can consistently
vote “No” in the conscription referendum.
The first principle of unionism is that a
wrong done to one is a wrong done to all.
On that foundation the whole structure is
built. Now we have formed a union of
nations. Suppose we were invaded. Sup-
pose,' in some way or other, Germany had
been able to send down half a million troops
to Australia, and a bitter campaign was
being fought in our own country. What
should we say if our Allies told us: “Oh.
no, the invasion of Australia isn’t hurting
us. That is a matter for Australia”? What
should we say if Russia, having driven
the enemy across the border, said to Bel-
gium : “That’s all we need trouble about.
Finish it yourselves”? What would the
trade-unionist say if, during a strike, one
section of the workmen got what they
wanted and sneaked back to their jobs, leav-
ing the others to fight it out as best they
could. When we have joined a union of
nations “Home Defence” means something
more than our own home —it means as well
the home of our French mate. And the Ger-
man has still his grip on that.
[S. H. Prior, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
The large; public-spirited Victorian
farmer has been exposing his unself-
ishness again. At the recent Mel-
bourne, conference of his new political
union he decided to let the Customs
tariff stay where it is except for a
reduction in the duties on agricultural
implements, manures and other things
required by the primary producer.
Just at present the cocky is getting
3s. a bushel for his wheat, though this
is war-time, and in war-time wheat
usually bounds up. As a matter of
fact, it has bounded up this time ; in
England it is about Bs. per bushel.
The other ss. the cocky doesn’t get
because his market is 12,000 miles
away, and there is a lot of unpleasant-
ness in between. In other words, the
cocky loses ss. or thereabouts a bushel
mostly because he has been a fool
who has been deluded into barracking
for a system which congregated the
consumers at the other end of the
earth. Now cocky is fool enough to
decide that he likes losing ss. so much that
he intends to do all he can to keep the con-
sumers a long way off.
As Premier Peacock candidly put it in his
Budget speech, he “will continue to exercise
the strictest economy” in public affairs. His
expenditure was last year £11,402,715 ; this
year he wall reduce it to £12,025,475. Last
financial year he afflicted the State with a
deficit of £232.815; this year he will make
a strenuous effort to lower’ it to £531,860.
He estimates the coming year’s railway
revenue at the record figure of £6,042,000,
but he will try to make ends meet by not
spending more than £6,422,007. He in-
culcates economy in others by lowering the
railwaymen’s minimum wage from Bs. 6d.
to 9s. per day and by granting certain taller
poppies increases from £24 to £5O a year.
But he seems to have overlooked the State
Excellency. What has that sweated party
done that he shouldn’t- have his salary
reduced from £5OOO to £BOOO a year? Even
that tremendous official,, the State sheriff,
gets an additional ISld. per week. -
It seems only a fair thing that Holman
should go to England as Agent-General, as
he no doubt will shortly. During the next
four years the State has £38,000,000 of debt
falling due, and the whole of it simply has
to be renewed on some terms, because the
State hasn’t any sinking fund except a few
small pieces of paper. The Agent-General
is going to have a delirious time before the
end of 1920; and nobody has earned it
quite so well as Holman.

The Labor party has no argument against con-
scription. It cannot have any. It was the first
party to make it a party battlecry to have conscrip-
tion of men for the defence of our country. I quite
ANOTHER ULTIMATUM.
M. Venizelos has declared that the provisional
Greek Government, of which he is head, will establish
itself at Salonika, collect taxes, raise and equip
troops, and exercise the functions of a duly-consti-
tuted Government.
VENIZELOS (to Constantine) : “You have
your choice; either you use these weapons
or I do.”
agree with Mr. Watt when he says that once we
admit the fact and accept the principle of conscrip-
tion it is a matter of indifference where the men
shall be called upon to fight. It may be a policy of
discretion that we should carry the fight as far as
possible away from our doors.
Next listen to the anti-compulsionist Catts,
who recently meowed from the highest roof-
tiles of patriotism:—
So far as I am personally concerned, it does not
matter whether compulsion is introduced, because
when my country is fighting for its existence I am
prepared to go wherever I am ordered.
Now, when the defeat of the referendum
proposals will .have a tremendous moral
effect, not only in Australia, which doesn’t
matter much, but in Germany and among
the Allies, where it will matter a lot, these
men are fighting against their declared prin-
ciples for all they are worth. Why?

That very deliberate trifler, Premier Pea-
cock, is just now in a state of violent ani-
mosity mingled with aggravated perplexity.
For months he has been “considering” the
feasibility of an amusement tax, and now
Higgs has got in first and that door is shut;
also there are few other openings visible,
and they are small and mostly “blind.
True, there is the tote,
which would yield a
good round sum; but,
then, Peacock to-day is
a timorous, peaceful
person who doesn’t
like taking a bull by
the horns —he would
rather sidle up to the
animal and pat him,
and ask him to be a
good cow. And Ikey
isn’t the sort of cow to
consent.
Some State depart-
ments still make a prac-
tice of encouraging the
foreign toiler at the
expense of the Aus-
tralian. But iti a
brochure printed be-
fore the war by the
Vic. Dept, of Agricul-
ture and still being
issued, the engineer to
the Department says,
in regard to concrete
silos, that “the locally-
made cements are to
be preferred to the im-
ported makes, as they
are all reliable and
slow-setting, while the
i m ported brands,
though very good in
some cases, are not so
in all.” The merits of
Australian cement are
now recognised on all
hands; yet at the out-
break of war there
was still a large pro-
portion of slowgoers
connected with the
various building and
engineering industries
who used or specified
the imported dirt.
High war-freights have
lately given these mis-
guided people a rude push in the right direc-
tion ; and they will never come back if the
Commonwealth Government does its duty.
There was a yarn drifting around a few
weeks ago that Harry Hoyle, N.S.W. Minis-
ter for Railways, was going to be dropped
into a vacant Commissionership. He was a
probable starter for the job, but somehow or
other he has been knocked right back in
the betting and is nowadays quoted at about
100 to 1. But the odds against Minister
for Works Cairn are getting mighty short.
There are one or two members of the Min-
istry for whom something must be done
before the smash comes, and putting them
ou the national pay-sheet seems the easiest
thing to do. Qualifications —oh, talk sense!
What do they matter?
PARALYSIS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Must we, proud kin of those who royally spent
Their life-blood at bleak Anzac, hesitate,
Upon our duty to deliberate?
Or, with a bitter controversy rent,
With ballots, not with banners, be content?
In the hot stress of battle must we wait _
With patriotism palsied by debate,
And let our honor hang- on argument?
Shall deeds that lit the world with noble flames
Be candles meanly quenched, in darkness lost,
Because the hand that lit them feared the cost?
Shall we deliberately their splendor blot?
Shall Pozieres and Anzac be but names
Forgotten —when Australia forgot?
SYDNEY.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
THE CUSTOMS SKYSCRAPER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The conversion of Sydney Customs House into a
skyscraper “adorned with specimens of Hellenic
art” having commenced, a huge signboard has been
stuck up outside the premises announcing that
This Building Is Being Reconstructed by
King O’Malley.
Let us erect it to the happy skies,
A towering box of steel and polished stone,
At which imagination vainly shies,
Blinded from wonders till that hour unknown.
Splash it with fragments of Hellenic art,
Statue and column, capital and cope;
But keep the structure in its nobler part
Like an ornate, upended bar of soap.
Buildings we have, but these, in scorn of pride,
Attempt but lower flights of lift and stair,
Though some Hellenic touches nailed 'Outside,
By way of ornament, we hold as fair.
Some on a slender archway from the street
Raise the square plinth to hoist the weighty lid,
As though some graceful dryad’s shapely feet
Should bear the load of a vast pyramid.
Such play of fancy gives us keen delight.
Huge advertising hoardings crowd the walls;
And, in electric glory through the night,
The puce pill from the highest roof-tree calls.
We have done something to express our thought—
A gentle thought confused, but always keen.
Now, like a speckled chimney-stack o’erwrought
With chaste design, our fancy shall be seen.
Right in the gateway of our crowded port
The tall, amazing thing shall proudly stand,
While wandering tourists with a start and snort
Baulk at its glory with uplifted hand.
Yet, when we gaze upon it as we pass,
On those Greek splashes we shall scarce reflect,
But, thinking of its purpose, curse the ass
H7;o builds a tariff that will not protect.
