SPORTING
NOTIONS
Principal stake winners at A.J.C. Spring-
meeting-: Trautwein (Quinologist and Bursar),
£6086 ; Steitd and Nuttrell (Kilboy), £4597 ; Stead
(Sasanof), £ll9l. Add to this the 2id. that they
won from Ikey, and it makes racing look a good
business —sometimes.
* * * *
The 1016 A.J.C.’s Spring Meeting will
probably be remembered as “Quinologist’s year,”
because a common-looking nag that cost £42 came
to light and won three races in three starts. With
the cash acquired by appropriating the Trial Stakes
and Metropolitan, this animal emerged on the last
day in the Waverley Handicap (If mile). The time
(3min. sJsec.) was nothing to brag about, but on
such a course time meant nothing. The going was
as dull as a sermon. Under pressure Quinologist
could have done a lot better. It was a solidly-run
race, anyhow. The same stable provided another
winner in Bursar, who appropriated the Rand-
wick Plate (two miles), with a brilliant run beneath
9st. 31b., cutting down Carlita and Cagou in the last
bit without great difficulty. And Bursar, don’t forget,
is in the Melbourne Cup, too.
The luck of the game! Quinologist won
the Metropolitan Stakes mainly because a bloomer
was made in not scratching him. His lucky owner,
pubkeeper Trautwein, of Sydney, went to the A.J.C.
office some weeks before the race intending to scratch
both Quinologist and Bursar. Bursar was!eliminated
and then Trautwein got into conversation with a
friend and forgot all about the other nag. A day or
two later Quinologist showed his trainer a wonder-
fully good gallop, and on the strength of this all the
odds that could be got at 100 to 1 were taken. It
is stated round the clubs that Trautwein won £19,000
apart from the stake. Incidentally it is worth
recording that when Bursar downed Carlita in the
w.f.a. event on the last day of the oft-postponed
meeting the stable omitted to invest a bean. The
owner told his friends that Bursar was not ready for
action .just yet, but would be cherry-ripe by Mel-
bourne Cup day.
» * * *
The imported nag and the animal by the
imported sire were at the right end very often
during the A.J.C. and Tattersall s Club meetings at
Kandwiek. In five days 30 races were decided. Of
these 15 were won by the progeny of horses foaled
oversea, six by imported prads, three by Maoriland-
ers. Horses by Australian-bred sires were in front
only five times. There is something wrong some-
where.
* * *
Victoria sent a smart ’chaser over to
Randwick. in Louvre, an imported moke who won
both Steeplechases, and may do something of the
same kind again if given sufficient provocation. He
is only a little chap, but good cross-country horses
are often that way, what has been lost in height
going into diameter. Still, Louvre might have had a
lot of trouble to win on the first day if the Maori-
lander New York hadn’t lost his rider two jumps
from home. This quadruped is wonderfully fast over
the flat, but, Heavens, how he ran down his fences!
* * * *
A racecourse certainty nearly came undone
at Sydney Tattersall’s meeting on Saturday. If ever
there was a good thing on paper it was Rosedell in
the Flying Handicap. The public backed her down
to 5 to 4, and felt as good as gold when the mare
sailed past the distance with the other half-dozen
runners astern. Then the ex-Bananalander Delinacre
came with a wet sail and just failed by a head to
hit the front at the right moment. Inia maintained
her reputation for consistency by running third. That
mare’s performances in her last four starts are worth
putting down: Eosom Handicap, fourth; A.J.C.
Shorts, third; A.J.C. Highweiglit Handicap, first;
City Tatt.’s Flying Handicap, third. #
Ex-bookmaker Teddy Knight owns a
good ’un in the three-year-old filly Roman Queen
(Charlemagne II. —Majesty), which appropriated the
race at Tatt.’s meeting for nags of her age. The
lady was always well up, and won comfortably by
three parts of a length at the end of a mile run in
Imin. 41:}soc. Last season the filly landed a race,
and looks like doing better. Scythion, who was
second, is one of the Earlston tribe, and when a
year younger won his full share of events on subur-
ban and country courses. Roman Queen’s owner has
been racing horses for many years. One of the best
was Roval Purple.
* * * *
Maoriland sent over a weird-looking old
plug to have a shot at the Randwick Steeples this
year, and did well over the deal. This was Okaihau,
a dejected-looking chestnut who, standing in his
stall, seemed to be waiting for a boy to come along
and climb aboard with the meat-basket. But
Okaihau when he gets on to the track bids dull care
begone and grows about 15 years younger. In the
A.J.C. Second Steeple he ran second to the lightly-
weighted Louvre. At Tatt.’s meeting he came out
with rollers on both forelegs, and his tail tied up
out of the way in a knot. It was this tail that
the rrst of the field pursued across the fences and
over the hill. The old nag sprinted off to the
front as if he was in a hurry, and stayed there. In
the last furlong another Maorilander (Troublesome)
got alongside and pushed his head into the lead for
a moment, but the ancient pulled his legs further
underneath and leaped past the post first amid great
applause. Then he wandered back to the paddock
and looked as gloomy as if he had to do it all over
again.
# * * *
“Mujik” : Caulfield Cup, to be run on Satur-
day, looks an open race. Over a dozen horses have been
well backed by people who feel confident about the
harvest. The surest way for a punter to avoid
losing a lot of money is to put only a little on.
Maoriland is going to have a big say with Sasanot
and Bee, but there are other nags at concert-pitch.
On Levando’s running in the Caulfield Stakes he
must be held in great respect. It was said after
last Melbourne Cup that, though he won at Caul-
field, no one really knew how good a horse Levando
was. At Flemington he got badly left, and was
afterwards interfered with when Marculfus and
others fell. But this writer gets back every time
to Sasanof. He is a queer-looking cuss, but is a
galloper right enough. True, there is also Polycrates,
. and a very brilliant moke he is, Nobody need be
. surprised to hear that at some stage of the journey
he was putting up a weighty argument for the
dollars.
* * * *
It isn’t often that Melbourne sees a first-
class youngster winning races thus early in the
season, but the shape and form of llemmon, who
scored his second win in the Debutant Stakes last
Saturday, suggest that he is a topnotcher. Remmon
is a son of Endorus, a handsome English horse that
won the Caulfield Futurity Stakes before he was sent
to the stud. The colt is a fairly-quick beginner and
a resolute finisher.
* * * *
Tlie results of the two classic events on
the first day of V.A.T.C. meeting seemed to discount
the form 'of Kilboy, the A.J.C. Derby winner.
Lavendo defeated the champion 3-year-old brilliantly
in the Caulfield Stakes, and the unexpected Ettefred
was much too good for Wolaroi and Stageland in the
Guineas. Kilboy thus far is proven only to be the
best of the 3-year-olds that ran at Randwick re-
cently. There is no proof that they were not a
merely moderate lot. The Maorilander is certainly
a fine lump of a horse —so lumpish as to be rather
clumsy in a crowd —and he promises to grow into a
champion if he isn’t one already. All the same,
Lavendo seemed to give him 181 b. and an easy beat-
ing without having any the best of the luck on
Saturday.
JOE CHIP, of America, lover of the back-
hand stripe.
From “0.K.,” who has watched more
than 30 Melbourne Cups tor this paper:—
For the past six years the Caulfield Cup winner
-would have been included in anybody’s choice of half
a dozen against the field. This year the race pro-
mises to be crowded with “possibles” unless some-
thing has happened on Wednesday to give a very
commanding show to one or two of the candidates.
The most fancied ones are doubtful g-ood things at
time of writing, and some of the Randwick form that
seemed good enough to swear by is now open to
question. For instance, Sasanof £ot a lot of credit
for beating Woorak over nine furlongs at a difference
of 191 b., but the subsequent running of both nags
in the Craven Plate was disappointing. At the same
time, Potlycrates came under suspicion of having
staled off.' As the runner-up in both the Epsom and
Metrop. the imported horse would have an undeniable
chance for Caulfield Cup. He has nothing more to
carry than in the Metrop., and there is no Quinologist
to heat him. But it is not certain that Polycrates
will be at his best, or second best, next Saturday.
Lavendo may be brilliant enough to win with 9st.
41b. up, which is 301 b. more than he carried to
victory last year; nevertheless the gait of Lavendo
when he was led around the birdcage last Saturday
suggested that he may go amiss at any moment.
Besides, his easy defeat of liilboy is no guarantee that
he can beat such dangerous lightweights and recent
winners as Tcrbane (7st. 21b.), Sasanof (6st. 111 b.).
and Racl Locin (6st. 71b.). The most con-
sistent performers among the kindly-handicapped can-
didates are Bee (Bst. 31b.) and Rael Locin. The
latter is the dark 4-year-old that beat Blague and
Highrock and 16 others in the Toorak Handicap. He
may be a very good horse, and he certainly can be
trusted to run kindly in the hands of a tiny lad. He
is full of health just now, and although his stamina
is unproved the chances are that he can stay 12
furlongs, anyhow. His sire, Delaware, was a notori-
ous plodder. The writer prefers Rael Locin to any-
thing else. A ridiculous name —which appears to be
Lear Nicol spelt backwards —is not the fault of the
poor animal.
* * * *
Hawker’s Boontree and Hamilton’s Bour-
lang had the surprise of their bripf existence in the
South Aus. Derby. They have grown quite used to
beating each other in turn, with Mostyn Owen’s
Marmur waiting on them and sometimes dividing
them. In the Derby Marmur made all the running
for a mile while they waited handy for him to come
back. He came back all right, but Cudmore’s Novka
(Pistol —Guncarriage) came forward, and won as he
liked from Boontree —also a Pistol. Ncvka was bred
in S.A., and this was his first win. The totalisator
takings at the two-days’ meeting were the smallest
for about seven years; but, even so, the S.A.J.O.
was glad to be back on its own course, lately
occupied as a camp. Last year’s Derby was run on
the old course, lent by A.R.C.
“Wart”: By the death of Chiron, im-
ported about six years ago by W. L. Field, of De o-
raine, the Speck loses about the best sire in the
Island. Most of Chiron’s get were sprinters. Desire
was, of course, easily the best, and could move o\ei
a couple of miles when he liked. About the smartest
of the others were Chiron’s Faith, Silver Claim,
Tasman’s Isle, Sweet Lil, Silver Ijake, C liirone,
Friendly Foe, Prince Chiron, Selbago, Tonga mil,
Lady Chiron and Chi Chi, who raced in South Aus.
The main effect of this bunch of names is to illus-
trate the poverty of the racing man’s imagination.
In all Chiron produced 96 winners, who have won
239 races (£18,025). t
“Spreader”: Yes, and Maori land owner
Ormond has run a juniper occasionally, too. Napper
was a scorcher across country while he lasted, lie
blew out through miscalculating the distance at the
onlv jump that stood in the road of the big Auck-
land double, Hurdles and Steeple about five years
ago. He had the first leg in, and the field well
behind him in the second. Sir Tristram and Bollm
were a pair of milers that deserve a word. Both
were descendants of Lady Mersey, Carbines full
sister; but, as the pedigree writers say, "this line
lias been gradually going to the wall.”

TIPS FOR TO-MORROW.
[For Tub Butj.eti.v.]
The headlines say in their shriekv way
On East and West there is hell to pay.
“Thousands of Dead” is the line to-day.
Down in the Balkans no one looks gay.
But there’s one gleam in an outlook grey—
One line of hope in a page of sorrow
Shining before us: “Tips /or To-morrow!

» * * *
Billjim is the sport. The writer of this
paragraph, when down at a transport the other
dav seeing l a mate off, noticed a chap who had just
got through a tearful parting with his mother, beckon
a cobber. “Say, Squeaker,” he remarked, “put this
on Deadbeat for the Flvin’ ; the cow owes me
some,” and he passed over ’arf a quid. What odds
against one of these chaps on arrival at the molten
gates offering to bet the Bloke with a tail two to
one he cannot find the ace?
* * * *
Galloped his last mile, somewhere in
Egypt, Footwear (Ganymede—Footmark), the South
Aus. racehorse. Mostyn Owen, his owner, gave him
to Harry Aldridge, who got good war-work but of
him till a shell found him out.
The Victorian State Parliament im-
poses a tax on betting which leaves the business in
the same scandalous position as the N.S.W. Legisla-
ture left it. Less disgraceful, perhaps, in so far that
the new taxation will extract more revenue than
Holman's Bill to perpetuate Ilcey Mo, yet less defen-
sible because it presses more heavily upon the little
frequent punter in the metropolis than upon the
larger and merely occasional local punter at country
race-meetings. All taxation of the tickets of men
who sell the odds is bound to be passed on to
the punters who buy them, anyhow. The licensed
individual parasites will shorten their prices on a
yearly average in order to spare themselves the tax,
whereas a legalised tote would lengthen the odds and
pay twice as many dividends in most cases. The
Government’s old hypocritical excuse that it dare
not “legalise” the Gambling Evil, not even in the
financial interests of the public hospitals, is clean
blown out by its tax on bookmakers’ tickets. The
excuse that the tote would increase the Gambling
Evil remains as false and foolish as before. Coming
from the lies of well-informed Parliamentarians it
was always an audacious lie. Uttered by unin-
formed wowsers it was an honest contradiction of
the truth. How the wowsers are going to square
their conscientious objections to Government sanc-
tion of betting with their acceptance of Peacock's
new policy the Lord only knows. Perhaps the
triumphant Association of Federated Bookmakers can
offer a suggestion? #
They catch ’em young for the nag-pilot
industry. When Little Titch (100yds. behind) won
a pony trot at Parkes (N.S.W.) lately from half a
dozen others it was discovered that the rider, Jack
Palmer, had been alive six years and nine months.
* * * *
Dave Smith went close to taking the k.o.
from George Chip’s brother Joe at Sydney Stadium
on Saturday—much closer than lie cared about.
Joseph is a puncher, and he punched David good
and hard, not to say painfully. In the twelfth
round the Yank administered two wallops each of
which sent David to rest for nine and handed oyer
another which just missed doing the trick. Smith
was down and. spread out when the gong beat the
count by one. It was a torrid round. But Smith
came back like a giant refreshed, and at the end
of the disturbance was pronounced to be the winner
on points. It was a good, vigorous scrap, and the
boxer won because the fighter failed to wooden him.
# * * *
Mick King, one-time middleweight cham-
pion boxer of Australia, left for America a week or
two ago without any brass-band music. Over yonder
he will come across other Australian pugs who within
the past few months have felt the lure of the game
pulling them to the U.S.A., though the money for
second-raters there is not nearly as big as it is here.
* * * *
Cricket is still being played around Syd-
ney, but not much more interest is taken in it than
in marbles. On Saturday the opening first-grade
matches were deplorably dull. North Sydney, former
holders of the premiership, managed to rush up a
scratch team one short against Petersham. In the
first innings it made 14 runs. Five players were
guilty of 0. Forty-six balls completed the damage.
Scott’s average was 6 for 7. Petersham declared
with 5 wickets for 143, and the others went on for
a second knock. This time Punch remembered his
name, and had 47 on when the smack was adjourned.
Yet there is one good feature about the general
calamitousness. The youngsters arc getting a chance.
Charlie Ivellaway, international cricketer
from Glebe (Sydnev), who has been thrice wounded,
found out the 'other day that he had also been deco-
rated with the Military Cross.
»* * *
H. A. Merewetlier, dead at Sydney, played
for N. S. Wales against Victoria in the first inter-
state tennis matches in ’B3. He was a great smasher,
and an opponent to be dreaded in the games of a
quarter-century ago. The tennis-playing Merewethers
of that time consisted of two sisters and six brothers.

“Mynn”: Stumped by Time —Adam Hope,
one of' the oldest of Australian cricketers. Coming
to Australia 60 years ago, he played for Victoria
against Ma at Sydney in Feb., ’63, accumulating
8 and 2 in a match in which the highest individual
Victorian score was 25. The veteran’s death recalls
the most glorious row connected with that match.
Vic.’s umpire gave S. Jones, one of the N.S.W.
men, run out. Ma’s umpire said that he had called
“over” before the bails were removed, and Jones,
like Geora-e Oiffen at a later date, refused to be
regarded as dead. The crowd rushed
and the Victorians retired to their hotel, where much
whisky assisted discussion of the positio ;' ontim „,
outcome was that Jones was allowed to
batting next day, but “Jerry” Marshall, the
lUackham of his time, and Greaves, anothe P
nent Victorian, had the sulks so fiadlvthat they
refused to continue and the match was tmisl
out them. Victoria was badly beaten in the games
and when the dispute was referred to the M
Cricket Club she received another smack in tne eye.
Ma and Vic. refused to speak for some time attei
this incident. So there was no match m 64^

* * * *
American motor-cars are pouring into
Australia in thousands, and as these cars come shod
with tyres, it means a considerable loss to Australian
rubber manufacturers. They have _ little chance of
fitting tyres to the new cars, seeing that only &
nominal sum is allowed off the price of a car which
p imported with bare rims. In the case ot one
fitted with 815 x 105 m.m. tyres, only £ll Is. per
set was offered an Australian agent as a rebate it
it was shipped in a tyreless state. The public is
the loser, because the tyres fitted to most ears
coming into this country are considerably lighter
than is deemed essential for our roads even 2o
per cent, lighter not only in covers but in tubes. In
the case quoted above the four covers weighed
671 b. and the tubes 91b., as against 821 b. and 141 b.
respectively for Australian Dunlops. A difference
of 201 b. of rubber and fabric represents a lot of
miles. The Australian manufacturer does not put
more material into his tyres for philanthropic
reasons, but because it is essential to his trade.
* * * *
This season’s racing on Sydney Harbor
has been remarkable for the poor display made by
Kismet. During the previous two or three years the
craft was invincible. So far this season one solitary
fifth place is the old boat’s share. Quibree heads
the list.
* * * *
“Bias” : Some of the Sydney bonding clubs
have got so large that selection of a pennant team
makes a heart-searching problem. In clubs with 200
members on the active list 8 per cent, play in the
pennant games and 92 per cent, watch them monopo-
lising uie rest of tne green. In some of the clubs
the selection committee has hoisted the white flag'
and refused to select. Then the clubs have had to
resort to the system of ballot, and while this ensures
that quite a lot of “decent chaps” are picked, it often
enough results in strong players getting the cold
shoulder. The trouble is that the bowling enthusiast
wants to play, while the average cricket enthusiast
is willing to look on and see somebody else play.
* * * *
Sydney Grammar scooped the pool in N. S.
Wales Great Public School rifle-shooting champion-
ships at Sydney. It won the Rawson Cup (200yds
and 500yds.), Schools’ Challenge Shield (500yds.-and
600yds.), Buchanan Cup (800yds.), cadet match
(200yds. and 300yds.), and second-grade premiership.
Grammar School, therefore, keeps a firm grip on the
championships it gathered in 1915.
THE QUICK AND THE DEAD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Don’t touch it,” said the knowing one to me—
We’d met at a suburban racing-track.
He, kind soul, said he’d tell me what to back--
“Don’t touch it, man—it’s dead, that bloomin’ gee!
“D-E-A-D —a stiff ’un! Take my tip,
Leave it alone. If you’ll give me your brass,
I’ll put it on a trier of some class —
A cert. 1 know the owner! It’s a snip!”
And so I gave him, wondering- the while,
Two pounds. “Don’t move,” he counselled,, “and
don’t talk;
Just watch the horse I back win in a walk ;
When 1 bring back your divvy you will smile.”
lie disappeared. What were his feelings when
The race was run? For, marvellous to say,
The one he said was dead won all the way.
I'd never seen a miracle till then.
Did he, like all the punters round, evince
Signs of great gladness, cheer with might and main,
Because that gee had come to life again?
I’d love to know—l haven’t seen him since!
VICTORIA.
J. MUGG.

Gear Bulletin, — In 1913-14, just before
the Mad Dog of Europe got oil the chain,
Australia’s seven Governments spent
£69,000,000; for 1915-16 their total expen-
diture, apart from the war, will be
£81,000,000. In 1913-14 the Federal Gov-
ernment scraped along with a peace expen-
diture of £23,161,000; for 1915-16 it cannot
manage (leaving the war out of account)
with less than £32,585,000. The total annual
expenditure of our numerous Governments
at present is about £180,000,000. What I
want to know is: What is going to happen
when the false prosperity, engendered by the
fact that £80,000,000 of borrowed money is
now being spent on defence, vanishes like
the baseless fabric of a dream? —Yours,
Copy Boy.

“Stargazer” : I don’t think the advertising
experts ever lavished so many endearing
adjectives on a comedy as were thrown away
on “A Full House” before its first appear-
ance at Melbourne lioyal. After its disappear-
ance that evening the patrons provided the
adjectives. They were not endearing. One
infuriated party from the stalls asked me a
hot, indignant question. “What,” said he,
“would happen to the back of the head of a
restaurant-keeper who advertised with fic-
titious gaiety that on a fixed date he was
going to give buyers of seven-and-sixpenny
tickets the brightest and most delightful
dinner that love, ability and cash could
produce, and then, when his tables were
crowded with paying guests, provided noth-
ing but three courses of debilitated and
senile eggs?” The comparison is apt
and perfectly just. “A Full House”
is a three-course meal of vitiated eggs.
It is the sort of play that could not
continue for five minutes if every character
in it were not utterly insane, and when all
was over I had the feeling of satiety one
might expect to be afflicted with after staring
for two hours through the pickets surround-
ing a madhouse, having paid 6s. for the
privilege.
“ J.F.H.” from London :
Among the many things the Kaiser has strewn
about is the London dramatic season. Nobody can
say now where it begins or ends. Before Bill broke
loose it always came to a close at the end of July,
and the papers then reviewed its results. Some of
them through force of habit have continued the old
custom while admitting that the war has made a
hash of times and seasons. The Daily Telegraph,
the morning paper that gives the greatest space , to
the theatres, alleges that “the plays of the past
season have been vastly better than we had a right
to expect,”' and singles out for special praise Dot
Bcucicault’s production of Somerset Maugham’s
“Caroline” —'“a brilliant piece of light comedy.”
The Referee declares that, in spite of eroak-
ings and taxes and the absence of young men at
the Front, the theatre has not only kept, going but
has actually advanced in art, usefulness and popu-
larity. It dwells on “The Tiger’s Cub,” the full-
blooded Canadian melodrama of primeval passions,
in which Madge Titheradge made a hit. The season
was remarkable, too, for four pronounced Anglo-
American successes —“Peg o’ My Heart,” which
holds the record for war-time runs; H. B. Irving’s
“The Barton Mystery”; Doris Keane’s “Romance”/;
and the amusing “Daddy Longlegs,” written by
Mark Twain’s lately-deceased daughter, Joan Webster.
“For war-time an amazingly fruitful season” 'is
the verdict of the Referee.
“Yarra Ben”: All eyes at the hash-house
breakfast-table were turned on the new arri-
val as he took his seat. He was every inch
a mummer, from the property spats on his
boots to the grease-paint on his coat-collar.
He ran his fingers through his well oiled
mane, and after giving his order to the
waitress he fixed the young man opposite
with his eye. “This is the sixth boarding-
house in this city that has sheltered me
during as many weeks,” he confided in a
stage whisper. The youth did not seem to
be impressed; he resumed his chop and
drawled, “Why?”. The tragedian seemed non-
plussed for a second or two by the question.
Then, with a shrug that sent his padded
shoulders up to his ears, he answered: “I
have the-a cursed-a wanderlust in my blood !”
and started on his porridge.
Exit Major D’Arcy Edwardes, only son of
the late George Edwardes, of London
Gaiety. Young Edwardes was educated at
Eton and Sandhurst, joined the Royal Dra-
goons in 1907 and, after service in India and
S’Africa, went to France in 1914. At the
time of his death he was in command of a
battalion of the Welsh Regiment.
Clyde Meynell assures London inter-
viewers that things theatrical in Australia
are wonderfully well, thank you, consider-
ing. J.C.W., Ltd., had, however, called off
its South African branch because “we found
it needed more attention on the spot than
we could give it.”
Australian composer and pianist Percy
Grainger has provided something to head
the list of “novelties” to be presented at the
next series of Promenade Concerts at London
Queen’s Hall. The latest thing he has
turned out he calls “Clog Dance: Handel in
the Street” —an enigmatic title that has set
the cockneys wondering. The Star sug-
gests that it ought to have a secondary ex-
planatory title such as “Or Have a Banana.”
“O. Blin” : Fred Penley. son of the late
W.S., of “Charley’s Aunt,” and a member
of the Whip Co. which appeared in Australia
a few years ago, is a guest of the Queen of
Holland. Fred held a commission in Wim
ston Churchill’s brave but immaterial Naval
Contingent which tried to stiffen the defence
of Antwerp. In the subsequent chaos he
and a number of others escaped into Hol-
land and were promptly wired-in for safe
keeping at Groningen. Recently the monot-
ony of his internment was lightened. Jby. the
allowance of a few days’ leave on parole in
England—a‘ special concession granted on
account of illness.
STANZAS COMPOSED AFTER A BOUT WITH
CULTURE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The audience was sad to see,
Its ties “made up,” its pants not pressed ;
Its women were, it seemed to me,
In cretonne curtains mostly dressed;
And in the intervals no bioke
Went from his seat to have a smoke.
The play had cobwebs in its hair,
The plot a wreath of whiskers wore,
Its puppets came on, pair by pair,
And talked and sighed, and talked some more ;
In slippered feet the dull-eyed cast
Went shuffling through the moss-grown past.
I snorted loud in righteous scorn,
And went home with an aching head.
I seized my papers in the morn
To find out what the critics said.
It was, both rags informed me, a
“Typical repertory play” !
VICTORIA.

and Stimulated Its Growth by a
Simple Home Process.
She Tells How She Did It.

her grey hair by a simple home process,
made the following statement: “Any lady
or gentleman can darken their grey or faded
hair, stimulate its growth and make it soft
and glossy with this simple recipe, which
they can mix at home. To a half-pint of water
add loz. of bay rum and 1 small box of
Orlex Compound. These ingredients can
be purchased at any chemist’s at very little
cost. Apply to the hair every other day
until the grey hair is darkened sufficiently.
This mixture relieves itching and is excel-
lent for dandruff. It does not stain the
scalp, is not sticky or greasy and does not
rub off. It will make a grey-haired person
look 10 to 20 years younger.”— Advt. [3

October 15 was the centenary of the
birth of old Jock Robertson, five times Pre-
mier of X. S. Wales. He died 25 years
ago.
The Victorian Government is hitting up
the bookmakers for some of the cash it
wants. The scheme is on the lines followed
in N. S. Wales, and Peacock reckons on
thereby raising about £70.000. The tax
ranges from £5O on licenses issued for the
V.R.C. grandstand enclosure to £5 on
licenses for the small fry at outside meet-
ings. Also betting tickets are to bear a
stamp. The howl from Ike has. of course,
been loud and long, but no one pays much
attention to him. except those people who
wonder why the < Jovernment objects to
making a clean job of it and giving the
tote an innings. The Government’s explana-
tion is that a bill to legalise the machine
“could not be passed this session.” Well,
there was last session, and Peacock knew
then that he was hard up. And there will
be next session and a Peacock more busted
than ever.
lie’s as sincere as a truthful child.
But beware, thou who work injustice.
Beware if you make him wild.
For thou his anger, fierce and Idaok,
Would shame a wounded tiger
Or a snake with a broken back.

NO MORE FAT PEOPLE.
Most fat people seem to think that the
only way to take off fat is to use dangerous
drugs, follow a starvation diet or take stren-
uous exercise. But all this is nonsense, and.
thanks to the recent discovery of an eminent
scientist, thousands of men and women are
taking off several pounds of useless fat a
week by simply rubbing on to the fat part—
hips, arms, chin or abdomen—a simple lotion
made by pouring a cup of hot water over a
dram of quassia chips; let it stand for a
minute, then strain through a cloth and add
3oz. of Cirola Bark Extract. Pour the mix-
ture into a bottle and apply night and morn-
ing, rubbing it in with the hands for about
10 minutes, using a circular motion, and tin*
fat will almost seem to melt away before
your very eyes. The ingredients, which are
inexpensive, may be had of any chemist, and
the results to be obtained are really remark-
able. — Advt.

IN
BANANA LAND
Cauc-us has handed Harry Coyne £lOOO a
year and the portfolio of Railways, vice
Adamson, who resigned because he refused
to play Germany’s game by keeping silent
on the conscription question. Coyne spruiks
for Warrego in the Assembly, and was the
Napoleon of the tramway strike of 1912.
Until lately he has been Chairman of Com-
mittees, which dignified job has turned the
rabid strike-leader into as moderate, diffi-
dent and hardworking a politician as
Bananaland owns.
Costs having wrangled at Melbourne over
the matter for three weeks and fallen back
exhausted, the High Court had sufficiently
recovered by the beginning of the week to
announce that it intended to give judgment
in favor of the State in the action Duncan
and Trotman v. Bananaland. This is the
case that was to decide the validity of the
legislation and proclamations of the State
Government forbidding the passage of stock
across the border, and the judgment gives
another wallop to section 92 of the Constitu-
tion, which many people thought secured
Freetrade between the States. In a way it
was another “wheat case,” with this dift'er-
eence, that the N.S.W. wheat was taken over
by the Crown and became its absolute pro-
perty, while in Bananaland the Government,
while proclaiming itself trustee of the stock,
left the stock proprietors’ ownership allegedly
unaltered. The case in its original form was
a claim for damages by the owners of Moora-
berie station, who wanted damages for hav-
ing been obliged to sell various animals at
from £7 to £ll in Bananaland when they
eould have got £l2 to £l9 for them at Adel-
aide. The Commonwealth Government in-
tervened, and a special case was stated In-
justice Isaacs for the High Court, this
being practically a contest between the State
and Federal authorities. And the Federa-
tion has been outed. It was not very clear
whether the prohibition of stock crossing the
border rested on the Meat for Imperial Uses
Act or the Sugar Acquisition Act. But it
doesn’t matter, anyhow. Australia is one
country and has seven Governments, and sec-
tion 92 has another large gaping hole. And
the need to put the Constitution in the pot
is greater than ever.
“Richmond”: Seems to me that artesian
bores give a bigger flow during the night-
time than they do through the day. Where
the water is just trickling over the top of
the bore-head it is the custom to place a
flat|stOne over part of the casing to make the
watfr shoot out clear, and I have repeatedly
noticed in the morning that the stone has
been pushed off as if a greater flow had come
up during the night. These instances have
occurred where it has been impossible for
stock to get to the bore-head and elbow the
stone away.
“Dead Finish”: On Abingdon station, in
the Gulf country, Jim Harvey, a drover, who
was to lift some hornies, lost a couple of his
horses. He went out one morning looking
for them and returned in two and a half
days with the missing nags. During that
period he bad had nothing to eat, but water
was plentiful. It was all forest country,
with gravelly ridges, which made it hard for
tracking. This is looked on as a pretty good
performance out here, but perhaps they can
do it better in Melbourne.
Treasurer Theodore declares that com-
pulsory service is the greatest fraud ever
foisted on Democracy. He proposes to
sweep away every vestige of the cursed
thing from his own Department. In future
nobody will be compelled to furnish him
•with any money. It will be left entirely to
the Fatman to say whether he will pay land
and income taxes. Filielly, the Minister of
Justice, who agrees with Theodore, has also
issued instructions that no compulsion is to
be used with regard to the collection of
fines. The drunk and disorderly having-
pleaded guilty, the P.M. has received in-
structions to say to the prisoner: "As to a
fine, I’ll leave it to you.” The same Demo-
cratic policy is to be annlied by brother
Lennon in the Stock and Agriculture De-
partment. Nobody is to be compelled to
destroy either rabbits or prickly-pear. Bro-
ther Lennon has decided that, as the volun-
tary principle is quite good enough to rely
upon to carrv reinforcements to save Ins
son at the Front, it is more than good
enough to rely upon to save people from the
spread of prickly-pear and rabbits. Brother
Hardacre, of the Education Department, has
a Bill in hand to abolish compulsory
attendance at school: and when Bill Hamil

[S. H. Prior, 214 George-street, Sydney.]
Said Finlayson, the “anti” crank: —
The demand now is for reinforcements to back up
the men at the Front, and to assure them that they
shall have whatever support is necessary. We must
see that those men are adequately supported. The
only honest recruiting agent is the anti-conscription-
ist because, being opposed to forcing men, if he
tells them that they should go —as I believe it is
their duty to do—he does everything ’ possible to
induce them to go.
The moral of which preposterous utterance
is that the way to persuade an unwilling
man to enlist is to tell him he needn’t, and
the surest way of procuring the necessary
adequate reinforcements is by loudly
declaring that it isn’t anybody’s duty to do
his duty by his country if he doesn’t want
to do it. Some men are born anti; some
achieve anti-ism through family influence;
others have it forced upon them by the
1.W.W.,j operating through the P.L.L. or
P.L.C. Finlayson was born that way, per-
haps.
[Edmund Fisher, Bulletin branch office, Mel-
bourne.]
John Adamson is getting a fine reward
for his lifelong service for Labor. Actually
he has had to leave the Cabinet for being
a good Australian. Last week, in Bunda-
berg, whilst walking from a conscription
meeting, an infuriated opponent crept be-
hind him and knocked him unconscious, a
typical “anti” argument which cost the
responsible coward two months’ hard.
“Dead Finish”: North Queenslanders
have great difficulty in keeping white ants
out of ornamental and fruit trees. The fol-
lowing is the best preventive. Take a few
pieces of pine board, soak in arsenic water,
and bury just under the ground near the
tree. The ant sharpens his beak on this
board and throws a seven. His cobbers,
being Cannibals, try their teeth on the
slaughtered, and also pass put, and the
white hordes do not reach the tree. Dig-
ging dry arsenic into the ground acts well,
but is not as reliable. Pouring arsenic
water around spells ruin.
“Fitzroy": One species of Bananaland
brigand is feeling uneasy. This is the free-
booter who imports and sells seeds. For
years he has been getting consignments of
rubbish which were supposed to produce
grain and artificial grasses. The stuff was
unloaded on to the simple cocky, who
ploughed, harrowed and sowed, and then
looked to the future with hope. When
nothing came up he cursed the climate.
When lie was rewarded with a crop of weeds
he damned the soil. Half a century late
came the Pure Seeds Act, and Bananaland
is lucky enough to have it administered by a
man who takes an interest in his job. All
imported seed is these days bailed up at .port
of entry, and has to prove, its respectability.
Now the storekeeper who retails seed to the
small farmer is also receiving attention.
An official of the Department has been tour-
ing the country districts. When he asked
for seed for sowing he got it—at top price.
When he came out in his true colors and a
prosecution loomed in the distance, the stuff
immediately became birds’ seed. At one
store there were about three tons of “birds’
seed.” Another firm, in order to prove that
the shopman had sold birds’ seed by mistake,
sent down to the Agricultural Department a
sample of “pure seed.” The firm’s lawyer
announced this fact with all the confidence of
securing the final trick. “What do you think
of that?” asked the Bench. “Well,” said the
prosecution, “they had better plead guilty.
The seed they sent down was worse than the
tyther lot.”
Drifting Dreams.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thin black masts on a pale gold sky....
Could we tether the dream that goes drift-
ing by
I would tie white sails to those thin black
masts
And hie to an island where dreaming lasts,
Over the red of the falling sun
Over the ash of a day that’s done.
Green fields, lush on a burning day ;
Blue air precious with scent of hay ;
Could we keep the peace of the musky wood
And make it part of a constant mood.
I think we could smother all tears away
Under the piles of the new-mown hay.
A sunny garden a-foam with bloom ;
Roses rioting there for room :
Lush green fields where the shadows lie;
Thin black masts on a pale gold sky....
But never a ravelled rope, that I
Might tether the dreams that go drifting by !
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, October 14, 1916.
My Dear Thargomjndah, —
Our patriotic whim this week took the
form of laying the foundations of a league
to promote the sale of Australian-made
goods, or in their absence goods manufac-
tured elsewhere in thy Empire. Lady Goold-
Adams laid the corner-stone of the scheme
well and truly. “The Austral ian-Bntish
League" was the inscription.
A "Soldiers’ Queen” fete enlivened Vic-
toria Barracks tins afternoon. There were
no complaints. Spring girls were early on
the job, as usual, and what they don’t know
about trawling pockets on behalf of the
Boys in Brown their grandmas couldn’t
teach them. The catch went to the benefit
of the Returned Soldiers’ Residential Club.
That reminds me that many well-wishers
felt sore on hearing that only members of
the Returned Soldiers' Association are to
be allowed to use the Club. Women who
worked hard for its establishment fully
expected that any Australian lad home from
the battle line would be welcomed, and when
a whisper escaped a member of the R.S.A
that only men of “strong moral character”
were to be allowed to enjoy its hearthstone
even the most conservative of the Ladies’
Committee reached for her hat-dagger. I’ve
been making breathless inquiries as to the
exact brand of "moral” character that was
demanded of Billzac prior to his rushes at
Gallipoli and Pozieres, but nobody seems to
have the information handy.
The sth Light Horse Comforts Fund has
opened a tea-room as an auxiliary to its
kitchen. Pretty girls in pink frocks serve
the weary and hungry with rosebud smiles
and hand-painted cakes. Cash results are
spent on comforts for the Fifth.
The Queensland Art Society held its 28th
annual exhibition this week. Minister for
Education Hardacre uncorked the show, an
unusually interesting one, on Wednesday
afternoon. Lister Lister sent from Sydney
two water-colors, one a bush scene that
makes you long to shoulder a swag and pad
it out of town, and the other a sample of
the sand dunes at Narrabeen. The sand
looks as though it would trickle through
your fingers. James Crisp, another Sydney
skier, is represented by a flock of his beauti-
ful Australian birds. Queensland’s reputa-
tion is maintained by Oscar Fristrom.
Lancaster, Garnet Agnew, Salvana and
Mrs. Grant, and by Harvey’s wood carving
plaster figuring and oxidised silver plaques.
During the pei’formance of “La Mascotte"
one night this week a hen, appearing in the
farmyard scene, ostentatiously laid an egg
on the stage. Nobody took the spiteful
thing’s tip. On the contrary, bouquets were
plentiful until to-night, when the season
closed.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.

H. P. STURGIS, W. R. MEWBURN, S. R. LIVING STONE-LE ARMONTH, Directors.
A. C. WILLIS, Manager. IV. A. LAING, Accountant.
London, 11th July, 1916.
Auditors Certificate and Report.
In accordance with the provisions of section 113 of the Companies (Consolidation) Act 1908, we
report to the shareholders as follows:
We have examined the above balance-sheet with the books at head office, and have had access to the
branch accounts transmitted to the head office from Australia and New Zealand. We have verified the
correctness of the cash, money at call and short notice, investments and the bills of exchange held
in London, and, having obtained all the information and explanations we have required, we are of
opinion that such balance-sheet is properly drawn up so as to exhibit a true and correct view of the
state of the company’s affairs according to the best of our information and the explanations given to us,
and as shown by the books of the company and the said branch accounts.
WHINNEY, SMITH AND WHINNEY, Chartered Accountants, Auditors.
London, 11th July, 1916.

ESTABLISHED OVER 80 YEARS.
A Mr. J. Burns, one of Melbourne’s feder-
ated ironworkers, has thought of an inverted
‘gag” to suit the young shirkers of to-day.
When their inquisitive offspring, in time to
come, cry “Daddy, what did you do for your
country in 1916?” the proud shirker shall
answer : “My boy, I stayed in Australia to
defend its freedom.” (Loud cheers and much
stamping of cold feet.)
The heretical idea that if a State runs
railways it might also run steamers is
making progress amongst the Speck’s Lib-
erals. The other day the Assembly declared
in favor of a State-owned line of steamers
by 15 to six on a snap division, and the
majority included two Libs., Blyth and
Sadler.
1 When Austin Saqui won the Melbourne
Cup with Warrior in ’O9, he visited England
and brought back his son, John T. Saqui.
The youngster ran a cigar shop, made a
book and became a speculator on ’Change,
but found racing paid better. About 10
years ago he was set upon by a “push” and
so severely injured that he developed a
mental malady. But his bodily health kept
fairly good until lately, and he died only
the other day. He was 62.
At the recent pastoral and agricultural
show at Henty (N.S.W.) five shillings was
offered for the best 41b. of German sausage.
The Bulletin thinks this ought to be
stopped. We’ve got no right to kill them
merely for the sake of winning five shillings.
New definition from the Front: —
Raid: An attack that doesn’t come off.
Although there are a dozen industries
howling loudly for Protection, the Aus-
tralian Government has turned a deaf
ear to all bar one—the great wowse indus-
try. Treasurer Higgs proposes to foster the
manufacture of gloom by means of a stiff tax
upon amusements.
“Inver G.”: Our blockading squadrons
are laughing at themselves as they sweep
the wild North Sea. It’s the dyes again.
In a letter recently read from an officer on
the Australia it is mentioned that when
the wardroom wants to look its smartest
nowadays it puts on whatever pre-war
clobber remains in its sea chest. “These
new blue serges,” he writes, “are the limit.
They look top-hole when they come from
the tailor, and the material in them is as
good as ever. But the dyes! Lord, you
ought to have seen some of our chaps after
a bit of North Sea spray had got on to their
latest togs. The salt turned the blue dye
into patches of purples, reds, greys and all
sorts of in-between tints.”
The Customs Department the other day
dropped on the captain, chief officer and chief
engineer of a trader between South Africa
and Westralia, and the ensuing proceedings
at court interested them to the tune of £7O.
Making a false declaration as to dutiable
goods was the charge, the goods being ostrich
feathers, silk and tobacco. Up to this the
Customs had not gone in hot pursuit of the
revenue lost through ships’ officers turning
smugglers; but those good days are over, and
the poor seafarer will find the burdens of
his life harder to bear than ever. This war
is a curse.
In the Court cases arising out of recent
troubles between the members of the Engine-
drivers and Firemen’s Union and the Broken
Hill Prop, at Newcastle, 11. A. Mitchell
appeared for the company. That fact was
due to a bad habit, common to manv
unions, of ill-using capable officials. Mitcheil
was for very many years secretary of the
Union he now appeared against. When the
organisation first took shape he was the
Union. His energy was responsible for get-
ting the men in ; he bluffed, negotiated and
argued hundreds of agreements out of em-
ployers covering the conditions of a widely-
scattered craft. Probably no man knows
the industrial laws better, or could fight an
odds-against battle with more success. Yet
his reward was a meagre salary and sus-
picion from the men he had benefited. At
one time some of them received a mere 30s.
a week, and they are now amongst the bet-
ter paid of the workers. Finally intrigue
robbed him of his job—and then the
Broken Hill Prop, jumped in and secured a
man with the particular ability it wanted to
deal with its industrial work. It is a pretty
tough job for a Union to go into court with
a yarn that an employer is a skinflint who
underpays his men when there is on view a
man whom it pays nearly twice as much as
the Union reckoned a fair thing for doing
exactly the same kind of work.
The way Billjim humps his bluey through
the army:—
I d’know how it is, but some way, without once
getting into trouble, I’ve been in most units of
the A.I.F. during 24 months’ tedious scrapping. I
left the Infantry to enlist in the Light Horse,
drifted thence to Records, from Records got trans-
ferred to the A.S.C. and thence to the Camel Corps.
Next, because I wanted to do a bit for France, from
Camels to my present Artillery unit. I hope to try
Engineers, Pioneers and Aero in turn. I’ve had all
sorts of rank. Chucking a star in Artillery I rose
from trooper to full corporal of Light Horse; then
to lance-sergeant. In my next move I dropped
back to corporal, and jumped to S.M. Back to
corporal again I worked up to W.O. At present
I m back to corporal. And, bless you, mine’s not
a singular instance. In my old unit is a trooper
who resigned a captaincy; and I could name a
captain and lieutenant of other arms who did the
same to be in the first push and are now respectively
sergeant and lance-corporal. On the other hand,
one of my ex-corporals, who has just made the
stunning discovery that war has been declared,
lobbed here last week as my lieutenant. It paid
him all right to let his feet thaw gradually— very
gradually.
Thomas Strachan, who died lately at
Petersham (N.S.W.), was formerly con-
nected with IL G. Dun and Co., and
was one of the pioneers of “trade protec-
tion” in Australia. But not the pioneer.
In the early ’6o’s, 20 years before Mr.
Strachan arrived, some Melbourne merchants
and traders formed themselves into a Trade
Protection Society, of which Captain Rad-
cliffe, of McClure, Valentine and Co., was
chairman and Joseph B. Coombes secretary.
After a while Mr. Coombes took over the
concern, and with two others, trading as
Coombes, Ledwell and Ellis, carried it on for
many years.

“Eat to live,” but when your digestion is faulty,
eating causes your life to be a misery. To properly
nourish your body your food must be thoroughly
digested and its various constituents pass to the

is not done the food does you no good, but rather
harm, for it lies in your stomach, causing poison-
ous gases and disorders of the stomach —causes
constipation, which means that the food, instead

and the poisonous matter is absorbed into the
system. All this can be avoided by taking a
course of Chamberlain’s Tablets. They aid diges-

liver. They eliminate all impurities and' tone the

A disordered stomach may cause no end of

Tablets.
If you were able to live just right all the time
there would be little need for medicine, less need
for doctors. But some little thing is constantly
coming along to put us Out of balance—-exposure,
change of diet or drinking water, irregular meals
are little things that will at times upset the
best of us. Nothing particular the matter, but we
don’t feel good. At such times the wise among
us will take a few doses of Chamberlain’s Tablets
with certain knowledge that they will put us
right, right away.
Many women habitually suffer from headaches

For indigestion, biliousness, constipation, dis-
orders of the stomach and liver there is nothing

taken.
Back up a sewer and you will poison the whole
neighborhood, clog up the liver and bowels and
your system is full of undigested food, which

ache, bad breath, yellow skin and everything that

Much ill-health is due to impaired digestion. If

It is not natural to have that tired feeling day

There is no better medicine made for all disorders

To suffer with fortitude is
heroic, but to suffer needlessly
is idiotic. While most sufferers
regard pain as due to the
dispensation of Providence,
doctors see in it a certain
neglect—the neglect of simple
rules of health.
OF all rheumatic afflictions acute lumbago is the
most painful. It arises from a gradual accumu-
lation of excess uric acid in the system ; the muscles of
the lower back become impregnated until the muscu-
lar fibres stiffen and every movement causes agony.
The simple habit of taking Kruschen Salts every
morning cleanses the system of excess uric acid, braces
the nerves and gives to the user the daily enjoyment
of natural vigorous health. Get the Kruschen habit.

The Inky Way.
Perth pressmen have some reason to
throw chests on account of their vigorous
little Club, now a couple of years old.
Started in a basement on nothing more
than the determination of a few inky
waysters to have a club, and while
the membership was nil, the optimists
bought (on bills) £2OO worth of furniture
and a billiard-table costing about £l2O, and
the Club set out on a compulsorily teetotal
basis. The license to sell spirituous joys
came later, and the pressmen commenced
earnestly to drink the Club into solvency.
In the early days receipts wouldn’t run to
a steward, so the committee drew up a
roster for voluntary service. Then there
was seen the leader-writer bearing on a
salver a “beer with a dash” to a junior
reporter and looking keenly for the cub’s
sixpence. It was found necessary to admit
non-pressman members, and the Club grew
quickly out of its dungeon cells, moving to
a new building in the best part of St.
George’s Terrace, the one thoroughfare of
which Perth can boast. Here the Club
takes up the whole of the second floor and
accommodates four resident members. At
present the receipts run to something like
£3OO a month, and if the fell breath of the
wowser does not blight its young existence,
the Club will owe not any man by the end
of the current year.
* * * *
Unfit for active service, “Billy” Moore,
journalist, dramatist (more or less) and the
most eminent Melbourne Bohemian of recent
times, has for the past two years been work •
ing in England with the Army Service
Corps. In the fulness of time he was made
a sergeant-major, and a month or two back
was asked for a report on a branch of his
work.. “Billy” put his soul into that report.
It bristled with epigrams, apt allusions and
striking illustrations, its thoughtfulness
being equalled only by its elegance of dic-
tion. Its author posted the precious docu-
ment and waited. The answer came in a
few days :—■
Dear Sir, —It is not in accordance with military
etiquette to address a superior officer as “Dear Sir.”
Those who remember Billy in Melbourne
are puzzled to know how it is that his
superior officer was not addressed as “Dear
old chappie.”
Alan Prichard, who has given up his
Argus job of mining man to go into khaki,
was offered a farewell cigar at the Melbourne
Stock Exchange Club the other evening. The
mining editor has to work between two con-
tending hosts of bulls and bears, and he can’t
suit both; yet a really beautiful assortment
of felicitations has been handed Prichard
since his enlistment.
* * * *
George Wilson Hall, the old pressman and
politician who died the other day, was re-
sponsible for one great scoop. He supplied
the Age with the first account of the cap-
ture of Ned Kelly. Hall used to edit the
Benalla Standard, in the Kelly country.
* * * *
“0.K.”: One of the most fiery patriots
I’ve met is W. E. Williams, who, in the
piping times of peace, was on the business
side of a Melbourne weekly. At the time
of the Boer war he abandoned a comfort-
able income to go a-soldiering, and when
this, war broke out he dropped his billet
again and enlisted. He was Sergt.
Williams at Gallipoli, and would have suc-
ceeded to a commission if his division had
not been wiped out. There was nothing
left to officer. The sergeant came back
invalided to Melbourne and was pronounced
permanently unfit. Last month he left for
England and a year’s recuperation. In the
course of his rest cure he will probably
punch a few slackers on the nose. His
father, William Williams, was a well-known
preacher in England, and when a parson’s
son is combative he is combative.
* * * *
“F.H.”: So far I haven’t seen C. .T.
Dennis and Edmond Rostand mentioned in
the same par. Yet Dennis followed in Ros-
tand’s footsteps when some years ago he
retired to his bark humpy in the tall timber,
pen in hand, to escape from the interrup-
tions of the multitude and get on with his
writin’. It was thus that Rostand’s chief
work was produced; and I remember that
The Bulletin commended the Frenchman’s
example to Australian writers. It was that
tip which sent Dennis to find solitude in
the ranges—not necessarily chasing inspira-
tion, but seeking large quantities of time and
elbow-room in which to refine his brain-pro-
ducts. The example is still worth following.
COPY PAPER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It stands upon my table now,
A big, square block of copy paper;
O’er this for many months, I vow,
I needs must burn the midnight taper.
It’s ready for me, waiting but
An inspiration straight from Heaven —•
Two thousand slips, all trimly cut,
Just as I like them, ten by seven.
And ere a bigger theme I seize
I sit a little while, and ponder
The wondrous possibilities
Of that white, virgin paper yonder.
It is the fallow ground my pen
Will plough in rows half-inch asunder,
And sow with myriad seeds. And then
What will the harvest be, I wonder?
Perchance it’s lurking in the pile,
As in the stone once hid a Milo,
My novel, fine in theme and style,
Or only tracts on maize and silo,
And vagrant pars and tinkling rhymes
The better readers will make nought of;
Perchance the play which many times,
With great intention, I have, thought of.
Maybe those pages will be seamed
With lines to make me really famous,
Or stained with japes that might be dreamed
By any scribbling ignoramus.
Already visions seem to stir.
I fear me, though, that Fate’s black malice
Will make a whited sepulchre
Where I would have a fairy palace.
VICTORIA.
E. D.

Sorrow.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A SHADOW came between me and the
wine
That gleamed so redly in the crystal
cup ;
The golden ornaments had ceased to shine,
The flowers closed their scented petals up,
Drooped down and withered ; and the lights
grew faint
As paling stars at the grey dawn’s con-
straint.
Was it your lips, my Amaranth, that proved
But gates for liar’s pageantry ; or say
A trusted friend, all faithless, though
beloved ?
Or was I weary of the painted day
We turned night into? Now, it matters not,
When I have gauged the measure of my lot!
But they, whose laughter seemed so warm
and gay,
Grew cold and left me. Even as they went
I saw the marks of licence and decay
Upon their faces; and the subtle scent,
That once had been allurement to my sense,
Was stale with all the outrage of pretence.
Then, all around, a darkness seemed to close,
And through the darkness blew a night-wind
cold.
The world I knew, sweet-scented as a rose,
Filled with warm, living wine and lustrous
gold,
Was lost to me. Sorrow above me stood
A black-robed horror, with a sable hood.
But, as bowed head on my stretched
arms fell
In anguish for a loss that left me dazed,
I heard a soft, sweet voice of comfort tell;
By gentle hands my frenzied head was
raised ;
And lo ! I saw, within the sable hood,
A face of calm, compassionate womanhood!
There was no cruelty in that eye of hers —
No triumph in the conquest she had
wrought—
But it was bright with pity’s tender tears;
And two cool hands my trembling fingers
sought
To softly raise me; groping as I went,
I found a hope in dreaming her intent.
She opened wide a door that faced the east;
A cool wind laid its fingers on my head ;
And so we left the temple of the feast
As to the waking dawn my soul she led,
Not as a robber who my joys would steal,
But as a lover anxious for my weal.
Fresh broke the day and filled the world with
gold ;
How glad the dew on freshened grasses
shone!
“These are my tears,” said Sorrow. “And,
behold,
The darkness fadeth and the night is gone!”
And while the dawn’s bright lance-points
pressed the mist
My chastened lips the mouth of Sorrow kist.
A memory of Sorrow in my heart
Goes with me always—always. It is sweet,
Even if, in its sweetness, tears have part.
And I shall never fear it when we meet.
Life has its shallow pleasures and its toys;
But Sorrow holds the key to other joys.
N.S.W.
HAMER.
A Papuan Tragedy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A GREAT feast had just been partaken
of in the Papuan village of Kera-
puna, and at the first streak of dawn
the guests arose and commenced their pre-
parations for departure.
Aiva, son of the Chief of Kerapuna,
squatted upon his haunches, and thought-
fully chewed betel-nut, while he followed,
with half-closed eyes, the graceful move-
ments of a young girl, the daughter of one
of the visiting chiefs, who was busily en-
gaged in packing a large string bag with
provisions for the journey.
The Chief of Kerapuna slowly descended
the ladder from his house, and stood behind
his son, a slight smile playing round the
corners of his cruel, sensual mouth.
“Truly the maid is very beautiful,” he
murmured, “and she is soon to become the
wife of Ikavita, Chief of Mambare.”
Aiva started at the sound of his father’s
voice, then rising to his feet he growled
sulkily:
“Ikavita has many wives. I have but two.
Moreover, I have long desired to possess
Nana myself.”
“Then take her, my son,” replied his
father. “Her tribe is not strong enough to
prevent- you, and, as you say, Ikavita has
many wives, so that perchance the loss of
one will not anger him.”
The Chief turned and strolled away
towards the beach, and Aiva, after standing
for some time in deep thought, hurried
across the village clearing and joined a large
group of Kerapuna warriors, who wdre
waiting to bid the visitors farewell.
Ten minutes later the group dispersed,
and in parties of three or four at a time
disappeared unostentatiously into the sur-
rounding jungle. As each party became
hidden by the thick undergrowth they threw
off their appearance of lazy unconcern, and
hurried towards a spot on the banks of a
small creek about half a mile to the west-
ward of the village.
Within half an hour of Aiva’s conversa-
tion with his father, 50 fully-armed warriors,
headed by Aiva himself, were hurrying
almost noiselessly through the jungle' in
single file and making for a range of low
hills which ran parallel to the coast, about
three miles inland from Kerapuna.
Arrived at the foot of this range, Aiva
led the way along the base of it, until, com-
ing upon a faintly-marked track which zig-
zagged up the steep side of the hill, he
halted and held a whispered consultation
with the man immediately behind him.
Then turning to the right, he led the way
straight up the hillside.
When he reached the summit of the ridge
he lay down behind a clump of undergrowth
and, signing to his men to couceal themselves
also, he lovingly fingered the sharp, poisoned
point of his ebony-wood spear, and waited.
The guests of the people of Kerapuna, hav-
ing meanwhile finished their preparations for
the long journey back to their own country,
took affectionate leave of those of the Kera-
puna people who remained in the village.
With many expressions of eternal friendship
and goodwill from both sides, they set out
along the track.
The Chief of Kerapuna stroked his chin,
and smiled thoughtfully as he looked after
the departing visitors, and allowed his eyes
to rest for a moment upon the dainty Nana.
She hurried along behind her father, singing
happily to herself, and doubtless thinking
of the time so near at hand when she would
be the newest and consequently ' e most
cherished wife of Ikavita, the great and
powerful Chief of Mambare.
An hour’s walk brought them to the foot
of the low range of hills, and following the
zigzag track they commenced the ascent.
Half-way up the hill Nana’s father halted,
and, grasping his spear more firmly in his
hand, peered anxiously into the undergrowth
above him, while those behind stood and
waited expectantly. In a tree near at
hand a covey of birds of paradise at their
morning dance rent the air with their in-
sistent cries. A snake glided noiselessly
across the track a few yards ahead. Nothing
appeared that could give reasonable cause
for anxiety on the part of the travellers, but
still Nana’s father stood listening intently
and gazing steadily up the hill.
At last Nana became impatient.
“It is nothing, father,” she said lightly.
“A wild pig, perchance, but no enemy would
dare to attack us so near to our Mends in
Kerapuna.”
“You are young, my child,” growled the
old chief, “or you would know that our
friends are only our friends as long as they
have nothing to gain, by becoming our ene-
mies.” He continued slowly and cautiously
leading the way upwards, darting quick,
troubled glances above and around him as he
went.
As they neared the top of the hill he again
halted suddenly. Swinging sharply round
he pushed Nana into the bushes at the side
of the track, and jerked his shield in front of
him as he turned back again and looked up
the hill. A sbeond later a spear whizzed
through the air and stuck quivering in his
shield, and as the men behind him rushed
forward to his assistance, Aiva and his
warriors sprang to their feet and hurled
themselves down the slope upon their unpre-
pared victims.
Although Aiva’s warriors were consider-
ably outnumbered, the fact that, in accord-
ance with Papuan customs, they, being the
hosts, had not partaken of the previous
night’s feast, while their late guests were
still heavy from the effects of their orgy,
told very much in their favor. After a short,
fierce struggle, during which Nana’s father
and some 20 of his men were killed, the
visitors broke and fled in all directions.
At the commencement of the attack Aiva
had secured Nana, and, leaving his men to
finish the fight, had dragged her, despite her
frantic struggles, down the hillside and
towards the village.
The remaining women of Nana’s party, as
soon as they saw which way the battle was
likely to end, made good their escape, and
the Kerapuna men, having effected their pur-
pose, forebore to pursue them, and returned
quietly to their villas'e. They overtook Aiva
on their way and assisted him with the still
struggling Nana.
Arrived in Kerapuna. Nana realised that
it would be useless to attempt to escape.
Even if she succeeded in eluding the vigil-
ance of her captors, her owm village was
several days’ journey away, and she would
be certain to be captured by some other
tribe long before she could hope to reach
home.
Therefore, being a sensible girl, she
resigned herself to becoming the wife of the
future chief of Kerapuna, instead of marry-
ing the present chief of Mambare.
For the ensuing six months Nana’s life
was by no means a happy one. Aiva’s other
two wives, jealous of the kindness and
consideration which he showed Nana, never
lost an opportunity of holding her up to
public ridicule when they, with the remain-
ing women of the village, were at work
each morning in their husbands’ gardens.
From sunrise till dark, as long as Aiva was
out of hearing, they never ceased to nag
her. The consequence was that Nana,
knowing that whatever she did would be
found fault with, became careless and negli-
gent in her work.
At last there came a time when she began
to feel ill and unfit for the labor that she
was expected to perform. Knowing that she
could hope for no sympathy or considera-
tion from her fellow wives, and being unwill-
ing to speak to Aiva himself on the subject,
she continued to struggle on as best she
could, until one morning while working in
the gardens she omitted to tie up a clump of
sugar-cane which had grown too long to
support itself and was obviously in need of
attention. The matter was at once reported
to Aiva, who arrived upon the scene armed
with a stout length of lawyer-cane and a
ferocious temper.
So far Nana had received nothing but
kindness from Aiva. Now, exasperated by
the continual complaints regarding her
behavior dinned into his ears lach day by
his other wives, he seized her roughly by
the arm and flogged her unmercifully, until
tired with his exertions he flung her sense-
less and bleeding upon the ground and
returned to his house.
The sun was sinking behind the treetops
to the westward when Nana, dazed and
aching in every bone, rose and staggered
down to the beach. She threw herself upon
the sand and lay sobbing broken-heartedly

kettle may boil over or lurch from the hob at any moment, and there is
daily risk of similar mishaps to men, women and children alike.
Lighting one’s pipe isn’t always safe and painless; lifting dishes from
the oven and washing up have their own warm surprises; and fbo young-
sters learn many painful lessons about playing with "**e.
“A burnt child dreads the fire”—but if mother
has applied Zam-Buk it loves this pure, healing
balm ever afterwards. Zam-Buk is soothing and

often destroyed, and those who have watched a

which works hand in hand with Nature so har-
moniously and effectively.

i l] to the night. Then dragging her-
i i lier Jcet she returned slowly to
• vivh s house, mounted the ladder, and feel-
-ug her way to the corner where Aiva lay
asleep, stretched herself beside him, and
vmy timidly laid her head upon his shoulder.
After this never a day passed that Nana
v-as not beaten on some pretext or other,
and very frequently on no pretext at all.
News travels quickly and mysteriously in
the jungle, and it was not Jong before
lkavita, chief of Mambare, began to hear
more or less authentic accounts of the in-
human treatment meted out to the girl
whom lie had intended to make his wife.
lie had not been greatly concerned on
bearing of her capture by the people of
Kerapuna, for as Aiva’s father had
remarked, lkavita already possessed many
other wives, and one more or less mattered
little to him. But at the news that she was
being llltreated by her captors he became
seriously angry, and sent messengers to the
outlying villages of his tribe commanding all
able-bodied warriors to repair at once to
Mambare.
Some days later a hunting-party of
Kerapuna men came running into the vil-
lage in hot haste to inform their chief that
lkavita and all his fighting men were but a
short hour’s march away, and were heading
iu the direction of Kerapuna with obviously
hostile intentions.
The Chief of Kerapuna shouted, and
swore, and blamed his son for ever daring
to take possession of the intended bride of
the great lkavita. Aiva stood at a safe
distance from his father, and replied that
he would never have dreamt of doing sucli
a thing had not the Chief of Kerapuna him-
self suggested it. In the midst of the storm
which followed this probably truthful asser-
tion a stream of painted and plumed war-
riors entered the village clearing from the
jungle behind. At their head marched the
great lkavita himself, who, signalling to
his men to halt, came forward to where the
Chief of Kerapuna stood cursing his son.
“Messiana, Chief of Kerapuna,” said
lkavita, stepping in front of him, “I, lkavita,
great . Chief of Mambare, have come from
my village to take from you the woman
named Nana, who was promised to me seven
moons ago by her parents, and whom you
captured after treacherously killing her
father while he and she were guests in your
country. I bring with me many warriors,
Messiana, and if you refuse to surrender
this woman to me, I have but to command
them and they will fall upon you and kill
you and your men and your children, and
capture your women and burn your villages,
and the tribe of Kerapuna will be no more.
O Messiana, Chief of Kerapuna, where is
this woman of whom I speak?”
“O lkavita, great Chief of Mambare,” re-
plied Messiana in a shaky voice, ‘‘it was not
I, but Aiva, my son, who captured the woman
called Nana. 1 told him that Nana was to
become your wife, lkavita, and forbade him
to lay hands upon her. If you desire to
take Nana back with you to Mambare, Aiva
my son will surrender her to you, for I his
father and liis chief will command him to
do so. There stands Aiva, my son. Go you
to him, O lkavita, and repeat to him the
words of Messiana his father.” And indi-
cating Aiva, who stood a short distance off
glaring angrily at lkavita, he walked slowly
away.
lkavita turned and looked contemptuously
at Aiva, and then strode across to where he
stood.
“Aiva, son of Messiana, Chief of Kera-
puna,” he said sternly, “are you he who stole
from me the maid Nana who was about to
become my wife? Are you the man who has
beaten her, and starved her, and treated her
as I would not treat one of my dogs?
Answer me, Aiva, son of Messiana, are you
that man?”
“I beat any of my wives when they dis-
obey me,” growled Aiva in a surly tone.
_ lkavita stepped back a pace, and raising
bis spear slashed Aiva heavily across the
face with the shaft. “Nana is no longer your
wife,” he roared. “She is”
Before he could finish his sentence Aiva,
mad with rage and the pain of the blow,
rushed forward and throwing his arms
around Ikavita’s waist endeavored to bring
him to the ground.
The Mambare warriors, knowing their
chief to be in no danger, did not attempt to
interfere, but merely crowded round to
watch.
Aiva, though strong and thick set, was no
match for lkavita, who was almost half as
tall again as he, and had the additional ad-
vantage of being armed with spear and stone
club.
Giving Aiva a sharp blow on the head
with the latter weapon, he shook him off. and
chancing to glance to one side as he did so,
caught sight of Nana, who had forced her
way through the crowd and stood with wide-
open, fearful eyes watching the struggle.
lkavita, thinking that her fear was for his
own safety, laughed lightly, and raising his
spear took deliberate aim and hurled it with
all his force straight at Aiva’s chest. At
that moment Nana, who had been watching
his movements closely, sprang forward
between the two men, and the next she lay
dying upon the ground with the point of
Ikavita’s spear protruding several inches out
from her left shoulder blade.
With a howl of rage Aiva flung himself
on his knees beside her and raised her head
in his arms, while lkavita stood staring
stupidly at them both.
Nana opened her eyes and smiled at Aiva.
“Good-bye. Aiva,” she murmured. “I will
die now, and then there will be no one to
disobey you and make you angry, as I have
done. It is better that I should die to save
your life than live to make you angry.”
Her voice trailed away to a whisper,
and a moment later her body relaxed and
she lay dead.
Aiva bent his head and pressed her cheek
to his.
The next second he fell forward across
Nana’s body, his skull smashed like an egg-
shell by a terrific: blow from Ikavita’s stone
club.
lkavita dragged him off Nana's body, and,
after a long look at the dead girl’s face,
turned away with a deep sigh and led his
men in silence back into the jungle.
VICTOR P. ARMSTRONG.
Bombs.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Y' OU poke your head over the top of
your blanket and wonder what has
wakened you. From far off conies the
smooth purring of an engine. It grows
louder, and
Crash! Another: And the heavens are
raining destruction on the cantonments.
You grab your boots, slip them on, and in
a twinkling are in the protection of a dugout.
Against the steel-blue of the moonlit sky the
aeroplane can be seen flying low in an
endeavor to strike a vulnerable point. A
long beam of light sweeps into the night;
then another and another; and the merciless
flares focus the purring thing that is dropping
death.
There is the scream of a shell, a crash as it
bursts, and a puff of white smoke is hovering
near the enemy machine. Out of the dis-
tance come three more taubes, and in a
moment they, too, are adding roar upon roar
to the scream and crash of the shrapnel.
Another anti-aircraft gun comes into
action. Planing and turning, the taubes are
followed wherever they go by those pretty
balls of smoke which mean so much to them.
Now there is the vicious rattle of machine-
guns and the Bodies are circling amidst a
fire that gains accuracy every moment.
“Got him!” cries a gunner in exultation.
A machine staggers from the white smoke
a few yards from its wing, and drops twist-
ing a hundred feet or so; then with an
ungainly jerk it pulls itself straight again.
It turns its nose homeward now; its pilot
has had enough.
A bomb falls whistling a dozen yards from
us, a column of flame shoots high into the
air, and we realise joyfully that one more
incendiary bomb has done damage that
amounts to —nothing. Mercifully, there are
many that the mud renders harmless, but
where fire lias broken out there is already a
busy picket extinguishing the flames.
Only a few minutes have passed since we
scrambled to safety, but the taubes seem to
have no desire to wear out their welcome. At
the same time we cannot help paying silent
homage to the German airmen. These, at
least, are murdering no helpless women and
children, but are waging war on a camp that
can give as good as it takes.
Tlieir bombs all dropped, they wheel across
the sky and disappear as quickly as they
came, leaving our gunners white with rage
and disappointment.
“One more little minute an’ I’d have fixed
them....l’d give six months’ pay to have
downed one of ’em Ah, well! Better luck
next time.”
_ The stores of ammunition have not been
hit, and our casualties are few. We crawl
once more between our blankets and fall
asleep, the more soundly for the knowledge
that the unpleasant nightbirds will not dis-
turb our slumber again—this night, at least.
FRANK REID.
Children of Joy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IVT OT oue step may you go
j\ From the edge of the track,
For the hill is bewitched, dewy vines
hold you back,
Looping snares for your feet in a delicate
malice ;
And the grass is aglow
Where the day is begun
With lanterns of rubies tired through by the
sun;
With revellers flaunting red joy in a chalice;
With little wild poppies.
Not one step may you go ; ’twere unholy to
tread
Where this offering of tragic felicity, spread
In the path of the terrible Youth of the
Year,
Breathes transient beauty defiant of fear,
That teases the morning to dimple and thrill
With the mirth of the poppies,
The Bacchantine poppies,
The little doomed poppies that dance on the
hill.
Evanescent as dawn
And unstable as flame,
With torches of scarlet no shadow can tame,
They kindle the plumes of the blossoming
grasses;
So sharply withdrawn
From the arms of the earth,
Straight ravished by death from their rap-
turous birth,
Whirled away at the will of the first breeze
that passes,
What care the wild poppies?
Ecstatic and helpless, each turns a brave
eye *
To the pure and unpitiful blue of the skv ;
Each treasures her last shattered petaf to
fling
A gift of goodwill to the perilous Spring
As she flees from the hill, into nothingness
hurled....
But with each stricken poppy,
Each little slain poppy,
A Bream is set free that may hearten the
world.
Too eager to stay
For the night stillness deep,
Too fragile to storm the white ramparts of
sleep,
The children of joy seek the children of
sorrow; •
Through the clangor of day,
Through the brazen noon beams.
Oh, have you not heard them, the Comfort-
ing Dreams,
With their whispers of cheer for the untried
to-morrow ?
The souls of the poppies
That leap to their death on the wind-ruffled
slope
Are the Fancies who guide the shy footsteps
of Hope.
Oh, have you not felt in your blackest
despair
Soft lips on your eyelids, dear hands on
your hair?
Then baffle with laughter your fate, conic
what will,
Like the. riotous poppies.
The little wild poppies,
The unafraid poppies that dance on the hill.
VICTORIA,
ELSIE COLE.
